THE RED pAGE.
CHAMBERLAIN IN AFRICA
It lias become quite clear that the result of
Chamberlain’s peace-making journey to Africa is,
in his own eyes—Failure. Three of his speeches
prove this. At the outset, he was “appalled
by the difficulties that confront me. At the
end, he had realised that the Imperialistic dream
was dead —the colonies must share the burden
of F/inpire or be cast off. And on his return to
England—“l warn you against over-estimating
the results of my mission.”
We have always realised that the South African
war was a national crime. It is Cham her lain
himself, the maker of the war, who now realises
that it was—an Imperialistic blunder. Possibly,
among the execrations that History will pile upon
Chamberlain’s head, the worst will be penned by
his own countrymen.
In a pamphlet entitled In the Court of History
(Toronto; W. Tyrrell) Goldwin Smith—a dis-
tinguished writer, and a writer who, with what-
ever defects, may lay claim to a share of the his-
torian’s breadth' of view—refers to Australian and
Canadian experience during the war as an omen
of the course of events under Imperial Federation.
“ We have been made, incidentally, to see the
difference between a nation and a dependency,
and to know what, under the form of equality, the
real position of Canada, as a member of an im-
perial Federation, would be. In the Imperial
country, notwithstanding the overbearing violence
of the war party, opinion on the other side con-
tinued active and found expression. . . • But
Canada, on the other hand, has been simply swept
in the train of the dominant party m the Imperial
country'’. In our Parliament free speech has been
drowned in clamour. Our public press almost
universally has been a transcript of the jingo press
of England. Thus the main facts of the case have
never been allowed to come before the Canadian
people. How many of our people have ever heard
of the Conventions ; ever heard that self-govern-
ment as to internal affairs had been guaranteed to
the people of the Transvaal, or that the British
Ministers, Mr. Chamberlain among them, had
emphatically recognised the right; ever heard
that the claim of suzerainty was denied by
pendent jurists; or that arbitration had been
tendered by the Transvaal Government and
refused ? So it would always be under Imperial
Federation. Political life and leadership would
centre in the Imperial country. The colonies,
isolated from each other politically, as they must
be would be satellites revolving round the central
orb, subservient to Imperial policy, and drawn
blindfold into Imperial wars.”
EAITH-HEALING
Dowie, the religious charlatan whose mission-
aries are in Australia, and to whose Zion City,
U.S.A., some Australian farmers have recently
emigrated, is alleged to have sacrificed to his in-
sane creed even his own daughter, after the fashion
of the atrocious Jephthah
Dowie’s only daughter was a girl of twenty-one, and,
curling her hair one day with a spirit lamp, a draught
blew the flame in a gust towards her. Her flimsy
dressing-gown caught fire, and she was horribly and
fatally burnt before anything could be done to save her.
During the ghastly agony of her last hours Dowie and
all the elders of the Church knelt round her bed and
prayed for a recovery. Through all the torture the
girl laboured to beg forgiveness of her father—the use
of alcohol in any form and for any purpose being
strictly prohibited by Dowie—and repeated again and
ao-ain that his followers must remember she was dying
because of her disobedience, because of her own un-
worthiness *ler last passionate endeavour seemed to
he to leave Vr father’s reputation unsmirched. .
But, it is alleged, Dowie does not sacrifice himself.
He lives in personal luxury, and at the same time
refuses superbly to account for any of the sums of
monev entrusted to him. His horses are worth a for-
tune in themselves ; his carriages are emblazoned with
armorial bearings ; his wife- is said to dress with the
gorgeous extravagance of an Empress. When he
travels, hemmed round with a little army of liveried
servants, the prophet of humility and self-denial has a
special train chartered, and whenever spiritual burdens
become too great a tax there is a delightful country
residence belonging to him in which to retreat from
the clamour and importunate appeals of the faitniul.
-
Dowie is called charlatan advisedly. The root
of the faith-healing delusion (as preached) is
just this: it is possible to train and strengthen
the will, as a function of the body, just as one
strengthens a muscle by exercise. The nervous
system is a reservoir of forces—some explored and
defined, some obscure and not understood. Much
of our nervous energy is discharged unconsciously,
and it is possible to consciously utilise unconscious
nerve-centres for a given end. Quite truly, it is
said that many people die for want of the Will to
live—like Mrs. Dombey, annoying the doctors
Similarly, many people are ill for want of the will
to be well. The deliberate intention to be well is
sufficient to control and at times to cure some
minor ailments. The process, as explained, is a
deliberate concentration of nervous forces upon
the ailing nerve. As, by an effort of will, or even
by thought merely, you can send more blood to
some muscles, so you can send more energy to
some nerves—and thus (sometimes, in some eases)
lessen or banish neuralgia, for example. Ihe idea
has no particular novelty. “Faith-healing,
under the name of hypnotism or mental sugges-
tion; is a commonplace of medical treatment,
especially in hysterical cases. Auto-suggestion is
a scientific fact ; and power increases with exer-
cise, naturally. Those who advocate the
I nind-bracing habit are right, within un-
iettled limits dependent on the doubt about
•eactions. Just as the development of big
nuscles may ruin a man’s general constitution
ir as the cripple to whom a fire-alarm has restored
;he power of locomotion may become lamer than
lefore, so the deliberate draining of a man’s ner-
vous reserves, if excessive, may leave him weaker
—with a temporary ailment blotted out m a per-
manent disease. The amazing machinery of the
human organism exists in an equilibrium of com-
plicated forces that should be disturbed very
cautiously—either by quacks with degrees and
drugs, or by quacks without them.
&
Of course, the Dowie dogma that faith can set a
broken leg, or extirpate a cancer or even stop
flooding in child-birth, is grossly false— practically
speaking. Faith— i.e., the blind or deliberate
exercise of will, the blind or deliberate trans-
ference of nervous credit from trust account to
current account—can actually or conceivably help
a bone to knit, or fight a parasitic growth, or re-
duce the volume of a blood-current. It is within
the realm of physical possibility that such faith
may remove a mountain—if the faith be great
enough, or the mountain small enough. x>ut
railway contractors have to rely upon barrow
and shovel ; and people of even extraordinary
faith, assailed by serious accident or disease,
usually will do well to trust a surgeon.
If they do not trust, they will probably
die in the faith-not an unattractive end
for some people. (The inviting question whether
on the whole, it is profitable to numamty that
healthy fools should die prematurely, must be lelt
for another day. Even the question whether un-
healthy knaves like Dowie should be killed sud-
denly will bear argument.)

The author of A Londoner’s Log Book, 1901-2 (Bell; 2s.
id.) reproduces from the Cornhill mag a. a senes of
rather pallid sketches that have little interest for Aus-
tralians. , . , ,
Poor Fellow, by Mrs. J. H. Riddell (Bell; fa. 6d.) might
have been written to support the assertion (by the writer
if The Egregious English) that when an Englishman
-eases to be an employed person he usually makes a mess
of thino-s. Mrs. Riddell writes a matter-of-fact commer-
cial romance that holds the mind without thrilling it ;
and the book has solid value.
The Lucky Shot, by E. Way Elkington (Treharne ; Is.),
is a story ®f love and remarkable occurrences m Maori-
land It is written apparently by a girlish hand.
Zealandia’s Guerdon ( Long; 2s. 6 d.) exhibits the
indomitable William S. Walker still rolling his stone up
the hill of Literature. It has rolled up to moderate
praise in The Athenceum, rolled over and down ; but it
has gathered no moss. William S. has stored in his head
an extraordinary number of vivid impressions of Aus-
tralia and Maoriland ; and he can not vividly express any
of them.
“He was a thinker —enough not to be quite a poet; a
poet—enough to spoil his thinking,” says The Academy
fitly of Robert Buchanan, whose “ Life ” by his sister-in-
law and adopted daughter, Harriett Jay, is just published
(Unwin; 2 s. 6ci.). The book is a plain account of
luchanan, written in plain style,,uncritical and undis-
nguished. It is not the book that was needed ; but
imirers of Buchanan will read it with interest
id some tedium. The most notable of several con-
■ibuted chapters is supplied by Henry Murray, who
ivs that Buchanan was “a born _ gambler, as his
,ther had been before him,” and in later life was
regular patron of horse-racing. “At the time when
e was preparing a long commentary on Renan s views
igarding certain Scriptural episodes we went together
i Sandown, and in an interval between two races I found
im standing in the middle of Tattersall’s ring, serenely
nconscious of the charivari about him, reading his Greek
estament. When the bell rang he slipped the volume
ito his pocket, marking the place with a tip telegram,
nd plunged into the fray, apparently greatly refreshed
y his studies.”
The Circle, by Katherine Cecil Thurston (Blackwood ;
s. Qd. and 3s. Gd.) is a new writer’s book. Plot and
haraoters are always in the air, always a little unreal,
et not unreal in their air : the plot is sufficient, and the
haracters have distinction. The style is simple ; the
nanner original; and novel-readers will find the book s
[uality above the current English average.
For the sake of the story of “ The Girl and the Beetle ”
n In a Canadian Canoe— Barry Pain’s first and best
look—we shall not ask Little Entertainments (Unwin ;
Ls.) why they are not bigger. So many authors do big
ihings badly that we may be (sometimes) content with
me who is content to do little things well. Barry Pain
loes them very well : you see him, in these sketches,
standing away from his canvas with a cocked eye and an
nspiration, and adding artistic touches one by one.
We registered five laughs, besides smiles, and an occa-
sional thrill to Pain’s serious mood. His knack of final
mti-climax is especially neat. At a shilling, the book is
heartily recommended.
Plain and Veldt, J. H. M. Abbott’s second book of
stories and sketches, reminds one of a billabong fringed
by decorative she-oaks, of a churchyard where the monu-
mental statuary is not all ignoble, of the careful house-
keeper’s unused “best room,” breathing tomb-like
odours. The work is neither dead nor alive ; but the pall
of death is over it. Abbott has seen and observed much
and well ;he writes not badly; he has assimilated the
devices of Kipling and Stephen Crane ; and he is sin-
cere, with a mind for humour, a heart for sympathy. He
knows what to write about, though he does not know
precisely how to write about it. Sketches like “ A
Masterful Man,” “ The Majesty of the' Law,” “ The Bush-
rangers,” are of no common calibre. But their impres-
sion is temporary, evanescent. They want pulse of life,
breath of power. The whole book trails, nerveless and
fatigued. Abbott has many of the qualities of a memor-
able writer. He lacks just one quality essential-
strength.
BOOKPELLOWSHIPMENT
IF. \Y. Farrar, Anglican dean of Canterbury, dead in his
73rd year, was one of the disappearing bulwarks of
Christianity. Unintellectual, he dealt in emotions, and
“ put sophistic heotics on the pallors of the wise.” His
Life, of Christ and Eternal Hope were plasters for
the cancer of unbelief—like an Afghan’s canvas patch
sewn over his camel’s sore; and afforded great consolation
to women and fools. His profitable ventures intoithe
boys’ story-book domain— St. Winifred’s; Eric; Julian
Home— supplied prizes for a generation of Sunday-
schools, and gave to Kipling and other satirists a con-
venient summary of the nauseous schoolboy prig. He
wrote according to his lights, and left nothing for
Literature.
lie Goneourt Academy is. established, after six years
tigatiou, and the first monthly dinner of the members
held on 14th January. The brothers Goneourt,
iond and Jules, were celebrated figures in the Parisian
Id of the last half-century—writers, critics, and art
eotors. Edmond died in 1806, directing that his
aerty be sold and devoted to the foundation
a new academy for the encouragement of letters,
heirs-at-law contested the will, but at last
e been discomfited Edmond thought his estate
lid provide a pensioV of £240 a year each for ten
nbers, besides an annual prize of £2OO for a deserving,
iggiing author. But the estate is returning only some
iOO a year, so some of the Academy’s feathers must be
iped. The first Academicians, several of them nomi-
ed by Goneourt himself, are apparently L6on Henniqu*,
m Daudet, the Rosny brothers, Huysmans, Octave
•beau, G. Geffroy, L. Descaves, Elemir Bourges, Paul
rguerjtte. (Apparently f for three French lists to hand
agree/ They are a very mixed lot in aims and
tractorW and already there are rumours of dissension,
fact, one Parisian wit declares that the only way to
,ke Mirbeau sit down with Daudet is to nominate the
sny brothers to preside. “ They are warranted to
,ke everything sleep—even the passions.”
Che life of Charles Godfrey Leland, whose death in his
,h year is reoorded, was many-sided and full. Like the
lerican mediocrity, President Roosevelt, he desired
th the joy of action and the treasure of tranquillity ;
t Leland "had it in him to be what Roosevelt dreams.
>st Australians, found by a verse and fleeing from a
rmon, know Leland only as the author of Haw Breit-
Vnn'g Ballads:- but these are merely one of his
ousand phases-. His blood ran always hot and high,
i a student irf'-'Paris in ’4B he drilled and barricaded.
i a journalist in New York in ’69 he went to the war
id fought for the North. After that, travel, till his
ttlement in Florence in ’B6. Leland loved strange races
te Burton, quaint dialects like Borrow He was a
aster of gypsy lore and language. The labour of his
;e changed from carpentry to Virgil; and even those
■quainted with his versatility read Have You a Strong
'ill ?in 1899 with some surprise. Therein he formulated
discipline against sloth, indecision, postponements—a
•actieal faith-healing. But Leland’s will was backed by
strong body : the nerveless may concentrate their
reams : they cannot supply the initial lack of
sTvous energy, In praising Leland we pay a debt to
is Philadelphian parents, who married to make a Man.
G. K. Chesterton, writer of the chief article in Eng.
‘ookman’s “ Thackeray number,” declares that *
Thackeray, from the beginning of his life until the
end, consistently and seriously preached a gospel . . .
the philosophy of the beauty and the glory of fools . .
. . . amid men of action, men of genius, and heroes
he saw with absolute sincerity only one thing worth
being—a gentleman. And when we understand what
he meant by the phrase, the absolute sufficiency of a
limpid kindliness, of an obvious and dignified humility,
of a softness for noble memories and a readiness for any
minute self-sacrifice, we may, without any affected
paradox, but rather with serious respect, sum up
Thackeray’s view of )ife hy saying that amid all the
heroes and geniuses he saw only one thing .worth being
—a fool. * ■, , ,
Che paradox that'tfubs Thackeray’s gentleman a fool
•emains despite the disclaimer ; and the word “ fool” is
in fortunate. Thackeray admired simple hearts, loving
uearts true hearts, noble hearts; but these are not
necessarily fools’hearts. With the help of .the Pauline
phrase that “ the wisdom of this world is foolishness with
Hod,” you realise Chesterton’s meaning; but it is badly
expressed.
“ I look upon a study of Eucalypts,” says J. H. Maiden,

In Tolstoi as Man and Artist, just published, Dmitri
Merejkowski—who should know—refuses to take Tolstoi s
religious conversion seriously, and argues that at bottom
Tolstoi is a pagan with all the pagan characteristics of
extreme sensuousness, abundant vitality, and enormous
power of enjoying the elemental aspects and emotions of
life. But these unconscious depths have warred with his
conscious theory; and a half-hearted “ conversion is
Tolstoi’s compromise.
Herbert Spencer tells that he began First Principles on
the shore of a Scottish loch. His method was to dictate
to an amanuensis for fifteen minutes, then to row for
fifteen minutes in order to stimulate the circulation of
the blood so as to carry him through another fifteen
minutes dictation, and so on. This habit would account
for Spencer’s cold, encumbered style. Dictation breeds
long, involved sentences, and prolonged muscular exer-
cise during composition lessens their heat. Astonishing
that Spencer did not know that for acute thought and
stimulating style you need blood in
your muscles. Force in writing comes wRh congestion
of the cortical capillaries.

Bards of the Backblocks.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Mortgagee.
We bring the wilderness to bounds ;
We grub the bush to meads ;
In sour or sullen savage grounds
We sow Advance’s seeds ;
We grade the mountains, paint the hills
With patterned greenery,
And through Saharas lead the rills—
To feed the Mortgagee.
Our sons have blunted all their wits
With chores of field and wood ;
Their “poddy ” drudgery unfits
Our maids for motherhood ;
Our wives wear rags and yellow grow
With dairy’s agony;
Our own old hopes died long ago—
To clothe the Mortgagee.
We face the drought, that knows so well
To score the brow with care,
To hand us from the bar of hell
The stirrup-cup, Despair.
We live it down; then stem the floods,
Then locust-plagues bid flee—
To put those diamonds in the “ duds ”
Of Mrs. Mortgagee.
All mental joys we sacrifice.
Have no artistic goals ;
The daily press’s slush and vie®
Monotonise our souls.
But yet we tax ourselves to build
Endowed academies
That Culture’s rose may be distilled—
For sons of Mortgagees.
We build us homes in town, in bush;
Where’er we go, bring wealth ;
Plant vineyards, but the grapes we crush
Are soul’s and body’s health.
We see ourselves, wives, children brought
To servitude’s degree—
That high-class husbands may be bought
For Misses Mortgagee.
To dig canals through shifting sand,
Reclaim the wilderness,
And fit the solitary land
For servile Trade’s caress;
With girdling rail and madrigal
Of village greenery,
We pile the cairn of debt —and all
To grease the Mortgagee.
Our “ conscript fathers ” were so wise,
So prescient in their thought,
They “slung” the land as ’twere a prize
To whomsoever sought.
We, hungry, rushed it to a man ;
But ah 1 too late we see
’Twas but a well-concocted plan
To breed the Mortgagee.
vio.
DANTON.
Kate M'Leeson.
Oh ! wandering down the 3moky ways
Of suburb and of city,
The pale girl-faces, through the haze,
Touch all my thoughts with pity;
For I have camped by Goulburn side,
Right through the summer season;
And been for many a starlit ride
With bonny Kate M'Leeson.
Oh, Goulburn side ! green Goulburn side !
From Trawool to Maroona gate,
I long to cross the reaches wide
That lead the way to bonny Kate.
Her brown eyes beam with winsome light.
Her eyebrows arch to match them ;
Her neck and forehead, warm and wh ' te,
Have red-gold hair to thatch them.
She dresses in a frock of snow
That flaunts not spot nor speckle,
But on her cheeks full well I know
The taste of many a freckle.
She is no wee thing, sad and lone,
That fears the changing weather ;
Her head is level with my own
When we stand close together ;
Yet she is sweet as harvest-time
Before the hay is carted,
And homely as the nights of rime
When winter fires are started.
She is too modest to be shy,
Yet, ah! so captivating—
No city maid that I pass by
Seems riper for the mating ;
For she hath mould like Eve of old—
Blood-hot for primal pleasure—
All joy of motherhood to hold,
With grace to fill the measure.
So, Goulburn side, green Goulburn side 1
I’m coming back at lover’s gait
To claim from you my river bride,
My bonny, bright-eyed, buxom Kate.
MILKY WHITR.
A Heart’s Desire.
My soul ia away on the saltbush plain,
Where the ear is soothed by the silence loud ;
And the Bouth wind brings but a sandy cloud—
Never rain.
It wanders away to the gibber bare,
Where the sun-born lakes the senses mock ;
And the rivers are but glinting rock
And dancing air.
There let me go alone and die,
Where the Homeward road is wide and free ;
Vnd the desert god will go with me—
He and I. -

Nervous Prostration
A Dreadful Period of Suffering.
“Clements Tonic Saved Me.”
The Case of W|rs. E. WEEOEN
(BY A SPECIAL REPORTER.)
A* grest interest was manifested in all
circles respecting the following subject, a
representative waited on Mrs. Elizabeth
VVeeden, of No. 67 Cleveland-street, Rid-
fern, who is well known in the locality in
which she lives.
“ I am pleased that you have called,
said Mrs. VVeeden, “ as it gives me an op-
portunity to let the public know what was
my real portion at the time \ou refer to,
and also to remove any misapprehensions
which mirht exist in the minds of some of
my neighbours. It appears that the
general assumption was that I had profited
in health by following a certain course of
treatment recommended by my doctor. No-
thing of the sort was the case, for after
carrying out his instructions for a long time
my prospects were no brighter, so I gave
him up and tried to get relief through the
aid of seceral medicines which I had seen
advertised in the papers. They were no
less effective, and as they all failed to reach
the base of my afflictions I thought I was
never going to have my health back aga n.”
“ What was the nature of your com-
plaint ? ” enquired the reporter.
“The doctor said it was nervous prostra-
tion from which I was suffering, and I think
he was right, for I used to tremble as if an
electric battery was at work all through my
body, while the fearsome state of my mind
was most distressing, as I was always
fancying that some terrible trouble was
hanging over me. If the children were not
borne from school to time I worried myself
with the belief that, something had happened
to them, and such anxiety seemed to make
ne very irritable and short-tempered. I
>as alwas tired an(J extremely drowsy,
feoling at 9 o’clock in the morning as if I
could go to bed and sleep all day, such a
th ng as a desire to get my work done sel-
dom entering my head. Careless and in-
different to everything around me, my life
was one omtinual misery., There was a
depressing pain in my head as if a band had
been drain tightly over my e>es, before
which little daik specks frequently floated,
and sometimes , T could scarcely see for a
haziness which appeared in front of me. I
used to imagine I had a touch of ear-ache,
owing to a peculiar sensation like the sound
of a bell ring ng in my ears, while the
shocking headaches I had to endure I shall
never forget. You have no idea how
wretched I felt when I had eaten a few
mouthfuls of food. My stomach became
filled with wind, from which I suffered great
Jiain until sour belehings came on, when I
elt a 1 ttle easier ; but, Jn the meant me,
my chest was proving very troublesome,
with a feeing as if a load of some kind
was g ing to smother me. Then I com-
menced to feel s ck, and be'ore long my
food was retched up again. T had the most
awful pains in my side and shoulders; hut
the manner in which my heart palpitated
caused me many a fright. If I walke i a few
yards my heart fluttered dreadfully, so you
may depend I exerted myself as little as
possible, more especially as I felt faint and
giddy when I moved about. After the least
effort to do a little work I used to collapse,
my feebleness being so marked that if I
tried to sweep a room my strength failed so
completely that I felt powerless, even to
the tips of my fingers.”
“ You spoke about feeling so drowsy all
day. How did you pass your niehts?”
“ Well, I was in such a sluggish condition
that I could always sleep, hut the strange
part about it was that I never felt rested or
refreshed. This was due, no doubt, to the
fat t.that I had horrible dreams and night-
mares, which made me scream out whilst
asleep. The children have often come to
me in the middle of the night to wake me
up so that my screaming would cease, and
bow terrified I was when awakened l My
mouth was in a nasty stale, too, as if I had
been tasting copper, my tongue also being
covered with fur. I thought my skin would
never get white again, it was that yellow,
and I was just about as thin as I could pos-
sibly be. Nobody but those who have had
a similar experience to mine can form the
slightest conception of what mental torture
is involved when pain is endured and all hope
is lost. There was no pleasure in being
alive, but still I clung to what little life was
left, and now you see a different woman al-
together. lam quite well again.”
“ You are very fortuuate. But what
made you so? ”
“ It was a medicine called Clements Tonic
which helped me out of my difficulties, and
but for that remedy I do not know what I
would have done. As the doctor’s and
other medic nes could do me no good it is
only right to say that Clements Tonic saved
me, and I shall always deem it my duty to
make this fast clear to other people. Yes,
it was when I had taken nearly a bottle of
Clements Tonic that I felt warmed and
stiengthened, with a good healthy glow
running right through my body, and my
spirits brighter than they had been ever
6mce my illness began. Getting some more
Clements Tonic, that miserable drowsiness
commenced to leave me, and as I was al-
ready taking a little food I began to pick
up strength. I thought this was splendid
progress, and I was highly elated with
the great success which attended my uss
of Clements Tonic—a fact that was borne
out by the abatement of my tortures. My
headaches became so insignificant that I
hardly noticed them ; then they died out
altogether, and I can say the same about
the pains in my side Rnd shoulders. I
was so thankful to be thus relieved, and
still more encouragement to pe- severe with
Clements Tonic came when flatulence was
remedied and the heaviness was lifted
from my chest. The anxiety** which my
heart had caused by palpitating at every
turn was readily set aside by Clements
Tonic, which seemed to find its way to
every nook and corner of my internal
organs and root out every particle of
disease which had been left untouched by
previous medicines. The sleep which then
came was calm and refreshing, and when
I got up it was with a clean mouth and
a good appetite for my morning meal,
and also with a feeling of energy which
made it a pleasure to have some work
to do. All this time my nervousness was
becoming less apparent in every way. I
could soon tolerate any noise without
trembling, and my mind was , free from a!\
I recognised how different my life
was, and my grateful thoughts were all
centred on Clements Tonic, which I c n-
tinued taking for another week or so, when
I found myself as well as ever, with my
complexion clear and my weight regained.”
“ With your permission I’ll reproduce
this narrative.”
“ You can publish it in any way you like,
as I’ve only spoken the truth.'’

SATURDAY, APRIL 4, 1903,
Prick 6d.
LACK OF WORK: ITS REAL CAUSE.
UNEMPLOYED : “Why can’t we get work making some of these things instead of importing them?”
IMPORTER : “No! Australia’s £40,000,000 worth of annual imports are the backbone of its prosperity. Imports are the only true test of a nation’s
greatness. It becomes great by buying things, not by making them. These imports represent the factories you haven’t got, and the
work and wages you haven’t got—and that’s where I come in. Get to relief works!”

SATURDAY, APRIL 4, 1903.
A Petty Navy and a Bumptious Admiral.
Speaking at a luncheon tendered to him at Otago,
Admiral Fanshawe said: “No war could be waged
against us by any Power without a navy. Next time we
have a war we must come on the navy before we can put
a single shipload of soldiers on the sea, and we must see
that the sea is clear. I can conceive nothing more serious
than that the transport of the bravest and best soldiers
in the world should be exposed to attack by some fast
cruiser or torpedo-destroyer, and we must keep the
command of the sea. I think that had better be done by
one great navy. Why should we set up our little petty
navies? Let us stand to the old service which has done
everything for us in the past.”— Cablegram from Maori-
land.
Before the next meeting of the Australian
Parliament it would be well for this country
to come to some definite understanding about
the naval question. The eternal shiftiness
of Britain’s policy makes it difficult for Aus-
tralia to know exactly where it stands. Up
to a very few years ago Britain strongly en-
couraged this country to maintain a naval
force of its own, and the separate States
began to do so in a small way. They engaged
British officers to command their vessels,
and one of the results of this policy was that
Queensland had to send police to seize its own
under-sized cruiser to prevent a British officer
taking it away contrary to orders—bolting
with it, in fact. Queensland’s recovery of
its partially embezzled squadron was a thrill-
ing comic opera episode. Then Britain flew
off the handle in a fresh direction, and pro-
posed that Australia, following up
the new policy of maintaining vessels of its
own, should contribute to the support of a
British fleet, which was to remain at all
times in the South Pacific, and be available
for Australian defence. Australia fell in
with this idea, and the Naval Tribute policy
started. The first unpleasant reflection that
was cast upon the new system came in the
candid statement of a British naval officer
that in time of war he would not hesitate to
take the subsidised British Vquadron to
wherever the seat of hostilities happened to
be—to India, China, or anywhere else.
This was such an unvarnished declaratioh
that the solemn promise, league, and coven-
ant of Britain might not be worth the paper
on which it was written, that it ought to
have made Australia chary of further naval
agreements, but the unwholesome incident
passed over. Now Australia is requested to
increase its subsidy, and to abrogate the
arrangement by which the subsidised fleet is
devoted to Australian defence—to pay
heavily for a fleet which is to remain on these
coasts in peace time when it is of no earthly
use, and which, in time of war and danger,
may be 10,000 miles away. And a fat and
“feckless” Federal Premier has pledged
himself to this astounding proposition. It
is also rumored that the proposal includes
the handing over to Britain of the Austi-a-
lian coast defences and the few alleged war
vessels that Australia possesses, but about
this last astounding item there is no definite
information as yet.
* * * *
Under these circumstances all true Aus-
tralians naturally revert to the idea of
creating and gradually strengthening and
building up our own fleet, and against this
view the present British Government has set
its face hard. Its servile subordinates
naturally follow its lead. The ill-bred sneer
of Admiral Fanshawe at the “ petty fleets,”
which would naturally represent the first
beginnings of the Australian marine, reflects
the latest turn of the British political kalei-
doscope. Yet Fanshawe’s tall, bloated
scoff comes badly from the representative of
a country which was glad so very lately to
get the aid of Australia’s petty army, and
which is cadging so hard for Australia’s
petty cash in the shape of a naval subsidy,
and which boasts so much of the petty
dependencies which, added together, make
up its empire. If a separate Australian
marine force is a matter of such utter
absurdity, then a separate Australian land
force is equally ridiculous, likewise a separate
Australian subsidy. Britain’s own marine
force was petty enough at one time, and had
its first beginnings in the days of a petty
potentate called Alfred, of whose petty
doings England brags greatly to this day.
The beginnings of all things are petty, but
that doesn’t show that nothing should begin
at all.
♦ * * • *
The real difficulty lies in the bumptious-
ness of the British officer and the grudging
jealousy of the British politician. A petty
Australian army is strongly commended and
approved—so long as a British general is in
command and does the ruling and the order-
ing about. It is so much approved, in fact,
that schemes have been drafted for placing
it at the disposal of the British authorities
so that, in time of need, it can be sent away
under British commanders to fight Britain’s
battles in other parts of the earth, leaving
Australia practically defenceless. An
Australian navy, provided a British
admiral did the commanding and the
cursing, and could take the vessels where
he pleased, and provided that the officers
were British aristocrats, would be just as
acceptable. In fact Britain is even now
strongly urging that very idea. It proposes
that Australia shall pay for the fleet on the
instalment plan, and supply some at least
of the men, while British officers mono-
polise the cocked hat and the glory and
shout the commands. A petty fleet, so
long as British officers monopolise the
cocked hat and the glory, is no more ob-
jected to than is the petty army or the petty
contribution. The British conviction that
the British person who had courage and
energy enough to go abroad and help to ex-
pand the empire is, necessarily, an inferior
being, and that the British person who
stayed and festered in the old spot is the
only real representative of the empire and
the only person to be entrusted with
authority, is incurable.
m * • •
So long as Australia does not provide for
its own defence its dependence on its
aged mother for protection is the scoff of
the insulting British cheesemonger, and the
British magazine- writer, and the subject of
grave condemnation by the bald-headed man
at the back of the British twopenny omnibus.
If it does attempt to so provide, it is the
jeer of the British horse-marine who ridi-
cules its proposed fleet as petty, simply
because everything must have a beginning.
It is deluged with silly British verse about
“sons of the sea ” and “ boys of the bull-
dog breed,” and it rouses the indignation of
the narrow-minded British nautical character
when it proposes becoming a son of the sea
itself. If the Australian wants to emulate
the partly real and partly apocryphal naval
and military glories of Britain, he may
be a subordinate person in the Austra-
lian military forces with a British
officer over him, or he may (should the
latest Naval Tribute idea become a reality)
be a cleaner of decks and a polisher of brass
fittings on one of the ships of the subsidised
squadron, with British officers over him. If
he aims higher than these things he may go
to England, and become an officer of the
British army by going through the usual
routine—and then be boycotted out of the
service by the gilded cads of the Grenadier
Guards or some similar individuals, for not
being ofi purely English origin. There are
many Australians whose ancestors, before
they became Australian, helped to save
Britain at Trafalgar and Waterloo; also
there are many purely British persons whose
ancestors stayed at home on those dangerous
occasions and pushed the sale of swine and
agricultural products. And these latter now
desire to know what inferior Australian per-
sons can possibly mean by proposing to
meddle with defence matters, except in a
subordinate capacity and in humble subjec-
tion to the superior Briton who stayed at
home.
* * * •
Australia some kind of a military force
of its own, and when Britain lately got into
war, and was being knocked endways by a
foe one-hundredth part of its own size,
that force was placed at Britain’s dis-
posal in a way which, considering that^Aus-
tralia had no voice whatever in the making
of the war, was lavish in the extreme. Aus-
tralia had no interest in the war, and wasn’t
driven to take part in it by any special
personal danger, yet it took part in a toler-
ably large and reckless fashion. It would
be ten times more likely to take part in a
naval war, assuming that it had the
means, simply because in a naval war it
would be personally interested and it
would be urged on by a personal danger.
In view of the very recent past, the
British assumption that Australia can’t
be trusted with a navy of its own be-
cause it would, as a matter of course, refuse
its aid to the empire in time of need is a
shabby, insulting, low-down attitude of
mind. Australia is practically driven to
establish its own naval defence by the bad
faith and the vacillating conduct and the
overbearing attitude of Britain. It sees
Britain, with its Channel fleet, and its
North Sea fleet, and its coast defence
fleet, providing most carefully for the
security of its own shores in time of
war, and the absurdity of the Austra-
lian providing in like manner for the
security of his shores isn’t so visible
to him as it seems to be to the Britisher.
The Australian fails to see why a petty
Australian squadron hereabouts should be
more conte taptible than a petty British
squadron here, or a petty British squadron
anywhere else. The seas are dotted all over
with petty British squadrons, and the land
with petty British garrisons, and the news-
papers with the remarks of petty British
individuals on a large variety of subjects.
The Australian doesn’t see, either, and
possibly never will fully appreciate, the
British idea that a person of British descent,
because he went out to help to expand the
“glorious British empire” is therefore a
“ mere colonial,” only fit to be a private on
land or a swabber of decks at sea. He is
tired of the inferior scoffs of the British
Podsnap. because of his dependence on his
venerable parent for defence, and equally so
of the superior scoffs of the British salt-horse
individual when he suggests providing for
his own defence. He has received notice
from Chamberlain that he must quit the
empire if he doesrc’t protect himself, and he
is denounced as “petty” by the gorgeous
Fanshawe if he does. And these things
make him feel tired of many British persons
and many British circumstances.
Things Copeland Left Unsaid.
Agent-General Copeland, of New South Wales,
has written to the London press in fierce denuncia-
tion of the paper which asked “Is Australia
Bankrupt ?” It was natural that he should do so;
he and the other Agents-General are paid to do
such things, and if they didn’t do them some other
men would presently get their jobs. But though
Copeland replies with great fury he, unfor-
tunately, leaves Australia looking just as bankrupt
as it was before. He dashes about large masses of
rage, figures, and assertions, and doesn’t once get
down close enough to details to touch the real
questions at issue. He even mentions this
country’s luck at cricket and such-like as a proof
that its solvency is past all question. The follow-
ing are some of the points that are in Copeland’s
angry and futile yarn, and the answers thereto—
which represent the points that Copeland un-
happily left out:
Let me now deal seriatim with a few of Mr. Wilson’s
fallacies. He states that “each Australian is saddled
with £59, as against the £l9 per head that the British
population has to support.” Let me show the other side.
.Each Australian is privileged to ride on his own railways,
and, in some States, in his own tramway-cars, and the
fares which he pays go straight to the consolidated
revenue of his country, instead of into the banking
account of some rich railway company, the policy of Aus-
tralia being that the people who own the lands should
also own the railways with which to develop those lands.
And the fact which Mr. Copeland carefully
avoids is that, apart from money spent on rail-
ways, tramways, telegraphs, and telephones, the
Commonwealth owes as much per head as Britain
does. With no costly wars, and with all its
splendid land revenue, it has got as deeply
involved in unprofitable debt in 50 years as
Britain has in 200 years. And no Australian
rides in his own railway cars on the State lines of
this country. He rides in cars which are mort-
gaged to Cohen for the full value they represented
when they were new, and Cohen’s engine drags
him over Cohen’s railway till he reaches Cohen’s
station.
* * * •
Then here is the Agent-General again :
In developing and settling our Australian estate of
1,902,600,000 acres, of which only 113,990,000 acres are, so
far, either alienated or in process of alienation, we have
deemed it advisable to come principally to the London
market, and have borrowed up to 1901 a total of
£205,419,352, of which we have invested the sum of
£130,742,963 in constructing and equipping 13,821,mi1es
of railways, in addition to State-owned tramways. We
have expended £26,673,956 in water supply and sewerage ;
in telegraphs and telephones, £3,741,070, making a total
of £161,158,989, for the use of which the public pay
in the same manner that they would had these works
been carried out by private firms or companies, the only
difference being that the revenue is paid into the public
treasuries instead of into companies’ banking accounts.
In addition to the above, we have expended £44,690,000
in other works and services, such as fortifications, bridges
over rivers, and public buildings, instead of paying rent
to private owners.
The last clause is extra special foolishness, for
Britain and other countries don’t pay rent to
private owners, as a general thing, for “fortifica-
tions, bridges, and public buildings.” As regards
the land still unsold in Australia, it is mostly soil
with a five-inch, or less than five-inch, rainfall, and
practically desert. A huge proportion is sand-
drift, salt, chaos and Sheol. As for the great
“development ” idea, the trouble is that the land
has been developed with borrowed money, and
then it has been sold, and the proceeds, instead of
being used to repay the “development” loans,
have been used as revenue. In the last 21 years
N.S.W. has received £40,000,000 from land, fully
£30,000,000 of this from the sale of land. It has
spent all this money to pay ordinary current ex-
penses and borrowed £40,000,000 as well. The
“developed ” land is mostly sold and the proceeds
eaten up. The money spent on development, and a
lot more money as well, is still owing.
* * * m
Then there is this further outbreak :
“To say, however, that the £161,158,9.89, which has
added enormously to the value of our large public estate,
ought not to be treated as an asset is an infinitely greater
absurdity than to say one of your large railway
Panes was insolvent because it had a large nul»“i
current debentures unpaid, representing the sums it Li
borrowed with which to construct its railwaysjna mnn
assuch railway company would not possess the bounS
areas of land through* which its railway ran as l a
Governments of the several Australian States. Or L
would be thought if a merchant were deemed insolven
because he happened to have an overdraft for sav£in ana
which the banker was only too glad to continue inas’
much as the merchant aforesaid had £20,000 or £3O me
worth of commodities in his warehouse which cmdd hi
turned into cash at any time, just as our railways would
if we were foolish enough to be willing to sell them ’
And the Britisher, reading this, will probablv
almost imagine that in Australia the State does
own the “boundless areas of land through which
its railways run.” But it really owns very little
of them. It is possible to ride by rail from Bris
bane to Sydney, Melbourne and Adelaide and
hardly see any State-owned land in all that vast
distance. The railways did add to the value of
State lands to some extent. Now the lands are
sold, and the proceeds spent with no works to
show for them, and there remains a fading land
revenue, a railway system which returns less than
3 per cent, and which would sell for about two-
thirds of its cost, and nearly £90,000,000 of debt
over and above the money invested in the rail-
ways. And our banker isn’t glad—at least not
very glad—to continue our overdraft just now.
• * «
And then (passing by a lot of less interesting
matter) there is this peroration :
A people like the Commonwealth of Australia, possess-
ing property to the value of £1,083,838,000, with an
annual private income of £221,336,000, exclusive of the
revenues of the various State Governments, do not talk
or think of repudiation, but send annually to Great
Britain £16,644,000, of which £9,016,000 is paid by way of
interest on our Government and municipal indebtedness
and the balance of £7,628,000 finds its way to the pockets
of private individuals as income from property, &e.
I hope I have now said sufficient to answer the question
“ Is Australia Bankrupt?" and I think I may safely say
that, so long as the residents there retain British blood in
their veins, Australia is not likely to become bankrupt •
and at least it is time enough to talk repudiation when
some one of the States is half-an-hour behind time with
its interest or in retiring any matured responsibility.
It sounds very beautiful and convincing no
doubt. But in 1»90 the private wealth of
all Australasia was estimated at £1,169,000,000,
and the public debt was then £192,000,000!
In 1901 the private wealth had dropped
to .£1,083,000,000 —a decrease of £86,000,000;
and the public debt had grown to about
£260,000,000—an increase of £68,000,000. The
private debt had likewise increased, and the absen-
tee had multiplied greatly. Also, the public wealth
held by the State had diminished, for what the
State gains by the ownership of new public works it
more than loses by the sale of public lands. And
Australia doesn't send £9,000,000 a year to England
as interest on its public debt; it sends about
£2,000,000 or £3,000,000 and borrows the rest in
England. And whether, when it can no longer
borrow that £6,000,000 or £7,000,000, it will send
the £9,000,000 to England the Lord only knows,
for nobody has tried that experiment yet.
m • * *
Agent-General Copeland certainly casts him-
self into the fray with tremendous energy, but the
circumstances are against him. And these circum-
stances principally are : This is a country which
borrows more and more as the years go on ; which
spends more and more of its borrowings for purely
revenue purposes and less and less on productive
works ; a country where the aggregate value of
private wealth has been diminishing while its
debt increases; where the deficit has become a
regular institution; where all the revenue and all
the land sale money that can be extracted from
that great, boasted State asse£— the public lands
—doesn’t seem sufficient to keep the public
accounts square (and how these accounts will be
in future years, when there is much more debt and
less land revenue, heaven only knows); a country
which, to pay interest on its unproductive debt
only, has to raise nearly twice as much per inhabi-
tant by taxation as Britain does to cover the
charges on its whole debt; whose roads, railways,
telegraphs, telephones, bridges, tramways, water
and sewerage works, culverts, harbors, dredges,
public buildings, are all owned by Cohen,
the money-lender, and where the debt
§er head is always growing a little larger.
till there is no thought of repudiation
or default —any more than there was thought
of the great bank downfall till it came. There is
no thought of default while the new loans keep
coming in, just as the banks never thought of
default while the new deposits kept coming in.
But the question is: Will there be thoughts or
default or reconstruction when the new loans
cease to come in ? The British blood in our veins
didn’t prevent the great banking downfall. Ihe
British blood in our veins doesn’t alter the fact
that Australians treat default in a municipal
capacity somewhat lightly when the loans stop
and there is only the sordid bill left. And the
British blood is hardly good enough evidence in
any case ; no British community ever had such a
bill as ours to meet before.

The House of 35 Moderately Bloated
Landlords.
What is called a ‘‘satisfactory settlement or
the Victorian deadlock was being arrived a as
The Bulletin went to press last week, it w
“satisfactory” just so far as the lion didn «
the whole of the lamb ; it spared its hind-quarters
and its bleat. The “ reformed ” Parliament is to
consist of a Council of 34 members and one f°i
public service, and an Assembly of 66 and two mr
the public service. For the Council the ele
qualification is fixed at £lO freehold an
leasehold, and the members' ( l ua J l^?a^ l • n c
.£5O freehold. As to the rest, all that is m
any importance is that a process for the
ment of deadlocks is contrived, but con
in such a way that for ordinary Purposes
it is useless. For extraordinary purpo
may serve some day —possibly the day p '
lias concluded that the real reform of its
inent consists in the abolition o
second House. Only in two P 1
visible ways can that real refon
accomplished: one by tacking to a •
to extinguish the Council a provision for p y
a handsome pension to every councillor
sideration of his past invaluable servic ,
other to use this deadlock machinery to
same execution while saving the annuities,
deadlock provisions have thus an im P° ~ t
which, for ordinary legislative purpose.,
not belong to them. Unhappily, as'
marked, they don’t constitute a handy P. ‘
First, the Houses disagree, and the Assemop
dissolves, though it must not dissolve if it ls a
six months of its natural death. .Then, ,
has dissolved and a general election ha
place, the Assembly may wrestle witn
again ; and if it passes the Bill and the sail
culty again arises with the Council, the b
may grant a double dissolution, though, aga ,
dissolution mustn’t occur if it is l«s


nine months or more than 12 months since
the prior dissolution of the Assembly If
by a remarkable run of luck, a dispute has
got as far as this, both Houses “ will at the same
time be required to go before their constituents on
the definite point at issue and to obtain from their
constituents a settlement of the question in dis-
pute.” But there isn’t anything to oblige the
constituent who is more interested in the repair
of the ricketty bridge over the dry creek alongside
Mulligan’s than in any constitutional question—
there isn’t anything to oblige him to speak on
“ the question in dispute ” ; and so there will be
no more certainty that the country's voice on the
constitutional question is sounding through the
Assembly than there was before, and the Council
will probably be no more inclined to bow its head.
And after the double dissolution there will not
be any certainty that its constituents have spoken
on this particular question to the Council either ;
in any case, as they will still be allowed to elect
none but Rich Landlords to the Council, it is
difficult to see how they can give a vote against
the Landlord interest. The Assembly 0 will
send up its Bill, and the Council will say “No,”
and that will be the end of all the elaborate
“reform” provisions. The Commonwealth Par-
liament has deadlock machinery which is like this
up to a certain point; but, instead of leading up
to this final and almost inevitable fiasco, there is
in the Federal scheme a reasonable hope of finding
a way out of the trouble through the joint sitting!
which apparently doesn’t come into the Victorian
State scheme at all. Of course, things may, by a
run of accidents, turnout better than this. The
only at all substantial ground for the hope, though,
lies in the fact that possibly the Council may come
back from its constituents with instructions not to
go on making an ass of itself. Still it isn’t likely;
for, though the qualifications of both elector and
member of the Council have been slightly liberal-
ised, the electorate still consists of essentially the
more Conservative section of the people, while the
House will still remain the House of the Landlord.
The Forty-eight Rich Landlords no longer, per-
haps ; for the future, the House of the Thirty-five
Moderately-Bloated Landlords.
The Riot Act.
At Wyalong (N.S.W.) a sectarian meeting,
addressed by that industrious stirrer-up of the
boiling pot of strife, Rev. Dill Macky, was
violently interrupted by a sectarian mob of
another color ; the Riot Act had to be read, so
serious was the position, and several persons
named Patrick and Michael were arrested. It
is to be reasonably expected that, very shortly,
when Cardinal Moran, or some other violently
indiscreet person of his religious complexion, rises
to make the usual insulting reply, there will be
another row, and another recitation of the
Riot Act, and persons named Ebenezer and
Jabez will be arrested. All this is the
natural and inevitable result of religious
leaders stirring up strife in the name of Heaver,
and of the daily newspapers assisting them witn
reports of their inflammatory speeches. It is as
much the fault of the round, sleek man of God
who is called a “Father,” as of the thin, soured,
scantily-bearded man of God who is called a
“ Pastor,” and what of the responsibility for the
riots they do not bear, falls upon papers like the
Sydney D.T., which search out and record with
huge headingsevery foolish priestly and parsonical
invitation to the other fellow to come and have it
out. But all the damage falls on the community
the damage of the nervous irritation caused by a
dog-fight in the streets, or a melee in a park, or any
other public rowdiness; the damage to property
when sticks and stones fly around ; the damage to
that section of the community which really needs
some sort of a religious belief to keep it decently
behaved, and which is forced by these rows into a
contempt for all religious professions ; the damage
to the great aggregate nose and ear and window
of the community, and the destruction of the
community’s collar, and the casting of mud on the
community’s clothes. And the community—
especially a moderately sane and right-thinking
community like that of N.S.W., which refuses to
allow ordinary non-religious riots, and which won’t
allow industrial differences to run to strikes and
disorderliness—-should take steps to put an end to
this religious larrikinism. Perhaps it would
be a good thing to prosecute any parson
or priest making an inflammatory speech or
sermon, and any newspaper publishing the same.
Perhaps it would be better to insist on all
religious bodies contributing to an insurance fund
to make good any damage to property or to nerves
caused by their leaders. Then the broken win-
dows would be repaired at the expense of the
congregation, and the shattered nerves would
voyage to Europe under circumstances of extreme
comfort out of the same fund. And since what
the congregation had to put into the insurance
fund it would certainly deduct from the Sabbath
thrippence, the priest and parson would ulti-
mately find that stirring up strife didn’t pay.
And no clerical person nowadays is intentionally
guilty of anything that doesn’t pay.

A Victorian Landlord.
The simple-minded reporter who was sent by the
Argus into the Victorian Western district to sing
the praises of the big landlords is still hard at
work, and the resultant articles, printed on the
cable page of his paper, continue to furnish its
country readers with material for scorn and
derision. The following remarkable statements
were published on March 23rd :
The younger generation of these wealthy families, men
of education and refinement.. . have thrown themselves
with praiseworthy energy into the work of rural produc-
tion. They have thrown open their estates, in many
cases on liberal and generous terms, to the tenant farmer
• . . clear-headed, temperate, hard-working, they have
been true captains of rural industry.
The men on the share-dairies receive a penny per gallon
for all milk delivered at the factory, and a share of the
profits on all pigs fattened and calves reared.
The article from which the above extracts are
taken refers solely to the Manifolds, of Cainper-
down, and it is only fair to add that the last-
quoted clause deals with the operations of one
member of the family only, William Thompson
Manifold, of Purrumbete., Throughout the
Western District this “captain of rural industry”
is known as “ Penny Billy,” by reason of the
above-quoted penny per gallon. How on earth he
came to allow the fact to be blazoned abroad in
the Argus is a mystery. The true nature of this
extortion can be seen at a glance. The Camper-
down butter factory has, during the past 18
months, paid its suppliers at the rate of 4.9 d. per
gallon, or practically fivepence. The dairymen on
“Penny” Manifold’s estate, therefore—the men
who do the actual work—receive about one-
fifth of the proceeds of their labor. On share
dairies in this district the usual share of the
dairyman is a third; a few — “Penny” Mani-
fold’s brother Chester, for example have
allowed two-fifths ; wtiile a fourth is considered a
starvation rate. The feat of this captain of rural
industry in securing people who will do the work
for a fifth is, therefore, something extra. It is
airly easy to get at the earnings of the unfortu-
na e tenants. A man milking 60 cows will be
ery fortunate if he can average two gallons a cow
per day through the year. This gives him a daily
age ot LOs. to distribute amongst five milkers,
after paying for food and clothes. The few
pounds received from his “share ” of the profits on
pigs and calves are hardly worth considering,
ane work, of course, is seven days a week, and
anything from 10 to 15 hours per day. Of course,
w .n°l e business would be utterly impossible
re it not for the fact that the dairies are taken
by men with grown-up families, and the young men
and women work simply for their food and clothes
and sixpence to spend when they go into Carnper-
i Saturda - V n '& ht - Certainly the poo r P old
squatter- oving Argcs is, unintentionally, doing
nf S, W ?[ k T ‘Rowing light on the awful methods
of aq u after- land lord ism all over the West. The
tact that it calls the great aggregate Western Vic.
landlord a captain of rural industry ” instead of
a pirate of rustic energy doesn’t make any material
difference in the case.

The Highway Gospeller.
At the recent annual Wesleyan Wail—or was it
Congregationalist Corroboree’—in Melbourne the
necessity for carrying the Gospel forth into the
highways was vigorously asserted by Parson
Edgar. This shepherd has a weakness for loud
assertion. It was he who said a number of awful
things about Tom Bent, what time bull-necked
Thomas was Speaker of the Vie. Assembly, but
when they summoned the reverend gent to the
Bar of the House and asked him what he meant
by it the roar of the accuser dropped suddenly to
a mild whisper. Rev. Edgar’s attempt to play
the dervish reminded one, somehow, of the bygone
Bent episode. He swelled with enthusiasm when
he spoke of scattering the Master’s Word in the
Master's manner, but O ! what a difference when
he came to put his precept into practice in
Bourke-street t’other Saturday night. Probably
Mr. Edgar has a faint sense of humor,
sufficient to show him the absurdity of walk-
ing up and down Bourke-street—a tall plump
gentleman in clerical garments—uttering the old,
old platitudes of professional Gospelism in open
competition with citizens who were crying : “This
way to Rickards’s!” or “ Waxworks' tickets
here ! ” It is so easy for a man to put a bold front
on the platitudes he talks in church. And so hard
for the same man to practise peripatetic preaching
in a crowded unholy thoroughfare as though he
were filled with the Spirit, and really believed
himself to be saving somebody’s soul. Rev.
Edgar’s experiment furnished a striking example
of the hollowness of mere Word preaching. He
delivered the Word as he strode beside the tram-
track, with his hands behind his back, and
as the tram whizzed past the passenger on the
dummy caught a disjointed expression—something
about sin, or blood, or the devil, and he just
remarked to his fellow-passenger that the tall
bloke would get run over if he didn’t take care.
Nobody was aggressive enough to make sport of
the new departure. It was viewed just in the
same blank way that an aboriginal views the
Encyclopedia Britannica. The preacher’s dis-
comfiture was written on his face, and though
Parson Edgar professes to be anxious to carry
some more Word tli rough the streets, if the police
permit, he is not likely to repeat the foolishness
of marching without a drum. A modern highway
preacher needs the moral support of a drum. The
instrument of sound being frankly and aggressively
hollow, is at least more respected (for its honesty)
that the Word which professes to be full of com-
fort and joy. Besides, the drum is the only
universal awakener of mankind’s immortal soul.
Hundreds of millions of sinners in China, India,
and elsewhere haven’t got the Word and don’t
want it, but they are all of them staunch believers
in the everlasting tom-tom.

John See's Explanation.
Premier John See has informed the N.S.W.
public that he is, on the whole, rather proud than
otherwise of the great Cornstalk Public Debt, and
looks upon it as a blessing and a desirable
acquaintance. He does this because but for it
our railways and other public works would have
been impossible. This candid statement opens
up a large and pleasing avenue of considerations
for the State’s creditors, though it is quite pro-
bable that Mr. See didn’t in the least realise that
fact. Here are a few questions suggested by Mr.
See’s observation :
If it is quite impossible for N.S.W. to pay for its rail-
ways and other works out of revenue, then'is it not quite
impossible for it to pay off its public debt ? Paying off its
debt is paying for its works out of revenue.
If It wai quite impossible for N,S,W, to pay fnr it*
public works out of revenue at the beginning of its
career, when it had nothing to meet but the ordinary
charges of Government, what kind of a condition must it
be in now when it has to meet, not only the ordinary cost
of Government, but has to pay about £500,000 a year to
make up the loss on its public works —the amount by
which they fail to meet interest?
If it was impossible to pay for public works out of
revenue when the revenue from land alone was £5 per
inhabitant annually, what show is there of paying off any
part of the debt (which is the same thing) now that the
land revenue is down to about £1 9s. per inhabitant?
If it is impossible to pay for public works out of revenue
—either to pay for new ones by building them without
loans, or for old ones by paying off the loans contracted
to build them with —then do not two things follow : (1)
That, when loans are no longer procurable, public works
must stop; (2) that if, at any time, Cohen refuses to
renew an existing loan, then Cohen must whistle for his
money ?
Lastly, wouldn’t it be better for the O’See Government
to drop the loan subject, seeing that it seems to put its
foot in it a little worse each time it alludes to the matter?
Alleged Senator Reid’s Resignation.
At the time of The Bulletin going to press
there was no announcement that importer Robert
Reid intended to resign whatever claim to sena-
torial office the almost certainly illegal choice of
the Victorian Parliament gave to him. But,
presumably, that announcement will shortly be
made. The matter is not really of importance.
It is almost certain that Robert Reid has no
legal right to be considered a senator, and to
resign what you haven’t got doesn’t leave you
with much less than you had before, nor does it
alter anything very much. And, anyhow, if Mr.
Reid is able to establish a legal claim to a
Senate seat and does decide to take it, the
fact that his firm has been convicted of
frauds on the Australian customs won’t make
him any worse sort of a legislator than any
other typical member of the importing class. If
the seat is to be filled by one of the importing
class —that class whose aim and object is neces-
sarily to damage Australia, to keep the Australian
as a hewer of wood and a plucker of wool for the
peoples of the earth, a poor, slavish creature not
to be entrusted with occupations requiring brains
and enemy—well, it might as well be Robert
Reid as anyone else. It would amount to stupid
prejudice and a wilful blindness to admitted facts
to pretend that Mr. Reid is a dishonest member
of an honest class—an exceptional case of wicked-
ness among his fellow importers. For it is as
clear as noon day that the importers as a class
justified, not only in using
their best efforts to retard the progress of the
country in defence of their unpatriotic calling, but'
also in swindling and robbing the revenue to as
great an extent as possible. They have as little
public honesty as public spirit. The essence of
their business is to import from abroad some fake
of cotton and to sell it to the Australian as silk,
whilst passing it through the Customs as
anything at all on which no duty, or very
little duty, has to be paid. Mr. Reid’s misfortune
is that he has been found out, and that he was
donkey enough to make the matter prominent by
an expensive defence and by alleging that the
prosecution of his firm was a personal-motive
persecution by the malignant Minister Kingston.
Therefore, there is no special reason why Robert
Reid, if he can get a seat, shouldn’t sit in the
Australian Parliament'whilst that body and the
electors admit other importers and agents of
importers,
When Cohen Frowns.
n*r < ci i x ßESl>ONnENT *he on t^lo Tam worth
(N.S.W.) bye-election (hia warning was given
guarded approval in the leading columns next
day)
V ie c^*el causes of the great depreciation of New
south Wales stocks is that the financial authorities at
home are in fear of the alleged Socialism of the labor
party, arid another is the reckless extravagance of the
present Ministry. If Tamworth now elects either a labor
man or a direct supporter of Mr. John See, the news will
be at once cabled to London that the people here are
determined to endorse the Ministry’s past action, and
give it carte blanche to continue in the same way. On
the other hand, if a strong opponent of the Administra-
tion be returned, the effect at home will be very salutary,
and result in a rise in our bonds and in our credit -gen-
eraby. London will then think that the present
Ministry is doomed, and that we shall soon have wiser
and more economical men at the head of affairs.
So, to please Cohen and Co,, London, the Tam-
worth electors should elect Garland ! It is not
the sort of advocacy that is likely to be of
advantage to that gentleman ; in fact it is difficult
to imagine a plea more likely to prejudice his
chances at the polls. But it is of great value,
nevertheless, for it is the first timid note of what
will one day be a great volume of advice to the
same effect. The fate of the private individual
who emulates the Prodigal Hon, and plays the
deuce with his possessions, and mortgages all that
he has and a good deal that he hasn’t, is not in
real life, as a rule, Fatted Calf ; it is hard labor
as bond-slave to the money-lender. The same
fate comes to nations following the same course,
unless they can, when the mortgages fall due.
repudiate, and back up the repudiation against an
armed protest. The borrowing community in time
comes to eat the bread of bitter humiliation, and
to find that it must cringe and crawl before its
financiers, and add its immortal soul, and its flag,
and its traditions, and its honor, and its inde-
pendence to the goods under pledge in Shylock’s
safe. The timid request now that, for Heaven’s
sake, the Tamworth people should abandon their
convictions and their independence and vote for
Garland as the man who will grease the way
which leads to Shylock’s heart is the little cloud,
no smaller than a man’s hand, on the political
horizon in N.S.W.

New Anglican Bishop of Melbourne has evidently
the Marrow of the Matter in politics. To a Mel
bourne unemployed deputation, the other day, he
said
that he was surprised to find in this city that there
were not more mills, factories, and workshops. No great
city could live on itself, it must have established in its
midst productive and remunerative undertakings. This
country was full of raw material. They had wool, timber,
and plenty of coal-mines and precious metals, but this
was not a manufacturing country, and it struck him as
being strange and singular that wool should be sent from
Melbourne to England to be manufactured in the looms
of Lancashire and Yorkshire, and then sent back to Aus-
tralia, not even the high duty imposed making up for the
loss of the people of the country, incurred through not
having manufactured goods on the spot. One of the first
needs was more mills, factories, and workshops, to enable
them to manufacture for themselves, and not depend so
much on other countries outside.
The Rev. Clarke is the most sensible bishop The
Bulletin can just now call to memory. It is, in
fact, a puzzle why such a clear-headed man should
be a bishop.

The people of N.S.W. hear every now and then
of a proposed Bill for the regulation of servants’
registry offices, but the document never makes
much progress. A large section of the community
would be glad to see that Bill get a little extra
velocity on it. As one reason for this : A few
days ago the wife of a Bulletin man engaged a
new maid at Hittmann’s Labor Agency, “head
office, 87 Imperial Arcade, f-__driey ” (no charge
is made to Hittmann for this advt.), and the
usual half crown was paid. In most cases the
girl also pays half-a-crown. About a week after-
wards a long letter was received from Hittmann’s
Labor Agency to the effect that it claimed the
whole of the girl’s first week’s wages (lIs.), and
requesting that Bulletin man’s wife to see that
the girl discharged the obligation.- Being
tired the lady did not exert herself specially
to see that Hittmann’s got 18s. 6d. in all for finding
a girl an eleven shilling job, and the matter rests
there. In New York city all agencies of this kind
are licensed, and the fee is one dollar ; but if the
registry office keeper doesn't find Ms client a job
inside a, month he has to refund the dollar on appli-
cation. And if he takes anything beyond the
regulation fee, a remorseless mayor and corpora-
tion shut his office with a bang. It is a very
simple law, and N.S.W. would do well to copy it.
What view Hittmann’s might take of it The
Bulletin doesn’t profess to know.

of the jury, compelled to sit with 11 bad charac-
ters. Some time ago the subordinates of Jenkins
Iscariot, it is alleged, behaved badly towards a
Dutch ship which happened to be in port there,
and, it being clearly an “external affair,” repre-
sentations were made by ths British authorities
(to whom Holland had complained) through the
Australian Government. Jenkins Iscariot, how-
ever, obviously prompted by Attorney-General
Gordon, refused to recognise any such channel
of communication, which resulted in the British
Government speaking coldly to Jenkins, and
hinting to him that he had greatly over-rated
his own importance. Yet, even now, accord-
ing to a memorandum just forwarded through
the local Lieut.-Governor, “Ministers respect-
fully decline to acknowledge the Common-
wealth Government as an intermediary in
such cases in the future.” What if they
do decline? Even Jenkins probably got, in
his history-book, as far as the story of Canute, or
in some other book as far as Mrs. Partington ;
or, if he only reads contemporary newspapers, he
will remember that in Sydney, t’other day, the case
against Digby Grand proceeded notwithstanding
that, from the prisoner’s dock, Digby Grand
withdrew from the case. Meanwhile the question
has to be considered whether an American is
allowed to throw mud (even if he throws it “re-
spectfully ”) after this fashion at the Imperial
Government on Imperial territory. In his book-
canvassing days Jenkins doubtless declined to
accept a refusal, else he would never have sold
the book, but this isn’t a book question.

and has received the hearty daily newspaper-curse

does or doesn’t. But the Government- reply is
complete : Fegan was a fairly satisfactory Mines
Minister, and is an intelligent, useful man; and
why, if it costs the State nothing, shouldn’t the
State have the benefit of his brains in the Cabinet ?
Also (the Govt, doesn’t say so plainly, but it is,
nevertheless, true) Fegan takes to the Cabinet a
practical mining knowledge which no other
Minister has, least of all the Mines Minister.

ment is that, having joined a Government even as
honorary Miniateri a man cannot thereafter put
Our Own Navee!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
They were calling, “Come and help us in our
fight along the veldt,
And give a fall to Dopper scout and ranger ; ”
So we shouldered gun and bay’net, joined the
Saxon and the Celt,
And we helped to pull their Empire out of danger.
But now they coolly tell us at their banquets
oversea :
'* You’re a happy band of children round your
British mother’s knee,
And she’ll guard you and protect you whatso’e’er
your perils be,
But you’ll never , never do it with your own
Navee ! ”
And our rulers and our statesmen echo back the
silly tale,
And they bid us send abroad a tribute duly ;
For a fleet beneath our banners here would never
stand a gale
Or meet a foreign foe in battle truly ;
We were men when British Tommy met his master
in the Boer,
There was little he could teach us in the grisly
game of war,
And we proved it (ye were witness) we were quite
as good as he,
But we can’t defend our country with our own
Navee !
We have fought with drought and famine—met
the demons in the mine,
Faced the devils they know naught of in the old
land ;
We are sons of men who battled when they’d
crossed the bitter brine
Through the desert-hell to fortune in the gold
land;
We have striven, we have conquered, planted
cities in the waste,
And the land it beareth witness of the perils we
have faced,
And our nation bears a banner now of splendid
Unity,
But we cannot guard our country with our own
Navee !
But they tell us in old Britain, “ Ye have shekels
in your store,
And it is but meet ye render us assistance :
For we fend you from the dangers that may
pounce upon your shore
With the vengeance of our vessels in the
distance.”
Let us answer Britain kindly: “We have pon-
dered well, and Lo,
It were pity we should burden weary Britain with
our foe;
If we prize the fame of freemen we shall keep our
country free
By our native thews and valor—and our own
Navee!

THE BULLETIN will be published
next week on Wednesday instead of the
usual date (Thursday). This alteration
is necessary to suit the requirements
of a large number of the more remote
districts, where delivery would be inter-
fered with by the Good Friday holiday.

HISTORY REPEATS ITSELF.
About the time Australia was taken possession of by Captain Cook, the following
was law in some of the then British Colonies of America : “That whosoever kills, or
suffers to be killed, or sells or disposes of to any person whom he has reason to believe
intends to kill, any Ewe Lamb shall be guilty,” &c., &c.
Most of the thoughtful and patriotic colonists of New South Wales are of opinion
that our Legislature might do worse than enact something of the same kind for the
protection of the remnant of the State’s flocks which have been decimated by,“) e
droughts of the past seven years. If our Statesmen should rise to the occasion and do
what is necessary, Mutton and Sheep’s Tongues would of course be much enhanced in
value. In any case, you can now obtain the ROSEWARNE PACKING CO. o
brand, which is the best in the market, at the same price as they were in the time ot
plenty. Try them; they are delicious, wholesome, and economical.
his own personal brains into the work of Parlia-
ment ; he must thereafter be more or less a phono-
graph. But, anyway, so long as he is in the
Cabinet there is a bit of him in the phonograph ;
while, under our present system of party Govt-, if
be weren’t in the Cabinet he would still mostly be
a phonograph, with the added disadvantage of
barracking for a policy which he had no hand in
making. Perhaps if all the Government followers
were made honorary Ministers—but that idea
wants thinking over.
SUNDRY SHOWS.
