4
“Zaza” ends its tempestuous career at
Sydney Royal on Friday, and after that there will
be a week of gloom and reticence till “Dick
Whittington ” appears on the scene on Saturday
of next week. Williamson and Musgrove are
joint owners of the new spectacle, and it
is understood that they are putting into
it all the combined magnificence they can
rake up. The brevity of Zaza’s career is, in one
sense, a disappointment. “Zaza” is a moral
lesson to a certain extent. It shows that a woman
with a great stormy love in her heart, and a dirty
table-cloth and her other pair of boots on the
table along with the tea-things, and her spare
clothes on the window sill, can’t permanently hold
her own in a man’s affections against a nice
woman with a clean cloth on the table and her
other boots and her spare clothes in their proper
place. To that extent “ Zaza ” inculcates a great
truth, and the fact that it is going away before
the lesson has properly soaked in is to be regretted.
** « 0
“ Thie War of Wealth” fills the closing
nights of Bland Holt’s season at Sydney
Lyceum. It is a localised drama on the
“Breaking of the Drought” lines, but dealing
with mining shares, robbed banks, and other city
troubles. The plot is sufficiently absurd to suit
any respectable audience. Helen Raymond
(Frances Ross) hides in an impossible manner
behind a meagre Australian tree at North Shore
and hears the bad man tell Pa Raymond that
Helen is his wife, “but only in the sight of
Heaven,” and that if Pa wants him to reinstate
her in decent society he, Pa, must help to rob
the Antipodean Bank for him. Helen confides
to'the tree that this anecdote of her fall is a
black lie, but does not rush out, as any other
Australian girl would under the circumstances,
and make remarks. Pa Raymond seems to think it
very reasonable that Helen should have deflected
(perhaps he has been reading Coghlan’s statistics);
anyway, he goes out merely saying, “My Gawd,”
and prepares to rob the bank forthwith from the
noblest of parental motives. Helen allows him to
go, and then appears before the villain and informs
him, as 999 heroines have done before her,
that “she will reveal all.” Whereupon he
waits till she is on the Look-Out, and
then precipitates her into the calm blue harbor.
Naturally Baker and other good people rush to
the Look-Out, and after Helen has had time
to drown comfortably and oompletely ? Baker is
lowered by the flag rope, and reappears with Frances
Ross and himself both wrapped in the flag, the
orchestra braying wildly as at some patriotic
achievement. Act 11. deals with Helen s mind,
which, in the usual novelette manner, has lost all
recollection of the last month; however, she
remembers the proposed robbing of the bank
almost in the usual nick of time, but not quite.
The two good men enter the robbed bank
separately, so that the villain has time to lock
one of them to suffocate in the strong room
before making off with the spoil. Baker has
thus to waste valuable time in blowing open the
strong room with an explosive that happens to
be handy, but as the effect of the explosion
is very fine, the audience forgives him. A
“Run on the Bank” is thrilling in spots, but
the glimpses of an empty-looking George-street
and deserted Post Office are not convincing.
Everything comes right (including the blight
on Helen’s character) at North Shore; the
good people who stood by the Bank and brought it
bags of money are rewarded with shares in a pay-
ing gold-mine, and the villain goes off with three
or four policemen.
* • • *
Miss Frances Ross enacts the heroine with
her usual spirit, and Mrs. Holt has a part that
suits her —as a bright, impulsive woman, with a
noble heart and plenty of good dressing. Miss
Harrie Ireland has nothing to do with the plot,
but acts a wild colonial girl and marries a South
Sea missionary, well played by A. Harford.
Baker is solemn and good, and much as usual, as
the blameless hero; and Bland Holt, as an ex-
squatter, bullooky, and several other things, shows
what he can do with the Australian language at
its worst.
** * m
“On and Off,” at the Sydney Palace,
makes fresh fun at every representation. It is an
exceptionally well constructed farce, and the
“situations,” thanks mainly to Mr. Willoughby
and Miss Watts-Phillips, are made the most of.
The play, whilst travelling with some daring on
the path which leads to “ Zaza,” is always discreet
enough to stop at just the border-line which
divides the giggle of the young person from her
blush. At the same time, it gives the older and
wickeder person opportunities of immoralising on
the probable state, in the original French,
of the incidents that have been Bowd-
lerised. There is a great deal to be said
on behalf of a comedy which can thus be enjoyed
alike by the just and the unjust. The just see an
eminently respectable play. The unjust, with
wider experience, recognise the places where the
jokes that weren’t respectable used to be, and out
of their long-established sinfulness they guess
what these jokes were, and that makes them
almost feel better than if the jokes were really
there.
m* * *
Fitzgerald Brothers’ circus is to open a*
Belmore Park, Sydney, on Saturday. Herr
Pagel, the gentleman who throws lions and
elephants in the air and drags the full-grown boa-
constrictor round the premises like a tail, or
something to that effect, is on the programme.
Likewise the Zenobias, who do a “marvellous
rolling globe act in the dome of the tent ”
(which seems a very unstable place to roll
a globe), and many other marvels, beasts,
acrobats, clowns, horses, girls, dogs, kangaroos,
and impossibilities. The list of performers in-
cludes Oura, Gilleno, Reano, Marotto, Benato,
Pedro, and many others. The programme is too
vast for particularisation, and the evil-smelling
wild animals constitute a side show where they
can be avoided by anybody to whom that special
smell is unwelcome.
*» * *
The “ Lady Slavey ” is in the second week
of its revival at Sydney Criterion, but, after
Friday, will move off, followed by a compensative
benediction for the drama, and a cheerful curse
for some of the actors therein. The Sheridan
season closes with the “ Lady Slavey.”
** * *
The great attraction at Sydney Tivoli now
is provided by Hill and Sylvainy, trick cyclists
from Paris. Whether it is that Mme. Sylvainy
is an exceptionally piquant Parisienne, or because
Hill is more than usually expert, the “turn”
evokes heartier applause than the Tivoli has
known for some time. Fred Rivenhall, a comic
singer, is another good feature on the programme;
he sings with some freshness, and gives the im-
pression that he is at any rate amusing himself.
The average comic singer grinds out his banalities
with a ghastly simulation of humor that suggests
the smile on the jaws of a skull.
#* * *
A revival of “ Ma Mie Rosette ” follows
“The Emerald Isle” at Her Majesty’s, Melb.
There couldn’t be a better advt. of Nellie Stewart’s
versatility than a production of “Ma Mie Rosette”
whilst she is playing Zaza. The name of the opera
is associated with Nellie Stewart and Wallace
Brownlow in this country, and it is a standing
lament with many theatre-goers that they didn’t
happen to see the original Rosette at the time
when Brownlow of Navarre was a new-chum. Ten
years hence, when she is starring in heavy tragedy
Eirts, people will regret that they never saw Miss
lien Stewart as Zaza, and then, by special re-
quest, she will have to appear at a matinee in
selections from all sorts of her best characters,
ranging from Aladdin to Lady Macbeth. A
matinee was the occasion of her last appearance
in comic opera. Some two years ago George
Darrell arranged a benefit for himself at Her
Majesty’s, and he persuaded the principal boy in
the Princess’s panto, (that was Nellie) to play her
old part in the second act of “ Ma Mie Rosette.”
Result, a crowded house and a big success for the
principal item on the bill. Half of the crowd had
never heard of George Darrell, but he doubtless
accepted the overflowing audience as an army of
personal admirers. It is usual for the beneficiare
to reckon himself the attraction. When Melba
sang for the benefit of Armes Beaumont and
brought him a net profit of £750 the old familiar
tenor —but that is another story.
m* « «
Gus Glover, who died in Maoriland last
month, might have been a very distinguished
actor but for his tremendous voice, which took
possession of him some 30 years ago, and made
him its slave. The best work Glover ever did in
Australia was Shakesperian acting, when he tra-
velled with the Bandmann co., and his finest
effort was Othello, because the character of the
Moor allowed him to roll his eyes as much as he
pleased, and gave him just cause and reason for
indulging in great roars of passion. The voice
didn’t confine Glover to a seriously melo-dramatic
style, although it unfitted him for ordinary young
man parts, and put him out of drawing-room
comedy. He was a capital low comedian of the
broad, heavy extravagant type —witness his Por-
thos in “ The Three Musketeers,” and his Yankee
colonel in ‘‘ My Partner ” —but Gus’s colonel is
almost as forgotten as his Othello. In fact he
was the one man in Australia who had played
Bumble the Beadle almost as effectively as he
played Bill Sykes. Nobody could present a more
lurid stage ruffian, drunk or sober, than the ruffian
of Augustus the Terrible. Anything in that way,
from murder to delirium tremens, came easy to
the man with the Voice. His last character was
Cregan in “ Sherlock Holmes,” a small but con-
genial part. When Professor Moriarty wanted a
hideous crime committed he made a point of order-
ing Cregan to be laid on. “ Cregan for that.”
G ENERAL HECTOR MACDONALD, who
committed suicide, a few days ago, in Paris.
Photo, by Eden, Sydney & Melbourne.
Poor old Glover! He will send no more people to
their last account.
** * *
Musgrove’s experiment with “ A Midsum-
summer Night’s Dream” must bid, in a great
measure, for the patronage of a generation that
has grown up since the time when George Rig-
nold staged the fairy tale so beautifully. All who
remember Rignold’s show (Australia owes its
brightest recollections of Shakespeare to “ Hand-
some George ”) will go and pay their respects to
Musgrove’s production, as a matter of course.
But the question of profit or loss for the manage-
ment will rest with the young people. “Mid-
summer Night’s Dream ” is a spectacular poem
assisted by music. It is a delicate appeal to the
artistic taste and imaginative faculty of that
general public that roars at “ local allusions, ” sub-
mits to advt. curtains, and prefers melodrama to
any other sort of serious play. All the money-mak-
ing plays produced of late—from “Sweet Nell”
down to “ When London Sleeps ” —have been
melodramas. Perhaps Shakespeare’s unique
comedy is for that reason likely to prosper exceed-
ingly. And perhaps it isn’t. Musgrove is an
interesting speculator, anyhow. Hilda Spong,
then a slim young damsel, played Titania, the
Fairy Queen, in Rignold’s setting of the “Dream,”
and Miss Watt-Phillips was her King. Rignold
was Bottom. John Cosgrove and Cyril Keightley
were the two young men mortals, and Miss
Claudius appeared as Hermia. The Puck was
Miss Mabel Russell, who nowadays assists her
sister (Mrs. Jack Parsons) in managing a Mel-
bourne pub. And Ethel Buckley, the cockney
gal of Anderson’s co., frisked around in the
juvenile capacity of Cobweb, an elf.
*# # *
The Woods-Williamson Co. finished its
struggle at Melbourne Bijou on Saturday night.
“ Hands Across the Sea ” is too large a melodrama
for the Bijou stage; all the same the work of
Pettitt on any stage is a blessed thing as melo-
dramas go, and if the W.W. Co. could manage
to retire temporarily from the quarter-
deck of a British ship without seeming
to walk into the ocean every time its per-
formance would win greater respect. For the
next few nights the theatre will be closed, re-
opening in a swept and smartened condition on
April 11th, when the Willoughby-Geach Co.
appears in a new farcical comedy.
** # *
At the Opera House the impending change
of programme is slow to happen, but it may be
expected to occur by Easter at the latest. The
temple of Rickards will inaugurate its Easter
season in a fitting manner by holding a
Sacred Festival on Good Friday. The fes-
tival will be directed, it seems, by Napoleon
Boffard, also Madame Gay, and other well-,
known concert singers, will assist, and the story
of a Christian martyr, “with living pictures,”
is promised for the occasion. The living pic-
tures of a martyrdom should be both gory and
improving.
*« * *
The Anderson co., now in the last lap of
its long Melbourne season, is passing hence to the
slow music of “Honor Thy Father” or “The
Fatal Wedding.” “Honor Thy Father ” sounds
a little unfair to the heroine’s mother, while
“ The Fatal Wedding” is also open to objection.
From beginning to end the plot leads to nothing
so cheerful as a wedding. At the start, the father
of the deceived village-beauty is “dishonored,”
not by his chee-ild’s wedding, but by her omission
thereof. The villain’s other dupe (an heiress, by
the way) at one stage goes so far as to send her
bridesmaids to church on foot, while she herself
follows in carriage and pair to be ecclesiastically
joined to the schemer. But the bad man’s sin finds
him out just in time to postpone the function,
indefinitely. Villain Willard has a miserable
time, although he describes himself as “clever.”
Whenever injured Innocents call him hard names,
which they do as often as he injures them, he
smites them with his heavy-headed stick. This is
foolish, for on the stage muscles and morals
always go together, and on Saturday one grew
quite accustomed to seeing Hero Inman or
Father Carey grasp the Scorpion by his side-
curls preparatory to forcing him to his im-
penitent knees. In due course, the Viper’s
knaveries lead him to visit a lunatic asylum,
where he has immured the village-beauty’s dis-
honored father (Carey). The dishonored mother
(Helen Fergus) arrives there, too, to rescue her
husband, with the result that escaped lunatics dis-
honored parents, asylum-keepers, a doctor’and
the villain are all tumbling over one another
in the same room with an electric torture-
seat, invented by a mad scientist (,T. H. Nunn).
Saturday night the gallery was greatly worried
for fear the wrong people might squat on the
bogie-stool, so it came as a relief when Sinner
Willard was impounded, per favor of the mad
scientist, and blinded by electricity. Unre-
formed by blindness, he behaves worse than ever
keeps the torture-chair, with improved agonisers’
in his study for the undoing of Inman, and coaxes to
the altar on false pretences heiress Mercy (Ada
Lawrence). The quality of Mercy is not strained
-that is to say, she’s not too particular to take
up with a new love vice her slandered old flame.
If the “Fatal Wedding” had not been post-
poned, Mercy would have become a villain’s
bride and lived happily afterwards. Ada Guild-
ford, as the fallen angel, dies on the day appointed
for the fatal wedding, and becomes a risen angel in
view of the audience. Expiring a thin, brunette
penitent, she departs heavenwards, a blonde,
buxom seraph. Bert Bailey, as a cheerful mis-
creant, helps Ethel Buckley to sandwich the layers
of villany and woe with passable scraps of low
comedy ; and the acting all round is good. Some
of the woe grows wearisome, but the performers
deserve credit for not making it insufferable.
** * m
“The Fortune-Teller” has another week
to run at Melbourne Princess’s, and meanwhile it
proceeds as usual. Perhaps the ballet girls, who
take their tuneful dancing lesson in a court-yard,
are rather lighter footed than at first, but their
costumes are a little more dusty, so the general
effect of those pleasing pupils remains about the
same. Also the Count, who wants to marry into
the ballet for mercenary reasons, continues to be
a beauteous young man of the barber type,
although his intended victim speaks of him as
an old horror. “The Fortune-Teller” is a fair
success, in spite of numerous faults in the per-
formance. A reconstructed co. might make it
better than a new opera at some future time when
the “ Hungarian ” music has been acclimatised by
the local barrel-organ. Which suggests that
managers ought to seriously consider the question
of employing barrel-organists as advertising me-
diums. The best class of popular music obtains
the bulk of its popularity through being ground
into the public soul by the man who turns the
handle.
** * *
“A Midsummer Night’s Dream” follows
“The Fortune Teller” on Easter Saturday. A
company has been specially imported to deal with
this Shakespearian matter under the direction of
Robert Courtneidge, whose old and varied ac-
quaintance with Australian audiences entitles him
to predict that the forthcoming show will astonish
them.
** * *
“ The Emerald Isle,” at Her Majesty’s, is
also fated to retire on the eve of the annual
Holy Bun. Melbourne speaks kindly, but not
excitedly, of the serio-comic opera. Carrie Moore’s
weird endeavor to assume an Irish accent is
deplored, because Carrie’s casual remarks and
solemn appeals to “Bli-i-nd Murphee” break the
charm of her natty little Colleen character, and
discount the value of Murphy Bathurst’s brogue.
Another unfortunate drawback to the entertain-
ment at Her Majesty’s is Lauri’s singing voice.
Two of the most humorous numbers in the opera
make a demand upon Lauri’s warble, one of them
being a song and dance for himself and Maud
Chetwynd. Neither artist is very musical, except
about the feet, but in a top-note competition with
Hugh Ward they would doubtless carry off the
honors. Ward makes full atonement for his
minor indiscretions as a vocalist by .dancing Irish
jigs and reels until he drops, and subsequently
that fine comedian crowns his success by walking
off with an elderly piper who can very well be
spared.
# * # *f
An exhaustive statement of Wirth Bros.’
Circus can wait until next week, when the tent
will be the only Melbourne theatre which isn’t
changing its bill. The Wirth show was to have
opened last Saturday at 8 p.m., but a mighty
thunderstorm clashed with the arrangements and
smote a portion of the electric-light plant, and
there was wetness over the land, and the perform-
ance didn’t take place. Judging from the number
of people who arrived early and got their
money back, and the larger number who were
turned away from the pay-box the Circus
has come to supply a want. The tiger on the
tight-rope, likewise the football dog and “ the
whole avalanche of amazing attractions” were
introduced to Melbourne notice last Monday
night. The Howard Bros., a special item in the
avalanche, had been previously introduced at the
Athenaeum Hall. They are a remarkable—in
fact, a startling—improvement on the old masters
of second-sight business. A blindfolded Howard,
who talks like a red-hot cockney in a furious
hurry, gives descriptions of the artioles which a
lynx-eyed Howard is handling as he moves
among the audience. The Howard who draws the
information from his partner has a unique kind of
American accent. The swiftness of the blind seer
is wonderful. He states the character and the
number of a watch or a hat, maker’s name and
owner’s name, almost as soon as the other fellow
has discovered them. And sometimes be can fire
off a little volley of facts without getting a word
from the Howard with the lynx eye and the un-
paralleled dialect. At these moments the mind of
the seer is vaguely understood to be at work in
his brother’s head, or vice versa.

Mr. Mephan Ferguson, of Melbourne, reports
that there has been a great improvement in the general
outlook of the engineering business since the new year
started, and that his pipe making works in Melbourne
and Perth are working full-handed. Amongst the orders
now being executed at Footscray works is one for 7 miles
10 chains of 18in. diameter light steel spiral-riveted pipes
for the Victorian water supply; and at Perth works a
contract is in progress for over 6 miles of lOjin. diameter
spiral-riveted pipes, flanged joints, for the Greenbushes
Tin-Mining Syndicate. About two miles of these pipes
are to stand a test pressure of 8001 b. to the square inch."

A. Kozminsky, 106 Queen-st., Melbourne,
having returned from England, is now prepared to
purchase paying mines, and mining properties (not
merely surface shows), situated in any part of
Australasia requiring further capital for development and
machinery. Only genuine and legitimate ventures,
capable of bearing the strictest examinations will be
entertained.*
It is not generally known that in the treatment
of ore as much ot the valuable minerals is often lost in
the tailings as is saved by the appliances. This is due
in most instances to the inefficient machines used and
the difficulty in saving the fines and slimes. The best
machine to treat the latter classes are Luhrig Vanners.
These machines are in successful daily use in the principal
gold, galena, copper and tin mines in Australia and
Tasmania, proving their superiority over other so-called
slime machines. Messrs W. & J. Lempriere, 506 Little
Oollins-street, Melbourne, are the general agents, and full
particulars can be obtained from them.*
Norman Godkin, of 31 Queen-street, Melbourne,
is prepared to purchase or develop, with option of
purchase “big prospecting shows” (either gold or
mineral) in any part of Australia. Properties offered
must be subject to inspection. Cable address : “ Option,”
Melbourne.*

The attention of all mine managers and others
interested in mining is directed to the advantages claimed
for the Wilfley Concentrating Table. First: It will
handle several times more material than any belt concen-
trator made without any exception. Second : It will
save a higher percentage of the values than any other
table, and will catch any float gold, amalgam or quick-
silver which may escape from the amalgamating plates.
Third : It will make cleaner and more valuable concen-
trates ; thus saving useless expense for transportation of
valueless material, and also reducing smelters’ charges.
Fourth : It requires little or no attention when once
adjusted and no expert is required either to adjust or
to run the machine. Fifth : It handles the pulp with
less power and less floor space per ton than any other
concentrator. Sixth : It has no expensive belts to crack
and wear out, and no complicated machinery to keep in
repair. Illustrated catalogues with valuable notes on ore
dressing can be obtained free on application to the sole
Manufacturers in Australia, the Austral Otis Company,
South Melbourne*

(W.A.), on retiring from that mine, purchased the Pine
Lodge mine at Niagara in conjunction with his brother
George. After several months of development, a test
crushing of 211 tons has just been treated for a return of
4660 z. Mine is looking well, and appears to have a bright
future.

The Wild Cat Column.
On Jan. 1 next, Vic. bonds for 5$ millions will
fall due; before the end of 1910, Vic. and other Australian
States will have to provide for 24£ millions. Nearly the
whole of this money was borrowed at 4 per cent. ; it was
shown the other day that N.S.W. cannot now borrow
at that price. What is going to be done about this
25 millions or so 7 With care and honesty, and a
run of fair seasons, credit may. and, no doubt, will, im-
prove ; but it certainly isn’t likely to get very far a’long
that road before Vic. has to do something with regards
to its first 5£ millions. And the question arises, seeing
that the 25 millions has to be provided for somehow*
whether it would not be best to induce the Australian
Government to take over the debts, and undertake a
conversion. From several points of view the present
appears to be a most suitable time for such
a movement. In 1898, before the Federal
Convention struggled with the financial problem
and, in the end, more or less swept it into thi
corner and left it there, N.S.W. 3 per cents, were at 101.
Lately they have been at 87. Now, when conditions of
boom, or even of normal prosperity, prevail—such as
prevailed when the State stock was at 101—there would
be no anxiety to change one security for another, even
though the latter might be somewhat better ; the thing
that the people already held would look good enough.
And, of course, they couldn’t be compelled to change it ;
if you have agreed to supply a man with aone-eyed horse’,
you can’t comoel him to accept an animal with two eyes.
When, however, State stocks are down to 87, the money-
lender is naturally a good deal more anxious about his
security ; the price proves it. And, being anxious in that
way, he will naturally he a good deal more inclined to
jump at a better security with both feet, even though its
superiority is no greater than it was when formerly he
didn't reckon it worth while to jump at all. If a con-
version scheme is ever worth while, it is, therefore, prob-
ably worth while just now. What the saving would be
The Bulletin does not know. But whatever the saving,
much or little, it ought all to go one way—not in reduc-
tion of interest (which would only mean reduction of
already insufficient taxation), but into a sinking fund for
ultimately getting the country out of pawn.

• * *»
Treasurer Gardiner, of Westralia, wnere the
surpluses come from, lately took the trouble to explain
that he still believed in having an “uncle” and a debt.
He seemed almost sorry in a way that his State wasn’t
spending more than it earned; and, in order, presum-
ably, to clearly show that he has not sunk into the heresy
of thinking that it is good to pay your way as you go, he
has now announced that he will ask for a quarter of a
million at an early date. There is less excuse for Mr.
Gardiner than for some other Treasurers, because, prac-
tically without an effort, and in spite of what is alleged to
be a much over-manned and badly-organised public
service, a surplus of £280,574 has been accumulated
during the eight months of the current financial
year. Moreover, if any country in the wide world ought
now to pay its way, and ought to have done so for the
last eight or 10 years, that country is Westralia. Great
as many of its mines are, they will, in the natural course
of things, peter out some day; for a mine isn’t like a
potato-patch, which will go on growing potatoes eternally
provided that certain essentials are complied with.
Every public work, therefore, which serves a mining field
ought to be charged with a stiff sinking fund ; and most
of the works built in Westralia have been designed more
or less to serve the goldfields. Nine times out of 10—99
times out of 100—the surface stuff of a mine is the richest;
and if in the early years of its life a field doesn’t pay for
its public works out of its cream, it is generally hopeless
to expect the skim-milk to do it subsequently.

* • • *
The disgustingly fat A. A. Co., which had the
rare luck to strike this country before a way had been
found into the inland desert, and which has not, there-
fore, come to regard a drought as all in the day’s work,
refuses to regard its special fodder bill last year as a
proper charge wholly against the year ; it is going to
spread the total expense, set down at £49,734, over a
number of years, and begins with £20,000. This cuts
down the profits to £61,228, out of which it is only pos-
sible to pay 255. per share—a beggarly 6 per cent.; and it
was 17£ per cent, in 1900. But though the co. refuses to
charge all the year’s drought cost against the year, it
isn’t above taking credit for all the extra profit derived
from the wool clip, including the rise in price from 7fd.
to about 9d., comparing the last two years. To make
things square, why not spread that over a few years too?

perhaps, when Premier John See brings the State security
to the pop-shop, he will be asked if he can get the Ga B
Go’s endorsement.

There is cable news that the London Bank
ok Australia has adopted the heroic measure of writing
its capital down by £368,272 ; which is at the rate of
£7 10s. per share, in order to make “ the fullest provision
for losses of stock and depreciation.” The paid-up capital
previously stood at £915,865, of which £743,935 was
represented by ordinary and £171,930 by 5£ p.c. pre-
ference shares—the preference shares for which the wise
depositors exchanged their 4£ p.c. deposits in 1893. On
the cabled figures, on which it certainly isn’t possible to
build much and be sure of it, it looks as though profits
had shrunk considerably during the past year. They
are said to total £85,206, of which the 4£ p.c. deposits
absorb £71,516, leaving a shareholders’ profit of £13,690,
against £23,354 in 1901; but the latter figures were a
very substantial advance on anything the reconstructed
bank had previously known. Another 18 months’ divi
dend is provided for the preference shareholders, and this
payment for an extra six months wipes out the last of
the arrears. Reconstructed deposits compare with those
of the past in this way :
London Bank’s Reconstructed 4£ p.c. Deposits.
Dec., 1896 .. £3,716,087 Dec., 1900 .. £1,911,964
Dec., 1897 .. 3,260,678 Dec., 1901 .. 1,591,092
Dec., 1898 .. 3,159,09 S Dec., 1902 .. 1,569,271
Dec., 1899 .. 2,232,675
The progress in wiping off this incubus—for a 4£ p.c.
debt of this sort is an incubus—is not too satisfactory.
The year’s profits apparently equal the year’s preference
share dividend and leave £4234 to carry forward, bring-
ing up the reserves (so far as any of these things can be
yet stated definitely) to a total of t'10,024. On the paid-
up capital as proposed to be written down, the surplus
would yield £1 2s. 6d. percent.

* * • *
The city of Sydney, which has been paying a
one-and-tenpenny rate, is going to have its burden re-
duced to Is. 9d.; and this alleged saving of Id. in the
£ is presented as a great victory and a justification of the
Reform policy. Some day, possibly, even aldermen will
realise that reform doesn’t mean saving a ha'penny by
not buying a cake of soap. There are so many—so very
many—things that a civilised, up-to-date municipality
should do which Sydney leaves undone, that its case calls
loudly for more rates—not less. Moreover, in most other
places the case would not call in vain. Here, for
instance, is a small list of the rates which a few English
cities pay, and which presumably they find it pays them
to pay —anyway, they go on paying them, and prosper:
s. d. s. d.
Sheffield .. ..8 5 Bristol 7 4
Middlesborough .. 8 1 Manchester .. ..7 3
Nottingham.. .. 7 8 Leeds .. .. ..7 3
Stafford .. ..7 6 York 7 2£
There are some municipalities which pay more even than
this ; but the foregoing list is restricted to cities which
are making rapid progress and are more or less earning
a commercial reputation which Sydney might envy.
* » * *
x ne rumor that the Maoriland Govt, intends to
do something definite in regard to the £2,000,000 which
was guaranteed to the Bank of New Zealand in da} T s of
trouble and adversity grows a little less vague. The
idea is now said to be “ to convert the loan into per-
manent stock, thus giving the Government control of the
institution.” That plan, or almost any other, is better
than the existing arrangement, which was rushed through
wildly before breakfast one morning in 1894, and is just
the sort of thing which, in those circumstances, one would
expect it to be. Its most conspicuous virtue is that its
life is limited to 10 years ; its most conspicuous vice is
that, no matter how much profit the bnnk may make, the
Government is not empowered to draw more than 4 per
cent., which is exactly what the £2,000,000 costs the
Government. As to controlling the institution, the
Government has been doing that for some time past :
four of the six directors are appointed by the Governor-
in-Council, and instead of either the heavens or the profits
falling in consequence of this “ fiddling with Socialism,”
the dead loss shown in the last year of private manage-
ment changed to a profit of £289,502 in 1902 ; and no
serious reflection on the heavens has yet come to hand
either.

One great reason why the usually accurate Trea-
surer Turner persistently finds the revenue ahead of his
calculations is that his colleague. Customs Minister King-
ston, is constantly dropping on the Freetrade importers’
customs swindles and bringing in unexpected revenue.
Something like £300,000 of much-needed cash will
probably be gained this year, merely by the stoppage of
old-established frauds and steals done by the bell-toppered
gang of whited sepulchres.

Cobar Gold Mines (N.S.W.), with 100 stampers,
and Mount Boppy, with 40 stampers, run a close race for
supremacy. January return of the former is valued at
£9163 ; working and development charges £5457, leaving
the substantial amount of £8696 to the good. Boppy had
22180 z. from 2550 tons. With increased treatment
appliances, the immediate erection of which would be
quitfc justifiable, Boppy would be far and away the lead*
ing gold-producer of the State. What a difference it
would have made to the sanguine Eissler if he had made
this the “star” mine of the Anglo-Australian Exploration
Co.’B holdings instead of the Gibraltar or Gallymont 1
• • • •
Lawyer Lyons is persevering with the old
Junction Deep mines near Mandurama (N.S.W.). These
mines have achieved distinction in the past as the
disillusioners of inventors of appliances for saving fine
gold, and as wreckers of the fortunes of honest, enter-
prising men. Nothing is now being done with the large
bodies of decomposed iron pyrites. The deeper ground
is being worked for sulphide ore, which is treated in a
water-jacket blast furnace —that which was built for the
ill-fated Milburn Creek copper mine—copper ore being
purchased as a base. The plant is now idle for want of
water. If individual exertion will achieve it, Lyons will
get from these mines what no one else ever succeeded in
getting—a fortune.
• * * •
A strange story is in circulation respecting a
N.S.W. coppermine. Co. was floated about two years
ago on most glowing reports. Capital was freely sub-
scribed, and as freely spent on extensive development
work and in making preparations for prospective produc-
tion on a large scale. On reports that the work done did
not reveal prospects which would justify further expen-
diture mine was closed down, the shaft dismantled,
and most of the machinery removed. Allegation now
made is that a really valuable lode was passed through in
the deepest workings. This assertion may be absolutely
false, or it may be a wild exaggeration, but the fact
remains, as The Bulletin learns on good authority, that
the majority, if not all, of the men who worked in the
mine are eager to get it on tribute on anything like
reasonable terms. The mine has been practically aban-
doned by the co., yet it is said that fair prosposals made
by the working-men were promptly rejected.
• • * •
The bodies of the two men who, three months
ago, got under the falling ground in the deep levels of
the South Broken Hill and Central mines, are still buried ;
and, as the coroner cannot sit upon them, the Govern-
ment has appointed him a commissioner to inquire how
it happened, anyhow. So far as it has gone, the inquiry
has resolved itself into a row between the managements
of the two mines interested. Each accuses the other of
breaking a compact not to gouge out the ore within 15ft.
of its boundary. The commissioner has to suggest means
to prevent similar accidents, and it probably will not be
hard work for him to do it—he will propose legislation
compelling mining companies to leave pillars of ore
standing on their common boundary-line. Something of
the kind has been repeatedly recommended by mining
inspectors ; why the recommendation hasn’t been adopted
is a mystery which it will probably take at least two royal
commissions to solve.
• • •
A little of the big advance in .eaa has been .ost,
though the price still keeps about £l3, or £2 3s. better
than at the opening of the year. It is significant that
the jump hasn’t yet been explained. Advices at the end
of last year were that, though statistics were unfavorable,
the price was going up ; apparently it was expected that
the huge American Smelting and Refining Co., which
controls the bulk of the ’Murkan output, was “ going to do
something.” If that is the explanation—if, that is to say,
the jump is simply the result of monkeying with the
market —outlook is not very bright. The distinctly un-
favorable point about the statistics is that, whereas in
1900, when the mean price was £l6 17s. 9d., the imports
to Great Britain were 195,380 tons, in 1902, when the mean
was £lO 19s. 6d., the imports reached 231,818 tons. This
certainly seemed to show that the world could go on
supplying lead in increasing quantities at the lower price,
though for how long isn’t bv any means certain. Those
who ought to know believed that a steady advance would
come ; but certainly the statistics offered no encourage-
ment to expect anything rapid. What has most likely
happened is that, recognising that' a steady move was
assured, a few big producers have combined to discount
the future by rushing the price up at one step.
Copper also has moved back a trifle, the price at
the opening of the market this week being £64 17s. 6d. ;
but this is £l2 12s. 6d. better than the quotation with
which the year began. The large movement is also
alleged to be due mainly to ’Murkan operators, who
control this market more absolutely than they do even the
lead market. The Wallaroo and Moonta (S.A.) directors,
who have had longer experience with the metal than any
other body this end, have settled down to the conviction
that it isn’t the slightest use attempting to forecast
fluctuations. Meantime the ill-paid Moonta miners are
making out a good case for the advance in wages which
was more or less promised when they accepted the
reductions that followed the slump of 1901. The only
other notable movement in S. Aus. copper mining, the
result of the present jump, is the decision of the French
co. which owns the Parramatta mine, a mile or two from
Moonta, to turn its tailings dumps into cash by the now
popular cementation process.
• • • •
Its tailings dumps last year saved the Wallaroo
and Moonta Copper Co. (S.A.) from showing a loss. The
gross profits were £29,957 ; but from this £24,793 is
written off for depreciation of plant, leaving only £6164
to go forward. As the profit on the re-treatment of the
tailings was £26,247, the co. would have shown a deficit
of £2i,083 on the year’s operations but for this new idea
of cementation or salt-water leaching. Wisely, no doubt,
the silver-lead smelting works having proved so sorry a
fiasco, the directors write off the cost as quickly as
possible ; and £9325 is charged to last year on account
of that dead and buried experiment. As to the
mines, it becomes every year clearer that the
end of Moonta is not far away, although the
lower-grade Wallaroo property maintains its old pro-
ductiveness. The two mines together only showed a
gross profit last year of £8978, compared with the
£26,247 got out of the formerly-discarded tailings ; and
the supply of tailings can’t last for more than, say,
another six years.
The cementation process, as carried out at
Moonta and Wallaroo, where it was introduced a couple
of years ago hy a Spanish engineer, consists simply of
digging holes in the tailings dumps, loosening the sur-
face, pouring in sea-water pumped up from the Gulf a
few miles away, and throwing old horseshoes and other
iron scraps in’the ditches into which the copper-stained
water trickles at the foot of the dumps. The iron lays
hold of the copper, and the metal is then scraped off m
the form of a paste, dried, and smelted. By the time it
reaches the furnace the cost per ton is £lB ; by the time
the metal reaches the ship 50 per cent, has been added to
this.
• • • *
Adelaide Critic, of 28th March, opines that
The Bulletin has a “ serious pain in the stomach ’’ over
the Arltunga field. Par. was written, probably, on the
25th, when Weinecke’s Reward shares were £llO, but on
date of publication they were below £6O. Critic must
now have an uneasy sensation in its lower chest. “ Ade-
laide brokers have not boomed Arltunga,” it is alleged ;
«■ the field has talked for itself,” which is quite true—it
has been all talk. Paul Northev is now the best-cursed
man in Adelaide ; even the saintly newspapers join in
denunciation of him ; he is only a Ballarat man, and his
opinion is of no consequence. The dozens of other prac-
tical men who have sent condemnatory telegrams are
ignored, which is supreme flattery for Northey. As a
matter of fact, the only persons who, up to date, have sent
favorable reports have been Dr. (whatever that may
mean) Chewings and the “ special correspondents” of the
Adelaide papers. Chewings’ latest message is a bombastic
defiance of all the practical men ; he is conscious of the
soundness of his own judgment, and w-ill be content to
let the battery silence the croakers. The Bulletin chal-
lenges Chewings to publish a comparison of his estimates
of the value of several reefs in and around Ooolgardie and
the actual battery results, and to Btate how many mines,
the purchase of which he recommended, are now being
worked. The statement will be promptly published when
he sends it. As for the opinions expressed by “ our
specials,” they are worse than valueless. No Adelaide
paper was ever known to employ a qualified man to do
mining work ; the general-utility man has had to tackle
it, even though he could not distinguish ore from
mullock—a polite variation of a Oornishism common to
the bowels of the earth.

How some of the shows floated during the 1902
Charters Towers boom are fairing. The Queen Central
Block is inviting applications for 14,650 forfeited shares.
Several other shows have swags of their contributing
shares in their offices, thrown in owing to inability to
pay calls. With such a large amount of divs. leaving the
field, it is doubtful if many of the shows will succeed in
getting in sufficient calls to keep working. Exemptions,
partial and total, have been common lately.

Those who knew him knew that it would not
be long before general manager Greenway discovered
that new machinery was necessary at Chillagoe (N.Q.).
That is his weak point—he will pile up plant. His pro-
posal to spend £lO,OOO on concentrating machinery
amounts, however, to an admission that the ore isn’t of
the grade it was cracked up to be.
• • • *
Bendigo divs. last week totalled £12,524, of
which the Three Moon mines—Clarence, South New Moon
and New Moon—contributed £7lOO. A long succession of
handsome profits from these mines is assured. Interest
in deep-level mining on the field has been revived, as the
result of a payable crushing from the deepest workings
in Lansell’s 180 mine, taken out by tributors. There is a
large body of stone, the trial from which returned Bdwts.
per ton, which is highly payable—at Bendigo. It is
about time that some new development took place on
the New Chum line, which, in olden times, was the main-
stay of the field, but which of late years has been an
absorber of profits from other lines. It will not be long
before the New Chum Railway is testing the ground at
about 4000 ft.

A Victorian stock worth watching at the pre-
sent time by people who are disposed to treat mining as
an investment business is Long Tunnel Extended.
Within the next fortnight the lode should be intersected
in No. 21 crosscut, and if the expectations of the manage-
ment are realised, shares are bound to have a good rise.
The new battery will be ready for work in August, when
co. will show a larger proportion of profits to earnings
than it has done in the past.

The mild and gentle Kanaka has been
displaying his virtues at Mackay (Q.) rather
prominently. One of the sweet and patient
islanders was in gaol charged with murdering,
under revolting circumstances, a white girl.
Knowing the good character of the Kanaka, the
gaol people allowed him to exercise with four
other prisoners. He seized an axe and mur-
dered one of them, and, when a warder appeared
on the scene, murdered him, too. Then authority
reluctantly did violence to the colored brother,
and he is now enjoying the Christianising influ-
ence of Queensland life in a solitary cell.
An inquest at Melbourne Morgue on the
remains of a former inmate of the local Benevo-
lent Asylum revealed the fact that the rule of that
charitable institution is to transfer the shirt of a
defunct lodger to a survivor. Sometimes the name
of deceased is marked off his underwear, and
sometimes it isn’t. In the case which came to
light at the inquest aforesaid, the name of the pre-
vious wearer had not had a line put through it.
Probably it was quite a young shirt, likely to be
passed on to a long succession of wearers, in which
case the marking-off rule would seem calculated to
have a depressing influence upon distant future
tenants of the garment. The man who got into it
in its last stage of decay would feel like a walking
death-roll of Benevolents as he sang his doleful
“Song of the Shirt

A clerical witness in a recent Melb. will
case said that testatrix, whom he had always
regarded as a practising, as well as professing,
Christian, on one occasion swore for 15 hours con-
tinuously. This lamented sister would come in
handy just now to adequately express public
opinion on several subjects.
PALPABLY TRUE.
TALL PARSON (who has been to hear the old man
preach): “But, my dear brother, didn’t you
put the butter on rather thick?”
THE OLD MAN : “Well, well! It’s only bread
and butter, after all.”
An incident at Parnell, near Auckland
(M.L.), to some extent explains why so many
Boers were left alive after the war. Some
residents heard five revolver shots, and as there
did not happen to be any special occasion for the
shooting they set out to search of the tumultuous
one. They found a young man on the ground
with a five-chambered revolver, with all its
chambers discharged, lying by his side. The man
rose up and explained that he was a returned
cohentingenter, and had been rejected by his
young lady whose love had faded with his kharki.
He admitted shooting at himself and stated that
his intention was to die for the girl. On examina-
tion there was no trace of any wound. The papers
called it a determined attempt at suicide. Next
day he was charged with attempted suicide, but
the magistrate thought there could have been no
serious intention, and merely fined him a sovereign
for discharging a weapon without sufficient cause,
which is not flattering to the girl. Problem
arises:—lf a cohentingenter misses himself at a
range of six inches five times in succession, how
often would he hit a Boer behind a rock 800 yards
away.

Extract from an unusually vigorous S.M.
Herald leader of recent Saturday :
One would think that with all the patent ad-
vantages which the continuance of speedy and
commodious mail steamships confers on the Com-
monwealth, our obvious duty, if anything-, would
be to encourage them in every possible way. Cer-
tainly we should not go out of our way to impose
harassing restrictions and conditions. Let them
run their business in their ovvn way, employing
whom they please and paying the wages which
they and iheir employees consider adequate. Why
should we interfere by seeking to apply to these
companies the peculiar ideas of legislation which
commend themselves to a section of our lawmakers ?
If the Navigation Bill, which the Prime Minister
promises us next session, should have the effect
of reducing the quality and regularity of the steam
communication between Australia and the rest of
the world, or of making Fremantle the Australian
terminus, we shall have abundant cause to regret
the day when so-called “advanced” legislation
took the place of common sense and minding our
own business.
But surely there are compensations. In
former times it was the British or inter-
state newspaper that was penalised in
N.S.W. If you wanted to post a Courier
or Age or London Chronicle to a par-
tially-dead friend at Parramatta you were
charged half the value of the paper, while the
Herald, by a silly piece of “advanced” and
wholly un-English legislation, went gratis. The
Commonwealth Government has put that right,
and the Herald might give it credit for doing
some good in its day and generation.

A Sydney importers’gathering was holding high revelry

after another the guests had arisen and splashed oratori-
cal gems around in showers. They had toasted the King;
they had requested the Deity to bless our dear, deaf,
painted Queen ; they had drank bumpers to George Reid,
and cheered M'Million with a three times three ; and
apparently they had come to the end of their tether when
one gentleman scrambled up, gripped the table and
spake thusly : “ Shgenel’men, we’ve forgotten ter pro-
pose ther health of the shairman (hie), the shecretery,
to whose energy and geniush we owe the shuccessh of
the resourches of the asshembling together of this great
gathering. I’ve learned a little Latin in me time,
shenelenien, and I now recall one quotation which
fillsh billsh. It ish this: ‘ Honey swoy key mally
prong,’ meanin’, ‘ Honor to whom honor is due ’ ” (hie).
Then he sat down suddenly amid cheers, and the com-
pany struggled into an upright position, and poured its
liquor over the table-cloth.
“L.W.” : Have just returned from a
flying visit to Maoriland. The country is mad on
uniforms. There’s always some craze raging
there. First there was the borrowing fever in the
days of Vogel ; then came the drink madness,
which in time produced the cold-tea fanaticism ;
and to-day the country is delirious on uniforms
and Imperialism generally. You notice it on the
railways most. In the old days a railway
station-master was an individual who dressed
neatly in good, locally-made tweed, and the rest
of the hands went about in quiet, sensible serge
uniforms. The engine-drivers wore a cap with a
numberon it, which betokened their identity when
a stranger came along and found a train and
engine lying at the bottom of a lonely hill with a
dead driver under the fire-box. Nowadays a
station-master is a man with much gold lace and
frill. He wears a frock coat and an array of gilt
buttons, gold braid round the cuffs, and a gilded
ridge about two inches wide down the outside
seam of his pants. If he is a first-class station-
master he has another ridge about two inches
wide round his cap, with a crown of red and gold
in front; a second-ranker has the same sort of
bands, but a shade narrower; while a third-
ranker has only golden cords round the cap, but
all the rest is as massive as the clothes of a field-
marshal. At Dunedin I saw a man with enough
gilt about him to satisfy an emperor, pulling up
weeds in the station garden, and at a distance he
scintillated like a hunk of New Jerusalem. Alas,
Maoriland, my mother !

Bulletin rejoices to see advice, which it
has repeatedly given, at last taken. From a M.L.
paper
There was an uncommon incident during the Supreme
Court sitting at New Plymouth on Friday night. By
9.45 p.m., after the long day’s sitting, the case for the
prosecution in the Moturoa tent fire charge had closed.
Mr. T. S. Weston, sen., submitted that after the fatigue
and anxiety of the day it was physically impossible for
him to proceed and do justice to his client, Ince, who
was charged with murder. Mr. Justice Conolly was
anxious to get on with the business, and, after some dis-
cussion, said he would leave it to the jury. Mr. Weston
again protested, and said if it was decided to go on he
would have to throw up his brief. The jury decided that
the case should go on, and then Mr. Weston positively
refused, to do so himself, and said he must throw up his
brief. The jury thereupon held another consultation,
and finally decided to adjourn. Next day, after hearing
Mr. Weston’s defence, a verdict of not guilty was brought
in.
Trial by candle-light, with a tired Bench, and a
weary bar and a worn perfunctory jury, has been
the cause of many deaths. The man who is tried
late at night is sure to be hanged early in the
morning.

Murray Independent (a religious paper)
asks: —
How far can a man be honest, and yet retain his pulpit
in one of the conventional churches of the day ? Many of
the churches as at present constituted cannot endure pul-
pit honesty. Christ could not occupy the pulpit of any
one of our denominational churches for six months. And
for very good reasons. The fact is the churches -do not
want honesty, and will not have it. They want ortho-
doxy, which as a rule means colorless platitudes.

“Buy a shamrock, sir?”

The Centenary of St. John’s (C. of E.),
Parramatta, was celebrated on Sunday. All that
remains of the old church of Parson Marsden is
the towers. When the church was built Governor
King ordained that it should be named St.
John’s out of compliment to his predecessor,
John Hunter, Governor. John Hunter was a lot
more important in N.S. W. than John the Evan-
gelist those times.
MY SWEETHEART.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A little, chubby face, with a peach for a mouth;
A fickle, naughty “ case,” is this girl from the South {
But she’s mine.
A saucy, cheeky face, but a dream for a mouth;
A racy, flirty face, has the girl from the South;
But she’s mine.
A little angel’s mouth ; a devil’s heart it hides;
A dreamy, god-like mouth ; a devil’s eyes besides;
But she’s mine.
An eerie, fairy kiss when on my lips hers fell—
A devil’s very hiss—but ’twill lead me on to hell,
’Cause she’s mine.

ROA.
When this same shirt was new
That soon will be in ribbons
It bore the name (the man was lame)
Of poor old Hoppy Gibbons ;
The next to come was Brown,
And when he died of shivers
His shirt was wore—three year, or more—
By bandy Sandy Rivers.
Then Wilson (dusky bloke—
We knew him as " the Persian ”)
Says “ That fits me ”: and so, you see,
He got the next reversion.
But Wilson’s reign was short;
His lungs was always ailing ;
His soul took wings, and all his things
Passed on to Peter Grayling.
And when good Peter went
Away to regions fairer,
His shirt, alack 1 did clothe my back.
And I’m the final wearer.

The Law made a painful apology for its
past stupidity at Melbourne last week. It was
the usual trouble of a girl who went astray with
a man. He was charged with abduotion on the
ground that the lady was under the age of 18
She was herself willing enough to be abducted’
but her mother objected, hence the first trial last
December, resulting in conviction. The Full
Court, on appeal, ordered a new trial on the
ground of Chief- Madden’s misdirection as
to the girl s consent At last week’s trial accused’s
lawyer raised the question of the damsel’s
age, and as nobody could prove it the
case suddenly fell through. The infant looked
about 18, but she might have been 21 or 14 for all
the jury knew. Her mother thought her daughter
was born in 85, but wasn’t sure ; she knew the
family had been in Melbourne quite a while, but
oouldn’t say how long ; also she thought the pre-
sent year was 1802 She couldn’t even give the
date of her marriage or birth ; in fact, she was the
solidified essence of vagueness as to dates. The
abducted one’s sister was equally discrepant.: she
said the other was her youngest sister, there being
another between them, but that valuable piece of
information got things no forrarder, so the Law
had to give it best. The question that is left
unsolved is how on earth the man was ever con-
victed of abducting a girl under 18 when there
was not a shred of evidence to show that she
wasn’t over 20. The fact constitutes a very grave
reflection indeed on Justice Madden ; in criminal
cases a judge is supposed to take nothing at all
for granted.
* * * •
It was a sordid tragedy, that of Thomas
John Grimes, who committed suicide at Bruns-
wick (Vic.) the other day because of the rent. He
suddenly remembered that he hadn’t paid it for
two weeks, and smote his wife with a tomahawk
preparatory to finishing himself on the back
verandah. Mrs. Grimes stated afterwards that
she could not get the rent because she had to get
boots. All the family, except the father, were in
employment, but evidently didn’t make enough
for shoe leather and landlord both The daughter
stated that she took her tomahawked mother, who
was covered with blood, out into the street and
appealed for assistance, but most of the passers-by
merely looked at the mother in an uninterested
fashion and went away, saying nothing. A com-
munity must be growing very much accustomed
to tragedies when it can’t even stop to look at the
bloodstains.
IN THE FASHION.
PHOTOGRAPHER: “ Cabinet or panel, madam?”
MADAM: “ A miniature, please; my ’usband
wants to put me on a chain.”
“Vul.”: Re Bulletin par anent the
system by which the German criminal is com-
pelled, by useful work, to support his wife and
family while in gaol. A decent-looking woman
with five carefully-tended children attended Rich-
mond (Vic.) Police Court the other day and
applied for assistance, explaining that her husband
had recently been sentenced to seven years’ penal
servitude for a shocking offence, with the result
that she was left to support herself and seven
children, who, thus far, would have starved but for
the kindness of neighbors. When the magistrate
explained that she would have to part with her
children before the State could assist her and
them, she left the court, tearfully affirming that
she would rather struggle along as best she could.
What a barbarous system is ours which forces on
that woman and her children, all innocent, dire
and undeserved privations, while the father passes
his seven years doing no useful work in prison !
Also, what’s to be said of a State aid system which
forces the child from its mother, to be cared for
by somebody else, before it will assist her !

Something which the W. A. Govt, botanist
says is a very promising collection of tapeworms has
been blocking the water-taps at Perth ; and
pending the definite settlement of the matter,
there is even more swearing-off of temperance
principles than usual. At first it was supposed
chat what was getting tangled in the meters
was a vegetable of some sort, the result of running
luke-warm artesian water into the pipes ; but the
botanist says tapeworms, though he adds for the
consolation and encouragement of the W.C.T.U.
that, so far as he has found them, all the animals
are broken up into short lengths. What length
they were before the breakage he doesn’t say.

“T.D.”: Great doings this week among
the Melb. Salvartny people, and the streets have
been dotted with little squads and processions of
uniformed gutter saints. I counted forty-eight
female “soldiers” form fours and march. Every
girl in the squad was thin and shapeless, and
walked with the stoop that comes of blended
anaemia and semi-imbecility. There was not one
in the whole crowd that a patriot would care to
see become the mother of Australian childien.

“Argo”: Apropos “R.Q.’s” definition
of the note of Australian scenery as “weird
expectancy.” Was once talking to a lady friend
m front of the office of the registrar for B.D. and
M. The R. came out, locked the door, looked at
Wfen *' a f evv steps, then turned back into his
office and sat down. I saw him watching us
through the window. By-and-bye, I proposed a
move on. Looking back, I saw the registrar
bolting down the street to make up for lost time.
Presumably he thought I was proposing to the
i a dy, and wanted to be on hand to get his guinea
in case we decided to get married immediately, or
to register the infant, or something.
Current daily paper headings reveal
something of the unconscious cynical con-
tempt with which royalty is regarded by
civilised people. For a royal person to
marry the woman he loves, and thus sacri-
fice his throne-prospects, is “a royal scan-
dal.’’ Kings and such give scandal to
their kind when they behave decently and
honorably. When a Queen runs away
from her husband with a tertium quid , it
is a “royal romance,” and current com-
ment shows a sort of sympathy with the
woman. The public recognises that royal
t marriages are generally such disgusting
business arrangements between people
whose kindred of blood should keep them
apart, that a Queen is better justified
than the ordinary married woman in kick-
ing over the traces.

By way of a slight measure of relief
why not admit all the unemployed to the
Bar? Many eminent barristers have spoken
severely about the exclusiveness of trades
unions. This would be a chance for the profession
to show that it, at least, had no such sordid taint.
* . • *
A correspondent to the Sydney Freetrade
and cheap labor press on the blasting effects of
the N.S. W. Arbitration Act:—
To-day a baker friend is charging Id. for a roll which,
till this month of March, he sold for 4d. “ Not,” as he
tells me, “because of dearer price of flour. In former
times, before we were harassed and afflicted with legal
quips interfering with business, such change in the price
of flour as experienced would have passed without any
immediate advance in the price of bread. But of late the
expenses of carrying on even a moderate concern are so
increased that bread is fully 2d. per loaf higher than
should be necessary. We are really glad of any oppor-
tunity to try and adjust our income with the vastly in-
creased cost of carrying on our business.”
Accepting this as true—we don’t accept it, by the
way, but regard it as an inartistic and clumsy
fake —what sort of a robber must that baker friend
be who advanced his prices 100 per cent, because
of a very small increase in bread-carters’ wages
decreed by the Arbitration Court 1 The Court
fixed the minimum wage for bread-carters at 455.
a week, and that was practically the only change
it made in the baking trade conditions.
. * . *
Melbourne’s gaud mayor struck a very
pointed snag of reminiscence the other day when
a deputation of unemployed waited upon him.
One of the workless citizens reminded Mayor
Gillott that they were brother officials in a subur-
ban lodge of Oddfellows more than 30 years ago,
and that the one now looking for a job was then a
more important Oddfellow than the future Samuel,
K.C.M.G. Gillott took a little time to remember
his old acquaintance before shaking hands with
him. After which he glanced nervously at the
other unemployed, as though wondering whether
any of them had ever belonged to the same Order
of Unusual Blokes as himself. The true signi-
ficance of the term “ friendly society ” is beauti-
fully revealed, when a beaming capitalist receives
an appeal for assistance from a long-lost Ancient
Druid or Enslaved Bricklayer who “lodged” with
him 30 years ago.
m • • *
At Newcastle (N.S.W.):
A consignment of 57 rabbits was seized by the city
sanitary inspector. AH the carcases were unfit for human
consumption, many of them being putrid. One contained
a “ beautiful” hydatid cyst. On the previous Saturday
the inspector made a similar seizure, with a similar
result.
It isn’t too pleasant to think what is liable to
happen in the hundred towns where the sanitary
inspector is also the town clerk and isn’t really
expected to use his nose, being a town clerk first
and a sanitary inspector a long way afterwards.

“ Oh, rather t Why, on Sundays she never lets

Charters Towers (Queensland) has also a
violent outbreak of typhoid fever. The hospitals
are crowded, while outside cases keep the few
medical men on their feet night and day. The
origin has, just as in Coonamble, been traced to
a soft-drink and milk-shake shop. One medical
man says that in his opinion few of the cases are
true typhoid, but rather a form of malaria.
Another leading northern medico of much tropical
experience states that, although closely resembl-
ing typhoid, they are not such. He had many
cases in this outbreak which he regarded as 44 out-
laws,” inasmuch as the symptoms resemble noth-
ing he had previously met in his professional
career or in medical works. He considered it
44 one of those yet undefined tropical epidemics
which have become so common of late, and have
never been accurately diagnosed.”
* • * *
Fierce language from under rigidly waxed
moustaches is the order of the day among the
volunteers who have sworn to defend New .South
Wales from invading foes. The language is
against the Government. The cause of it is worn-
out breeches. Times are so bad that the usual
supplies of clothing were withheld, and warriors
who had almost got down to their bare pelts com-
plain that they were finally appeased with second-
hand duds of faded colors, and with moths or
humming-birds or something in them. What is
the use in such a rig of trying to captivate a nurse-
girl?
* * * *
Two Adelaide constables have had an un-
pleasant experience of “touting.” The practice
is not common in that virtuous city—-perhaps
through the vast expanse of park lands on every
side making it too difficult for sportsmen to track
down their game. Anyhow, an indignant young
man explained in the city police-court, the other
day, that he was sitting harmlessly on the river
bank with a lady, when various larrikins came up,
struck matches, and “simpered” at him. He
made it quite clear to them that they would be
grievously mauled and battered if they didn’t go
away, and they went. A few minutes later, they
came back, and he battered them furiously. Un-
fortunately it was not the same “they,” but two
policemen, who had been attracted by the first
disturbance, and came up just in time to get hand-
somely bashed about the head. This cost him 70s.
But it must be a moral and well-behaved city
where being “simpered” at by the push is so un-
usual that it arouses a citizen to nearly slay
two large men with his bare hands.
Sydney Restaurant and Caterers’ Em-
ployees’ Union met t’other night for the purpose
of prevailing on the women workers to join. The
men urge that the union is useless for all practical
purposes while the women stand out, for the
reason that the employers discharge the men
wholesale, and fill their places with girls at
sweating rates. As an indication of what depths
of sweating this position of affairs is responsible for
some of the speakers declared that, in Sydney to-
day, females were working in 4 4 disgusting, insani-
tary, and nauseating surroundings” 90 hours per
week for ten shillings—less than three-hdlf-pence
an hour l The trouble seems to be that the
females won’t join the union for the fear that,
male and female wages being fixed at a like rate,
employers will take the men from preference, and
so leave the women without employment at all.

“ N.” discources in vigorous capitals on a
public grievance :
Here is a current form of robbery, which runs in all
kinds of shapes. One species is the Hospital Saturday
contribution by the employees of a great firm. Goldbug
and co. make a race with Giltgore and co., and, of course,
every employee has to put in something like a day’s
screw. Then the firm mops up all the glory. This is
Robbery, unless the man, or the poor girl, really wants
to give. But that case is not so bad as the Testimonial
Robbery. Some clapper-claw of a Koo-tooing Cringer is
seized with the idea of currying favor by getting up a
Testimonial to a Boss. Well, there should be no more
need for me to write another line. A chorus of fifty
thousand voices will arise—“ We’ve been there ' ”
A few days ago at Singleton (N.S.W.) a
woman was charged with horrible cruelty to an
“ adopted ” child two years and three months old.
Evidence showed that the accused brute regularly
tied the unfortunate mite to a tree, bound her
hands behind her; then putting a gag in her
mouth, left her without a hat in the sun for hours
at a time. The medical evidence showed that the
hair had been rubbed off a portion of thechi Id’s head
at the back, where a scab had formed in its place,
s Hair similar to the unfortunate girl’s was found
adherring to the tree to which she was in the
habit of being tied, demonstrating conclusively
that the agony endured had been frequent and
prolonged. After listening to the revolting
evidence, and seeing the condition of the unfortu-
nate child, an intelligent Bench remarked that
there was little doubt that the child had been
cruelly treated, and fined the brutal defendant
£1 and costs I From present indications, the
next Mrs. Makin who transpires will be fined the
staggering amount of £5, and be given twelve
months to find the money. The average police
court functionary seems to really love the worst
forms of cruelty, so anxious is he not to discourage
them more than he can help.

At the turning of the first sod of the
Gladstone to Rockhampton (Q.) railway, t’other
day, Queensland’s Governor took off his frock
coat, spat on his lily-white hands (at least, he
ought to have done so), and, taking up a shovel,
filled a barrow-load in true navvy style. Then,
getting between the handles, he wheeled it about
twenty yards off, where he tipped it up and
returned to his starting point with his barrow.
This spectacle of a Governor spontaneously (as it
seemed) doing something useful so astonished the
crowd that it cheered enthusiastically ; then some-
body upset the whole thing, and showed it to be
premeditated circus, and but an obeying-of-oi'ders
by presenting his perspiring Ex. with a miniature
silver barrow. Of course, the local Governor is
imported only to obey orders, circus and other-
wise, but the responsible fathead really oughtn't
to obtrude the fact so cruelly before a disrespect-
ful crowd.
* * * *
The law squabbles over old Tyson’s money
are still bobbing up and affording employment to
the legal profession. Latest is an application for
leave to appeal to the Privy Council on behalf of
Margaret Tyson, the lady whose claim to a share
of the estate has hitherto failed, because the old
yellowy-browny documents on which her case
rests are insufficient, and the recollections of the
eldest inhabitant are too hazy, in the opinion of
our courts, to be of much value. The old man’s
dollars are flying gaily.

would have no luck till it returns a Labor
member.

THE TRAILING SKIRT DIFFICULTY
OVERCOME.
The Westralian Government is gradually
feeling its feet in connection with the manage-
ment of the new State hotels. A deadly proposal
to pay the manager a commission on the sales has
been flattened out; profits, it has been settled, are
to go, for a while at any rate, in supplying the
premises with the comforts of a club (mostly the
new pubs, will be on those goldfields which iie at
the back of civilisation); and every three months
samples of the liquor on the shelf are to be sent
for analysis, and the results will be published.
THE WINE GATHERER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The vineyard of the skies
She wanders thro' and thro’,
To gather from the clinging grape
A soft dream-giving brew.
And when a fearful gloom
Doth cloak our souls o’ night,
She comes with wine-stained hands to spill
Forth sensuous delight.
THOMESON.
THE STAR SHOWER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I saw a tide of stars
That rippled down the sky.
The planets turned, it seemed,
To watch them flowing by.
They vanished into space,
I saw them disappear,
And found them all again
Within your eyes, my dear.
ELISE ESPINASSE.
