THE VARNISHED BOARD
A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, Marcli SI, 1903.
My Dear Artni.—
In an article on “ Decay of Home Life in
England ” in current Ladies’ Realm, Marie
Corelli sears her sex with a red-hot nib for their
neglect of home, indifference to domestic duties,
and general jostle and rush and love of dining in
public at swagger hotels. The old-world chatelaine
who surrounded herself with choice furniture,
sweet linen, fine books, preserves of her own
making, and who took an interest in the welfare
of her, retainers, is well nigh extinct, the writer
says. Mothers aspire to seem as young as their
daughters, and among the vanishing graces of
Society is the grace of growing old. Men of 60
wed girls of 16, and women of 50 lead boys of 20
to the sacrificial altar —that’s a good phrase for
Mrs. Langtry and Lady Randolph Churchill, and
womenki nd with means and ti me patronise the d yer,
the steamer, the padder, the muscle improver, the
nail polisher, the wrinkle remover, and eye em-
bellisher. “ Our gracious Queen Alexandra,”
remarks the writer, “supports her years with
grace and scarcely diminished beauty,” but she
does not hint that the beauty parlor has anything
to do with the support. Perhaps it is the Queen’s
example that is somewhat responsible for other
women’s efforts to look thirty years younger than
their age. Emily Soldene notes that the Queen’s
hair is “ dernier cri ” in Venetian red.
Miss Corelli also refers to the case of many
leading “ beauties ’ who never scored a success in
their teens, but who are now, in their forties,
trampling triumphantly oil men’s hearts. Men’s
hearts cannot be hard as of yore, when they
wouldn t be trampled upon by anything over 20
years and natural blushes.
“ The decline of the body,” says the authoress,
“ is the natural withering ot the chrysalis which
contains the ever-young and immortal Soul.”
Personally, I think that thb ever-young soul in an
old body is rather a mistake, and I don’t blame
women for trying to even-up tilings and keep the
chrysalis up to date. The 50-year old woman who
marries 25-year-o!d Guy may have a 20-year-old
soul, but nobody gives her any credit for it. On
the other hand, many women (and men) have
chrysalis only, and no soul, so it behoves them to
keep the only thing they have nice and glossy.
The horde of richly-garbed ladies of whom
Corelli writes, who drive up in smart broughams
and motor-cars, and fight and scramble for seats
in a divorce court during the hearing of a fashion-
able suit, and who neglect their husbands and
homes, probably haven’t a soul among them—
catechism and Corelli notwithstanding—and a
good job, too. This belief in the prevalence of
souls goes too far. Look at the hordes of youths
in our city who stand at the intersection of King
and Pitt streets, day in, night out, and expec-
torate as they lean against posts and think of
nothing. Could you imagine a soul amongst
them ?
One wonrfers how Lady Macdonald, the late
H motor’s wife, liked her husband’s frequent pro-
motions. He was a sergeant when he married
her, and she was in Egypt with him when he got
his captaincy. It was generally understood that
Macdonald was unmarried. In the rush of news-
piper articles alxiut him during the last few years
there wjis never a mention of his wife. No
doubt, the poor woman found her position as an
officer’s wife among other officers’ wives, who
were daughters of belted earls, an anomaly—very
much what Dinah Shadd’s would have been had
Sergeant Mulvaney risen as Macdonald did.
Dinah was a lovable, industrious woman, and
nothing but her husband’s “consumin’ thirst”
stood between them and high military rank—and
splendid isolation. You couldn t expect the
Colonel’s wife (who was the Hon. Miss Something)
to bring into her circle the Captain’s wife who, a
few years previous, was ironing her crested frills
at a rupee a score.
A certain drapery firm has had a losing experi-
ence. The shop-walker saw a quietly-dressed
woman coming out with a parcel properly tied up,
but which she seemed to be carrying in a suspicious
way. He challenged her right to it, and demanded
that she produce her receipt. She said it was in
the parcel, and refused to open the same.
He took her to the police-station, her husband
was telephoned for, and the package was opened.
The receipt was there, and now that firm has just
paid £5OO rather than go to court. Another well-
known firm never prosecutes, but it is alleged
that it has a quaint way of dealing with shop-
lifters. It generally compels them to swallow a
box of pills in lieu of being given in charge. But
the other day one of the proprietors caught a
woman hiding a certain article of underclothing
in her umbrella. He promptly compelled her to
put it on over her clothes, and made her stand
there in the shop for an hour or so.
The man who yearns to buy pictures by Aus-
tralian artists, and complains that he cannot
because the canvasses are mammoth, will next
■week have his excuse plucked from him.
Art Society holds an exhibition of small work—no
picture to exceed 18 inches—and the artists are
resorting to the dodge of wide frames to escape the
limit. Examples of the new method of painting
with sticks of solid color will be on view. Most
of the artists have had a try at it and say it is
like working with putty on glass. Sid Long has
finished his portrait of State Premier See, and
Fred Leist has a fine portrait in French pastel of
Miss Murray, a Gore Hill girl—fine brown eyes,
dark brown hair, sensitive face. But these works
will be shown at the Spring exhibition.
The really artistio event of the year up to date
was the unveiling of “ Pliryne before Praxiteles,”
at Sydney Art Gallery last week. The work cost
£B5O, ana weighed two and a half tons. This is
an awfully Philistine statement, but it will inter-
est people of tbe Peter Amos stamp (“Niobe”
farce) who say there is no money in art, far better
than any heroics on the beauty of the nude
hetaira. It is proposed to model more bronze
every five years, until tlie six niches of the Gallery
which ache for art are filled. Perhaps within the
next decade local art will have attained such a
pitch as to allow the modelling to be done in Aus-
tralia.
A local tenor told a certain Italian conductor
that his friends were about to get up a benefit
concert to send himself home for further study
and a Career. The conductor was very unsym-
pathetic : “ It ees yust as bad,” he said, “ as eef
my friends would get up a benefit to send me
home to learn ze box. ”
At Rosehill races on Saturday some of the new
gloves were on the old hands that reached out to
back the horses. Kid to a little above the wrist;
the other half lace or embroidered net with butter-
fly, dragon-fly or flower designs. The other kind,
the all-the-way kid glove, with its three rows of
narrow velvet or chenille threaded through the
top of the kid, the ends fashioned into rosettes or
sprawling loose, displa3 T ed itself under very
transparent sleeves. There was a mild outbreak
of the blue and green mania which fashion writers
promise is going to be acute. One advanced
person displayed under a navy blue dress an
underskirt of regular St. Patrick’s day green, and
on her dark blue hat a green bird.
Music-loving Sydney public has been com-
plaining of the want of good chamber music, and
the moan has found recognition. A quartette
has l>een formed under the name of the ‘ Sydney
Chamber Music Society.” First and second
violins will be bowed by Henri Staell and Mowat-
Carter, respectively. Mr. H. A. Jacques will
operate on the viola, and Herr Gerard Volimar on
the ’cello. Four concerts will be held during
the season, and, amongst others, there will be
rendered works of Mendelssohn, Brahms, Boc-
cherini, Haydn, and other big eagles of the
musical atmosphere. Vocalists and pianists will
be selected with the most rigid care. Mr. John
Laure, of Paling’s, will give you further par-
ticulars.
Sydney stopped yawning for half-a-minute last
week to smile at an item in the telegraphed inter-
view with Edward Lloyd. The great tenor, in-
stead of saying the usual tiling about his combina-
tion of artists—that it was the biggest combine
the world had ever seen —explained that the
artists (the Misses Foxcroft, Jean Newman, and
the basso, Peterkin) were not yet famous, but that
they all hoped to be.
The engaged girl’s sister doesn’t seetn to be such
a blister as the deceased wife s sister, but she was
part cause of a breach of promise case in Sydney
this week. The young barrister who appeared
for the plaintiff girl must be quite used to this
kind of business by now. His office is sodden
with tears, and his blotting paper has lost the
power of absorption through the deluge
of weeping upon it. But in this case he
failed to get more than £2O damages—a sum which
plainly disgusted him. The smallest heart frac-
ture is worth at least £5O. Defendant was a
returned soldier, who now holds a billet as hos-
pital attendant. Plaintiff sent her younger sister
May to say good-bye to the departing warrior
when he was leaving for the front some
years ago, and from that time forward he
saw only May. Every month was May to
him, and from behind the kopjes and on the
veldt and in the midnight watclffires floated the
face of May. Yet still he continued to write
lovingly to his fiancee, the elder sister. On the
margin of a circulating library novel I once saw,
scrawled opposite a tender passage in which a
man is musing on which of two girls he loves
best : “The pangs of love is grate—so grate that
a man can love 2 women as much as he can
love 1, no matter what peeple say, and which-
ever one he marries, he’ll find he loves the other
one best.”
Friends of Mr. Charles Bright, the well-known
lecturer and journalist, who took charge of the
Unitarian Church when Worthington “went up,”
will regret to hear that he lies seriously ill at his
house in Darlingliurst.
The Chilian training ship, General Baquedano,
has gone after a stay of 12 days in Port Jackson.
The officers picked up enough English during that
time to ask their way to the G.P.O. and be mis-
directed. Tbestock phrase among them was: “Are
you ver tired?” They used it in the sense of “How
d’ye do ?” The captain spoke English and French,
but his company was only at home in Spanish, yet
two of the officers were named O’Brien and
O’Reilly respectively. They probably came from
the Chilian province of O’Higgins. At a private
dance, one Spanish-Irish warrior, not being a good
dancer, breathed softly into the shell-like ear of
his partner, “Excuse me, please; my feet are
chump 1 ” Then he went on to explain in French
that his good English was due to the fact that he
had studied the language four years before taking
the trip.
On a day last week an influx of passengers by
three liners broke the record of our biggest hotel’s
booking. Over 100 persons registered within a
few hours. The lady, who for some weeks made
herself a centre of interest to the guests of this
hotel by coming to meals with a live chameleon
wriggling on her bodice, lias gone. He was a
good sized reptile, and he was fastened by a
slender gold chain and safety pin to the corsage,
and the only time when he really annoyed the lady
was when he took a header into her soup, and then
climbed back embroidering the crepe de chine with
a wiggle-waggle pattern of mulligatawny. Per-
haps if this lady visits W. A. she’ll meet the tribe
of blacks who wear a wreath of live lizards (and
nothing else) and come back to exhibit the fashion.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“Senator Sargood’s will, proved at £680,000,
shares everything equally among widow and
children, except that the former has in addition
the income derived from £15,000. The sons get
their shares, absolutely. The widow and daughters
are to draw the incomes of their portions, which
will be invested for them. At death, the
daughters’ money passes on to their children, the
widow’s goes back into the estate. More than
half-a-million of money made out of drapery !
Some people have cause to congratulate them-
selves that Old Man Adam and his wife fell into
the fix which led to the adoption of clothes.
“ The old terms of Melba’s intimacy with the
Governor Clarkes are stated to have undergone a
change. The public hasn’t had much chance of
judging, for the reason that, since Christmas, the
Clarkes have not been about the exact spots where
the diva has whirled. But after many"uncertain
arrangements as to Miss Mignonette’s assistance
at the Grange fete, where Melba was to play the
Person of Most Importance, personal pars in the
daily press confirmed the rumor that Miss Clarke
would take part in the scone or d’oyley stall. She
didn’t. And the thickest-headed at length begin
to see evidence of estrangement or disinclination
to revolve in the same orbit.
“Melba, flanked by the Federal chiefs —civil
and military—coughed and used her cambric
vigorously to afford the mixed gathering at the
Grange fete a reason for her backing out of the
opening speech. Anon she grinned and as good
as winked at chums in the crowd while Barton
did the business. She made no pretence of lis-
tening to Toby’s platitudes, but played with
a gold chain purse, and giggled. But when a
most gaunt shepherd started along discourse on
the Braille Charity, which the fete proposed to
help, she murmured her tiredness and impatience
in an undertone to General Hutton, who had to
look innocent and serious under severe provoca-
tion.
“ Society presided at carrot, cake and pin-
cushion stalls, in its best bib and tucker, at that
Braille Fete, and the suburban flutterers stood
around and took in dress and manner points.
One stall, where the Elect stood for
‘counter’ attractions, had the cream of the
visitors within its tarpaulin walls. There
Janet Lady Clarke, in a swell resurrection garb
of grey silk p’anted thickly with lace plots, was
welcomed back to the whirl. Mostly elderly
dames led these particular stall junkets, but
simple white frocks, artless pinafores, and shady
hats, went bond for their youth. The most
startling of all the kiosks, held by Miss Landale,
Hall Thornley, and one of the Fisken brothers,
ran a race-game with a remarkable amount of
go. Mrs. Thorley and a Landale s ster sat
by the gamble machine, and supplied fair pic-
tures in profile.
“ Second day of the Braille fair, Melba loomed
large in white muslin, inserted with fine silk
Irish crochet and embroidered chiffon. A lovely
gown, but Melba and white muslin are best apart.
The majority of the diva’s bazaar colleagues—
smart matrons, mostly with grown-up children —
affected toilettes of the girly type : white muslin,
green sashes, pink roses, transparent-lace blouses
—the sort of springlike garnishings that on a
sultry, tiring day “turn dog ” on a wearer out of
her teens and .show up her proneness to spread or
sharpen, as the case may be. Cornmandantess
Hutton looked pleasant and dignified in pale-
blue ; Mrs. Edward Miller toned down the sum-
mery tints of hot-weather plumage with a big
black hat, untrimmed, save for a gauzy white
scarf twisted round the crown. There were few
girls on view and fewer belles. Among the pro-
fessionals giving a hand, Eugenie Duggan (Mrs.
W. Anderson) caught most notice, thanks to
flowing lace polonaise over billowy, silk draperies,
trailing sleeve ruffles of chiffon, and rings hiding
most of her fingers to the knuckle. Edward
Lauri, sporting one of his gay little tan vests, with
V of pearl buttons, apparently rather shocked an
old clergyman, who introduced the artists, by bis
song of a widow who showed a good lot of leg
when crossing the street.
“ The woman mterjector is becoming a known
quantity at Federal political gatherings. The
other night, when C. C. Kingston held the floor,
some feminines in the front pews showed a con-
tentious spirit, and hurled remarks now and then
across the reporters’ table at the speaker. Some-
times be caught the remark and dealt with it—
respectfully. Other times it missed aim, and
Madam Malcontent repeated it, as a wise utter-
ance, to her bonneted neighbors, and the lot
rumbled incontinently till the men hushed them
down. The woman heckler is a fact to deal with.
At present she is courteously invited to come and
hearken, and is bowed (the very shabbiest of her)
into a reserved row, and consequently her con-
spicuousness makes her shy. In a little while
she will get over this, and she will yell her com-
ments. Fancy a woman of good nag-power at an
election meeting !
“Asa plain utterer of plain truths, a simple-
fact man, Charles Cameron Kingston is most
attractive. He fails as a ‘ pretty speaker,’ and it
is because he so fails that he revels in a personality
that grips a throng, and holds its ear in leash.
The broken sentence, the jerked word, the short
cut to a meaning, the colloquial term, the phrase
in the rough, the rugged inflections of the voice,
the nervous force that, at times, leaves the arms
quivering at the sides—all combine to show the
very heart of the speaker. No clap-trap, no
balderdash, no pause for polish, no gilt, no grand
period*. A simple, spasmodic, sincere talk—such
is the Kingston platform address.
“ C. C. Kingston is just now a man of'impaired
health, lie is paying for his 18 high
pressure, with its great loads of recrimina-
tion and abuse from all parts of the Common-
wealth, by well nigh a break-down. On Satur-
day, prior to leaving for Brisbane, he had to
submit to a medical test, and be tonicked for the
trip.
“So there was a Lady Macdonald ! Victorians
who feted the warrior when here were loud in
praise of the ‘genial bachelor.’ Some of our
smartest matrons were quite willing to be extra
kind to the mateless hero—but he didn’t permit.
The greatly-eulogised General passed from place
to place rapidly, and was hardly ever seen out of
tweed sac suits and hard boxer hats.

“ The excitement over a Ladies’ Cricket Match,
between a team of University under-gratis and a
set of erudite young Misses, may be imagined
when it is mentioned that at the end of the con-
test, the winners slung their red-haired Captain—
Miss Taylor—on their shoulders, and in that fashion
of risky homage, trotted her over the grounds.
You have noticed the ordinary Eve of to-day sit
down. It is an elaborate ceremony. She draws
her skirt to position, mindful of creases and pins
and nails, and, after a glance at the seat in a pre-
cautionary manner, settles down on it. Now a
female who is suddenly swept from terra-firma
and slung on to the uncertain seat of feminine
shoulders, risks a lot. There’s the possibility of
various punctures, leg display, skirt reefing, and
lots of unpleasant things.
“Little Dunky Gillies is on the mend. He
waxes jovial. The other day he fraternised with
Jimmy Mirams on the Brighton platform, and
told so ticklish a joke that the small man, of the
ever-green gamp and Parliamentary folio and
bank-boom records, grinned as he hadn’t grinned
for years.
“There’s method, as a rule, in Love’s madness.
A large circle of friends mourned the action of a
suburban girl a while ago in dismissing her lover,
almost on the eve of the nuptials, on account of
his agnostic principles. Everyone fretted over
the waste of pillow shams, etc. But they needn’t
have. The dear girl promptly wedded the curate
who had pointed out the iniquity of the agnostic.

“ The present cold season fashions run to mealy
materials. We have the oatmeal liopsack and
flake tweeds aspiring to flavor. Tartans, too,
have arrived. It is quite a braw Scotch season of
clothes. Head mounts are of too-military a
character to ‘ cotton on.’ After the chic and
shady Panama, with its style dependent on its
manner of skewerage fore and aft, the bold, curt
hussar cap and the cake-tin toque attract only the
class of indomitables who get away early in the
season with all its oddities —the odder the better.
Saw a smart, snub-nosed fashionable at the Fete
the other day in a hussar cap of blue felt, with
remarkable skewers of gun-metal. Front
view was Aunt Sallyish, but the profile plum-
puddingish. Why do the button-nosed go for the
round hat and the baker’s toque ?
“ Lady Chermside and her sister, Mvs Webb,
left pleasant and amiable memories in Healesville
after their trip a few weeks ago. The two
Englishwomen have more than the ordinary cubits
to their stature, and took wonderful walks over
the ferny lanes of the honeymoon district. When
Miss Webb was asked how she negotiated the
fences of one quarter, she replied simply and
directly, ‘Stepped over them.’ Now, Melba
while there—but that’s another story.
•‘ Many Victorians and not a few Northerners
lament the untimely death of Mr. HUneas Gunn,
on a station in the Gulf country from malarial
fever. The dead man, before he went to manage
the cattle-station where he ended his days, was
librarian at Prahran (Vic.). Before that again,
he did much exploring and roughing in the Gulf
Country, and still earlier in his career, he edited
a paper in the vicinity of Melbourne. He was an
honorable, kindly, whimsical soul, rich in good
stories and odd experiences, unable to content
himself long in one groove. In town, he felt as if
‘ the wilderness was drawing him,’ in the bush,
he fancied ‘the pavements were calling him.’
When he returned to the wilds, this last time, he
took a young wife with him, a regular brick of a
girl, and seemed more restful in consequence. He
wrote to bis friends that, even when he made his
pile, he doubted whether he should have the heart
to desert the ‘sun-baked plains.’ He used to
relate in pathetic tones the story of his missionary
friend, ‘John Sweetman,’ who was eaten by a
cannibal. The lecturer caught the cannibal, so
he said, and tattooed on him a funeral inscription
to ‘John Sweetman,’ ending with the letters,
* R.I.P.’
Yours affectionately,
AKENEHI.

Reeking a seat in the Senate.
Like the N.S.W. Government, parson
Tremayne Dunstan never paid interest out of
loan moneys. He always did it with his own
cheques.
Mrs. Ellen Close died last week in Riley-
st., Sydney, aged 90 years. She had lived on
Surrey Hills since the place was known as “the
hill outside the town.”
Died lately, at Port Elliot, James Long,
about the last of the ancient whalers of S. Aus.,
who made their headquarters on Kangaroo Island
and the neighboring mainland.
The N.S.W. portion of Daniel Cooper’s
estate comes to not much over half-a-million. He
was first Speaker under Responsible Government
in N.S.W. Of the eight Speakers since his
retirement, about one-half left large estates.
Lowthef Clarke, of Melbourne, is one
more bishop who promises to stay in his new
corner of the vineyard for life. They mostly say
things like that, but then later on they get a
“call ” and reluctantly tear themselves away.
Late Justice Moorhead (Westralia) pans
out financially with £7OO over debts. Admitted
to W.A. bar in 1891 he rushed into a large prac-
tice, became K.C., politician, M.L.A., Attorney-
General and Judge. Then finis, with £7OO to
credit.
William John Taylor, solicitor, who “ went
over ” recently, at the age of 50 years, will always
be remembered at Coonamble (N.S.W.) as the
man who demanded that the Court should provide
30 roosters with which to swear a batch of Chinese
witnesses.
A reminder of Jules Joubert’s poor little
attempt to run a Melb. Exhibition cropped up in
the local insolvency court the other week. Calixte
Denat, chef, of Little Collins-street, filed his
schedule. He was a partner in the Exhibition
fiasco, which included a scheme for attracting the
public with the offer of a French dinner for ss.

Concerning a very old man who has
dropped out of politics :
After 30 years of membership
Thomas Reibey retires from the
Tasmanian Assembly. He was
born in 1821. The son of Thomas
Reibey (whose real name was
Raby) and “ Richenda, his wife."
Thomas Reibey was for many
years a parson of the Anglican
frill and persuasion, and climbed
to be Archdeacon of Launceston.
In 1871, when he was already 50
years old, he retired from the
church, and later he decided
to give the tail end of his life
to politics The rather dreary
and ineffefttual old man repre-
sented Westbury for 29 years.
He was Premier for 12 months
in 1876-77 ; Colonial
Secretary in Dr. Crow-
ther’s Cabinet (1878-
79); Speaker of the
Assembly, 1887-91, and
held an honorary port-
folio under Braddon.
Thomas Reibey’s father
was the eldest son of
Mrs. Mary Reibey, said
to be the veritable
Margaret Catchpole,
which she was not,
and was born on a
farm on the Hawkes-
bury about 1796.
Father Reibey was a
sailor and traded in
early days among the
Islands, and on the
Maoriland coast —in
1817 he sailed his
mother’s brig, the
John Bull, and after-
wards the Governor
Macquarie. iHe
brought the old
Norfolk Island
people to Tasmania,
being employed for

N. S. Wales Govern-
ment.

Lionel Fegan, N.S.W.’s new Honorary
Minister, is an Englishman, just twenty years
Australianised. He worked at coal-mining in
Greta and Newcastle, and took to Sunday
preaching, total abstinence and Labor politics
with zest. The Labor party elected him for
Wickham, and since that date he has travelled
through the House with a shake hands for almost
every political principle.
Mr. F. Wegg-Horne, General Secretary of
the People’s Deformed League of N.S.W., is not
the first of his name who has dabbled in Australian
politics. In the first O’Shannassy Ministry (1857)
George Samuel Wegg-Horne was Commissioner
of Crown Lands and Survey. In the second
O’Shannassy Ministry (1858-9) he was Coup-
missioner for Public Works. He was a failure in
politics and became obscure.
Bill Lyne will be 59 on Sunday, April 5-
Much as the Sydney papers slang him, Lyne is
almost the most popular member of the Federal
Parliament. He is even popular among his col-
leagues, who are particularly taken by the happy-
go-lucky way in which he spends money. ‘ ‘ Oh,
put it down to the Government” is the response
of Home Secretary Bill to every legislator who
wants to visit anything or anywhere.
The defunct “Mint Sauce” Jones, when
he was tailoring at the top of Bourke-st., Mel-
bourne, long before the mint sauce episode, was
an ardent Freetrader, and was secretary to the
Banquet Committee which “ate’’Edward Lang-
ton, the Freetrade Brunswick-st. butcher, when
first defeated for a seat in the Assembly. After-
wards Jones became a Protectionist. Jones was
the sort of man who was liable to become almost
anything.
The just-deceased “Mint Sauce” Jones
was the politician who faced a hostile meeting in
his constituency once and commenced his address
in a tremendous voice which made itself heard
above all the din of battle: “ Gentlemen of
Ballarat and savages of Bungaree.” The Ballarat
part of the audience was so surprised and pleased
at hearing that it was a gentleman that it sup-
pressed the smaller Bungaree faction, and Jones
got a hearing.

William Henry Thornton, an old Sydney
identity of the military era, died last week at
Woolwich, Lane Cove River ; aged 86 years. He
was in command of the 11th Regiment when the
corps occupied the old barracks in George-street,
Sydney, and first commandant at the new Vic-
toria Barracks, Paddington.
Hector Macdonald was, perhaps, theluckiest
officer in the British army, barring only Lord
Roberts. He gave himself heaven only knows
how many chances to get killed before and after
his first visit to S’Africa, when the Boers had him
alone at their mercy on Majuba Hill, and forbore
from shooting him. Of his 20 immediate comrades
at Majuba 12 were killed outright and 8 wounded.
Macdonald’s was a charmed life or he never could
have lived to become a major-general, with a
handle to his name. And when his luck, not less
than his pluck, had carried him to proud distinc-
tion circumstances compelled Hector Macdonald
to put a bullet through his brain.
“ M.” writes •
Par. re George Fisher, Maoriland M.H.R. (B. 28/2/’O3) is
quite correct, but there has been a sad happening
since then. A few weeks ago his curse (the curse
of many a better man) led to his appearance before
the magistrate’s court at Wellington on a charge
of being an habitual drunkard. The prosecution
was merely to have him looked after, and as a result
he was sent for 12 months to the Inebriates’ Home at
Waitati. Writer saw him as he passed through
Christchurch. There was no trace remaining of
the old joviality that made him the darling of the
crowd and that enabled him to be returned on the
strength of one vinous burlesque of a political
address. Yet he has always upset prognostica-
tions of his being a spent volcano, and his
sojourn in the House of Sadness may set the
broken man up again. The late J. Evison once
described him thus : —“George Fisher, Esq , M.H.R.,
with a full-blown speech in his head, and a look of
deathly determination to fire it oft in his eye, is a really
sublime spectacle. When that gentleman can be detected
with a flower in his buttonhole, and the disjecta membra
of an erstwhile curly head of hair as unscattered as
circumstances will permit, and when the roof of his
revered head resembles not the white but the red bil-
liard ball, then it may be known that he is going to
electrify himself, the House and the country with a
40-horse power oration.”
Lately deceased Paul Castella, wine-
grower, was a blue-blooded species of vigneron
who frequently entertained Governors and such,
in his lovely home beyond Lilydale (Vic.). Once
when something in the way of a strike had
occurred in his household, and every domestic had
taken herself off, intimation came at very short
notice that vice-regality was bringing some dis-
tinguished Britishers to St. Hubert’s. Two of
Cas-tella’s pretty daughters cooked the lunch that
day, and a third, disguised in cap and apron,
posed as parlor-maid. Months after, at a Govern-
ment House ball, an aide expressed to the third
sister his regret that she had not been at home on
the occasion of the Governor’s visit. “But I was
at home,” said the lady ; “it was I who smacked
your face when you tried to kiss me behind the
hall door ! ”
The proudest class of men in Sydney when
General Hector Macdonald visited Australia
were the martial wielders of the yard stick. Most
drapers are military volunteers, and because the
General, in early life, was alleged to have been
for a short time some kind of a draper, they clove
to him and claimed homage through him. A
movement was started in York-street to present
the visitor with a special souvenir from Sydney’s
counter-jumpers, but it stopped short under the
raillery of Major-General French.
“ Harum ” : The late Mrs. E. H. Dicken-
son, whose death took place in S’Africa in
February last, was well connected in English
aristocratic circles. It is said she made applica-
tion for a position as a British spy, her services
being, however, refused. Have a memory of her-
self and the late Dr. Dickenson, when he was an
insurance doctor in the middle north of S.A.
Mrs. Dickenson studied anatomy, and the land-
lord of the hotel where I was staying would often
say to me, “Mrs. D. is a very dirty woman—a
very dirty woman. She has just been dissecting a
lizard on the parlor-table”; or “she’s just been
skinning a snake in the passage.” She was always
doing something of the sort.
A Melbourne pressman sends this :
Moulton, the Melb. Protestant Alliance secretary, who
is not likely to be extradited to answer the charge of
embezzling £428 from the society, had ambitions in other
directions than the stage and lecture platform. Shortly
before he left the dust of Melbourne behind him, Moulton
invited a number of pressmen to talk over a project for
establishing a new and up-to-date weekly journal in Mel-
bourne. In an eloquent little speech he touched on the
evident need for a high-class periodical which, although
sold at a price which would bring it within the means of
the masses (“ the democratic penny, gentlemen ”), should
yet appeal to the “ better class people in our community,”
Moulton urged his circle of prospective contributors
to assist him in his attempt to “ found a journal
which shall reflect lustre on both its editor and
staff” —one which, Moulton explained, would combine the
best features of the English weeklies with the brightness
and daring vigor of the American press. He wanted the
advice of his hearers with regard to the choice of a title
which would indicate in one short, expressive word the
characteristics of the journalism he had in view. Just at
this juncture a veteran pressman, who scented a fat
addition to his income as the result of getting a wealthy
young novice into the newspaper game, ventured to ask
Mr. Moulton the amount of capital he was prepared to
sink in the speculation. “ Well, gentlemen,” was the
reply, with deliberate impressiveness, “I am prepared to
put £6O into the scheme.” It was a very disgusted little
gang of freelances that collectively seized its hat and dis-
appeared down the stairs. “ This is the damnedest prac-
tical joke that was ever played on me,” snorted the
veteran. But Moulton, to do him justice, had no idea
that it took more than a “ pony ” or two to finance a
paper that would uplift humanity and point the way to
better things.

Aboriginalities.
To Contributors. —ln the case of ethnological and
natural history items, etc., always give date and
place of experience ;or as nearly as possible. Thus
many interesting observations will have scientific
value.
Melb. Age tells of a farmer, Kyabram way,
who has sold his hay for £5 a ton, whose wheat
crop averaged four bags, and whose grazing pro-
fits have been glittering. The land is “watered
by. the Rodney trust ” ; and the case is “ a striking
evidence of how irrigation pays.” Possibly irri-
gation does pay ; but the Rodney trust doesn’t—at
least, it hasn tup to now. Doubtless, with this
affluence staring them in the face, the Kyabram
reformers will see that it does.

A Sydney street-episode :
A Chinaman’s hat—a light straw thing with a blue
ribbon—blew off a Sydney tram. John smiled blandly
and awaited the issue. The tram had to go a good way
to the next stopping-place, but the conductor indicated
to John that the hat was all right and was coming duly
along. When the train stopped, an out-of-breath
aboriginal panted up and exultantly waved the hat.
John held out a hand, his smile broader than ever, but
the blackfellow’s" mouth shut like a rat-trap when he
found it was a Chow’s hat he had been running with
Ke swore 47 swears, spat on the hat, threw it on the
ground, and danced on it. Then he kicked the battered
article right into the Chinaman’s arms. The Chinaman
grabbed his property and still smiled.
The oncoming rabbit has scared out the
owners of Mandra Bellae, the best known station
between Eucla and the Westralian goldfields,
after they had held it for 30 years. The pest
has up to now done them little actual harm ; bub
they remembered that on the 8. Aus. side of the
border, holdings which, a few years ago, fed 120,000
sheep are now starving 20,000, and they quitted
while there was time. In the country the damage
isn’t all done by the rabbits ; the dingoes which
follow them are almost as great a scourge.
* * * «
“ Chass ” : Some of the Yic. agricultural
societies are urging the Agriculture Dept, to
advocate a law whereby the mixing of straw with
hay, and selling the chaff as the pure article,
would be punishable. This adulteration has so
long been practised without any fuss being made
that cookies and chaff-cutting firms have ceased
to regard it as a swindle. In fact it has been re-
duced to a science, and the latest chaff-cutting
plants have even an apparatus to do the mixing
swindle.
* * * •
In the land of Seddon they are painting
out the sweet-sounding Maori names and replacing
them with the cognomens of Imperial Ministers.
Not long since some euphonious Maori name was
replaced by Salisbury, and now the pretty little
Canterbury town of Opawa is to be re-christened
“Chamberlain.’’ If this sort of thing continues
it will result in a queer mess. Britain is changing,
like the Roman Empire after the establishment of
Byzantium as the capital, when the men who ran
the empire ceased to be Romans; and if they go
on calling the Maoriland towns after Imperial
Secretaries of State the time will come when the
land will be filled with such horrors as Cohen-
opolis, Beitville, Jurgenstein, Wernertown and
Port Ikey.
'•* * *
Description of a Westralian farm, in the
old, settled district of Busselton, by an Eastern
visitor:
The buildings and fences were in a shamefully dilapi-
dated condition ; although there were six cows with
calves at foot, they were never milked except occasionally
when a little milk was required bv the family ; no thought
of making butter ever entered the head of the farmer ;
kangaroo flesh was the only meat served up at meal times,
and the five strapping boys and girls of the family were
nothing less than a lazy, poorly-educated and badly-
clothed crew of good-for-nothings. And this sort of
thing had been going on at this place for 28 years.
“E.” writes :
Once took a holiday on my uncle’s station*
Don’t know if uncle is a typical Australian-
The property was left him by his father, and,
when the old man died, the place boasted 50
well-bred horses, and there were fully 20 acres of
land under cultivation. The property has now
.gone to rack and ruin ; all the horses are sold, and
grass is now growing where the land was once culti-
vated. Snakes are in the house, and occasionally drop
from the ceiling into the bedrooms, hut uncle is too
tired to shift camp. The first three weeks of my trip
I wasn’t allowed to have a wash—we had to cart the
water fully a mile—but, on the fourth week, I braved
uncle’s anger, and had a bath in the waterhole. On
arriving home, cleanliness was looked upon as
such a novel thing that I was closely questioned
as to where I got the water to have a wash with,
and, on uncle finding out where I had bathed, he
gave me the best hiding I ever had in my life. I
became so uncivilised and dull in three months—l
never chanced a wash again—that when my father
came to see me I thought he was a stranger and
ran away into the bush. Dad suggested I should
come home with him, but I didn’t like the idea of it,
as I was beginning to abhor civilisation ; however, he
dragged me into the buggy. The last I heard of
uncle he owned a sawmill and had several men hew-
ing timber, and was just as tired as ever.
» * * »
N.S.W. Education Minister Perry, fol-
lowing a United States and Victorian prece-
dent, is considering the advisability of running
“school coaches ” in certain sparsely-populated
parts of the back-blocks. In those districts
where there is a cluster of seventh or eighth
class schools, no two of which are more than
four miles apart, the Perry idea is to sub-
sidise, say, four farmers living at the four
corners of the district, and have them drive
along and collect the scholars and deposit
them at a central school every morning, and
return them again in the afternoon. The
central school would be a large one, giving a first-
class education, not a mere dog-box with a boy-
teacher, and it is reckoned that the system would
cost less than the present one.

“Yarrum”: A contributor (B. 21/3/’O3)
asks if flying-foxes carry their young about with
them. Certainly they do—in their pouches.
They carry fruit well up under their wings. I
have shot the varmints in nearly every part of the
State thousands about Albion Park, South
Coast; and claim to know as much about ’em as
the next man. Have rarely seen more than two
youngsters in a bag. On one occasion found
three, but it struck me at the time that the old
girl was up to a bit of baby-farming; she looked
disreputable enough for anything. The male
bird does all the fruit carrying.
• * • •
Justice as known in the early days.
Officially recorded that on October 24th, 1798, a
convict was charged “on a violent suspicion of
feloniously and privately stealing from His
Majesty’s stores in Sydney a cake of soap and
secreting same about his person in order to take
away the same.” Soap (produced) was found to
be of the same quality as that belonging to the
stores, but the act of stealing was not established.
All the same the convict received 50 lashes for
being “suspected.” Which, after all, is very like
the law of to-day which gaols a man for “ property
reasonably suspected of being stolen.’’
THAT FOOD ADULTERATION.
"Well, I don't mind my apple-jam bein’ pumpkin,
an’ my orange marmalade bein' melon, but I
draws the line at Bathurst burrs in my
pickles.”
Said that Wycheproof (Vic.) farmers find
the storms which pile sand yards high against
their fences, and distribute it feet deep over their
land, not an unmixed curse. In places where
the sand was but spread a quarter of an inch
thick, it is alleged that the soil kept, in a remark-
able degree, the moisture it had received from
rains 12 months ago. Seeing which, some of the
farmers propose to remove the sandy accumula-
tions about their fenoes and distribute it thinly
over their wheat-lands, whilst others intend to
drill it in as a fertiliser !

“Maccoo”: Will any Bulletin botanist
say whether the so-called ‘ ‘ Cape ” gooseberry (the
cherry-shaped and cherry-sized tomato found
everywhere in the virgin forests of the Tweed
River district, N.S.W.) is indigenous to Aus-
tralia, or is merely the American Solanum
Lycopersicum gone wild? Also, is the “Cape
gooseberry” (common in the N.S.W. Ulawarra
district and in N. Queensland) an Australian
indigene ? And why “ Cape ” ?

“ Jaf ” : A coach traveller in the far west
called for whisky at a wayside pub., and a bottle
of venomous-looking stuff was handed down from
the shelf. The traveller, after peering at it
thoughtfully, decided to try brandy, but a cursory
glance at that was enough. “What sort of rot-
gut is this to offer to a superintendent of police?”
demanded the traveller. “Why the didn’t
you say who you was at first! ” exclaimed Bung
as he dived beneath the counter for a different
bottle.
“ Vril ” : Once struck a railway carriage
full of road-repairers out Bathurst way and
drank m the followingßen had a’close
shave with lightnin’ near Bathurst. Knocked
kickin’, and would ha’ been done-for if t’other
chaps hadn’t covered him with sand, right ud
to his neck.” Thirsting for knowledge I
inquired reason of 90-per cent, burial
“Mister,” said one man with undisguised
pity, “ don’t you know that if you touches a
chap as has been struck, the lightnin’ comes
out of him and knocks you over?” I admitted
ignorance and was advised—“ Never touch ’em
or let your shovel touch ’em. Stand off and
throw the earth on ’em till they’re nearly
covered. It draws out the lightnin’ and saves
em. Look at them coves, near Orange ; they
leaves the boy lyin’ without sandin’ him, and
runs for the doctor. Course the boy was dead
when the doctor come. You see, they give
the lightnin’ time to its work.”

The straight talk of Dr. W. Harvey, de-
scribed as one who has held a number of offices
under the Westralian Govt, in the north-west nf
the State, and now in Adelaide
No notice appears to be taken of the illicit intercourse
between the races, and often the native gins are to be
seen in the townships, going about in male attire in
company with white packers and others. Diseases are
very prevalent. In reference to labor, the indenture
system of contracts between whites and blacks is in
vogue, and, as it is practised, appears nothing less than
legalised slavery I have seen and heard of many cases
of ill-treatment of the natives. In one township a gin ran
away three times from the hotel people who employed
her, and on each occasion she was brought back to her
duty under the lash of a stockman’s whip, while herblack
“ boy,” standing outside the store, was knocked down
without warning and so injured that his shrieks were
heard all over the township ; this merely because the
poor black did not wish the woman to live with the
brutal white. In this case the police were not very will
ing to proceed against the party, as one of their own
number was maintaining a lady of a color which contrasted
strikingly with her turkey-red dress. I also know of a
case in which a woman held the whip to enforce obedi-
ence from her black sister.
The same man, by the way, is authority for the
statement that the pearling industry is recovering
from the temporary shock which the Federal
Government’s White Australian policy gave it.

T. “ Hillier ” : Be recent Bulletin query
about large mouse families. In ’94 I kept a
general store in Berwick, Gippsland, and once, in
removing some bags of sugar that had remained
undisturbed for four months, I found, between
the last two bags, a nest containing sixty-seven
young mice, apparently only a few days old.
Both bags had been eaten away to a depth of 3in.,
and the nest was about Bin. in circumference. I
can hardly believe that these mice were all of one
family, but I am positive about them being all in
one nest, and I could not observe any difference
of size marked enough to suggest different ages.

un - aboriginaiised. Pot - boiling
contests are frequent in the

water, and the wide bush full
of fuel before them. White
box-wood is preferred, though

excessive heat. Gum-wood is
unspeakable. The first pot that
boils over wins. The races are
very close and exciting. An
old, well-worn pot boils quickest.
One min. 40sec. is record time,
but the stoking necessary to
achieve such is like to bring on
nervous prostration. On the
plains, where wood is a curiosity,
fires are made from thistle-stalks
(when they grow). The Govern-
ment has to periodically replace
the mile posts
there, as mis-
creants splinter
them up for pot-
boiling. The
chill of despair
felt, when having
boiled the quart,
you find there is
no tea, or having
put in your last
pinch of tea the
dog knocks it
over, is une-
qualled in the

“ Lind ” : The codlin moth has in the
Myrtleford (Vic.) district completely destroyed
acres of potatoes. Hitherto the pest has confined
its ravages to fruit gardens. Many farmers con-
sider the dry year has favored the moth’s taking to
the ground, and thriving and multiplying under
new conditions. The reckless laying of poison by
farmers and squatters is killing insectivorous
birds by thousands, particularly magpies, and the
wanton slaughter is bringing its own punishment.
The Reign of the Insect is at hand.
A REGRET.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The backblox bard sat down outside
The bush-hotel a “ broker.”
He wiped his brow and sadly sighed
The dry sigh of the soaker.
An Army lassie chancing by,
In search of souls a-straying,
Distinctly heard the poet sigh
And rushed the chance for praying.
“ Dear friend,” she asked in tender tone,
“ Oh why, why this depression ?”
And from beneath her bonnet shone
A heavenly expression.
“ Has true remorse,” she asked, “ at last
Saved you from dark damnation ?
Do you regret your beery past,
And now sigh for Salvation ? ”
The poet sadly shook his head :
“ Ah, no, that’s not the reason
Of my regret,” and then he shed
A boozy tear in season.
“ I sigh,” he cried, and clenched his fist,
“ As I sit here a-thinking,
For, ah ! the many drinks I missed
Before I started drinking.”

“Right-Angle”: Re the finding of the
North point on a cloudy day. In bush country or
on the plains where there are shrubs this is easy.
According to the blacks, the branches pointing
northwards are always the longest and the growth
on that side stronger as a whole. This rule applies
to shrubs as well as large trees, and the difference
of growth is more easily detected on the top
branches. This method of finding the north is
also followed by the Maoris. Writer, when tra-
velling througli the bush with them, used to
wonder why, from time to time, they would stop
and look up to the tops of the kahikitea trees.
Saw a German once in the King country (M.L.)
find the north by means of a pocket-knife, opened
with the blade at right angles to the handle. He
wouldn’t explain the “ howness ” of the thing, but
came out right every time. Perhaps
letin reader can throw a light. The simplest
plan, however, is to carry a compass—cheap by
prefeience, for then, when funds give out, the
bushman won’t be able to “negotiate” it, and
will have the North point always with him.

“ A. 8.” on the “ Has-Been ” :
One of the nuisances that infest this country is the
ancient prodigy. This old person spends most of his
time relating his own marvellous career. When he was
a boy about nine years of age he used to walk eight miles
to school, and milk 40 cows before doing the trip. He
always got up at 4 a.m., and went to bed at nightfall
Later in life he was a champion athlete. He fought ter-
rific battles against overwhelming odds, and blew his
opponents off the face of the earth. If, by any chance
his enemies sprang upon him unawares he could always do
a double-back-airspring and stretch them cold and'flat.
If he disobeyed his parents they flogged him for three
days with a stocking full of blue metal, and fed him for a
fortnight on boiled corn and dry bread. Despite this, at
18 he could do two men’s work, and he loved to do it. As
he grew older he mastered all the sciences, and soon after
shifted a cubic yard of dirt in 3min. without any great
mental effort. He didn’t make the world—not that he
lacked the capacity, but because his father wouldn’t let
him. Nothing, now, is as great as it used to be ; and the
majority of things are complete break-ups, compared to
what they were in his time.

“T.T.”: The “goanna” is one of the
most amusing acquaintances one can have about a
camp, always provided you don’t keep fowls. He
can swallow anything, and bring it up again as
easily if it doesn’t suit him. Once saw one take a
large knuckle bone, with little meat attached, and
climb his residential tree. On attempting to
round the first fork the weight over-balanced him,
and round he swung, wrong way up. Like the
illustrious Scottish spider, he tried again—several
times—but same result. Then he reposed head
downwards below the fork, and thought deeply
for several moments with his bone in his mouth,
after which he rounded the obstruction slowly and
cautiously, travelling backwards, and climbed to
his hollow, triumphant.

“Nym ” : Often spend an afternoon
chasing foxes—combining business with pleasure ;
for Reynard’s scalp means half-a-crown to his
destroyer. Generally my pack consists of a grey-
hound, a mongrel sheep-dog, and two fox terriers
mother and son. This combination is a deadly
one where vermin is concerned. The old fox
terrier bitch tackled a big dog-fox single-handed
t other day. When I arrived 011 the scene she had
her prey by the windpipe and Reynard was
chawing her front paw. Neither made a sound !

“ .T. A.C.” (8., 21/3/’O3) states :
It is reckoned that it will take £200,000 or more to
repair Townsville alone after the recent hurricane.
A hurricane can hardly be reckoned as “a useful
thing to have in a house,” but I know of an
instance where one came as a blessing in disguise to
very same North Queensland. In the summer
ot 1897-8 a cyclone did great damage to Mackay
and neighborhood. But it did more. In con-
junction with an accompanying flood it opened up
a new channel from the bay to the township.
1 his channel had been surveyed and reported on
by the eminent engineer, John Coode, years pre-
viously, but the estimated cost, £300,000, had
prevented its construction. The obliging hurricane
took the contract at a very much lower figure,
and, if I mistake not, the channel has been in use
ever since.

Thokan ’ : A phase of the tuberculosis
question. In Kangaroo Valley (N.S.W.) owing
to spasmodic hygienic activity on the part of the
foorce, much of the local meat sent to the city for
slaughtering has been condemned. The loss falling
largely on the local cookies, some of the latter
have discontinued selling their worn-out and
lumpy cattle-slaughtering them themselves
instead for family consumption. Money’s tight
and the “tubercle” seems afar off.

“ Lul ” : While carrying a goanna by tail
to a dam, to see if it could swim, the tail-up
position disagreed with it, and it sacrificed quite
a natural history collection: Three unfledged
sparrows, two minah’s eggs containing young
birds, one large moth, and two large brown
beetles similar to cockroaches. Have disturbed
goanna,s eating live rabbits, dead horse, hen-eggs
and chickens. Is there anything, bar cathedrals,
that they don’t eat!

Harry Stockdale writes re those crocodiles
recently mentioned in Bulletin as having been
shot on the banks of inland Flinders waterholes :
Very likely these were the “ estuary,” or larger Aus-
tralian crocodiles, generally spoken of as alligators. I
remember, in ’B4, at the telegraph station,
Katherine River, N.T. (south nearly 200 miles
of Port Darwin), Fisher and Lyons’ manager
was swimming a big horse over (behind a
boat), when the animal was seized and carried
away by a huge crocodile. Besides, I have
seen several of the larger kind quite 50
miles above the reach of tidal waters on
the Victoria, midway between Port
Darwin and Cambridge Gulf. I
have also seen them on the fresh
waters back from Port Essing-
ton, and in North Kimberley
district, and have shot them
on small fresh-water creeks
a long way from any tidal
influence. Of course the
croc, usually found in
the upper or fresh-
water reaches of our
Northern rivers is
the smaller variety
(Gavial John-
stonii), which
has slender
teeth and a
much
longer
and
narrow-
er snout
than
the
large
estuary
croco-
dile. The
gavial is
harmless to
man, and rarely
exceeds 6ft. in
length, whilst the
salt - water fellow,
at times, goes over
20ft. Captain Bill Mars-
den, of Darwin, shot one
27ft. long. It was buried
under a high, sandy point,
with the intention of after-
wards digging up the skeleton,
but, unfortunately, a big land-
slip rendered the exact locality
uncertain.

“ M. 8.” re big breezes:—
I had a look along the track of one that
on Ist Jan. last passed through the heavily-
timbered ranges near Kiandra. Neither man
nor beast could have lived in its route as I saw
it. Huge gum and ash trees lay up-
rooted, broken and burst into splinters
(in many cases as if struck by light-
ning), and many of the trees had huge
masses of earth and rock clinging to
their roots. This wind blew from the
west across the ranges, down into deep valleys, where
one would have thought the timber would have
escaped, but, on the contrary, the destruction there was
greatest. The southern edge of the ruin-track runs per-
fectly straight for miles, but on the northern edge the
margin veers in and out to the extent of 20yds. or so.

It isn’t healthy to get converted when you
are under the care of the Yic. penal system. A
prisoner, who described himself on admission as a,
Dry Baptist, or some such irregular religion,
finding himself without the ministrations of a.
spiritual adviser, changed his denomination at
Pentridge to the customary C.E. Thereupon he
got seven days extra “hard ” for “making a false
verbal statement.” Seven days for being con-
verted, in fact.

ANOTHER INSULT.
THE NEW-CHUM: “Oh, I say,
farmah, when's the close season for wabbits
he-ah? ”
THE COOKIE: “ Close season! ” (Then his usual

Photographs on page 16 represent Maud
Beatty, as Boccaccio, and her sister May. Both
are natives of Christchurch (M.L.), and made
their first appearance there with the Shakespearian
actor, George Miln. They afterwards joined that
excellent operatic school, the Pollard Lilliputian
Opera Co.—now\the Pollard Operatic Co. May
Beatty is just 21, being two years younger than
her sister. She joined Pollard’s co. when she was
10, and has the respectable record of G 8 parts in
50 operas during her brief career. She appeared
last year with J. C. Williamson, as Carmenita, in
“ The Runaway Girl,” and then joined Geo. Mus-
grove, making a hit in the low comedy part of
Pt-Fi, in “ The Chinese Honeymoon.” She, how-
ever, regards O-Mimosa-San and Alicia, the Doll
as her best parts. Her sister retired from the
stage on her marriage, but rejoined Pollard again
prior to her departure for England, last April.
1 hough only on a pleasure trip, she was engaged
by Arthur Collins as principal boy for the Drury
Lane annual. Ihe latest English reports indicate
that she has managed to keep her end up in the
city of 10,000 sorrows.

Admiral George Dewey, of the United States navy, recently interviewed, is
reported to have stated that the U-S. nary was the greatest in the world
The German navy was, he de-
clared, an entirely different organ-
isation of human material. Us efficiency
was greatly over-estimated. The men did
not begin to compare in education and
intelligence with the Americans, and
looked to, and depended upon, their
officers for specific instructions upon the
least matters.

A deputation recently waited upon the N.B.W. Govt, to
ask it to reimburse Mr. Wragge for expenses incurred in
connection with the weather station on Mt. Kosciusko.
“Was it necessary,” Mr. Hurley asked, “to have
whisky up there 1 ”
Mr. Wragge said there were only one or two bottles, and
they were medical comforts. If there was £2O for whisky
he had nothing whatever to do with it. He would not
allow such a thing for a single moment. “ But, surely,” he
went on, “ my poor lads who have to live in that fearful
solitude must have a few cigarettes?”

More Union.
There seems to be a general concensus of opinion
among the followers of the Australian Ministry—
both the direct supporters and the Labor Party
adherents —to let the tariff issue rest at the next
Federal elections. The disappointment that this
will be to genuine nationalists can hardly be
over-estimated. The present tariff is not so much
a Protectionist as a compromise one. As originally
framed by Mr. Kingston, it was strongly Protec-
tionist, though not even then a high tariff •
as it finally passed the Legislature it was—partly
owing to the Foreign-trade party, but more on
account of the mistaken attitude of the Labor
party far from being a scientific shield for native
industry. Still the excuse was put forward
with some reason—during the early days of
Federation, whilst so many machinery Bills
pressed for attention, a compromise of some sort
was necessary ; and if the compromise had been
of avail in facilitating business and hastening the
process of settling down to Federal housekeeping,
the excuse would have had due weight. But that
was far from being the case. The foreign-trade
party fought the “ moderate ” tariff with as much
bitterness as if it had been a M'Kinley Act, and
propose, even now that it has been modified into
something not very much better than a revenue
measure, to raise again the fiscal issue. The Pro-
tectionist Government, in the circumstances
would have done better at the outset, and
would do better now, if it were to abandon
the low-lying marshes of compromise and take
up a definite position on the high ground
of principle, and unfurl the banner of High
Protection. But it is impossible to get certain
weak members of the Barton Government to see
that, and Nationalists must perforce support its
tariff attitude--inadequate though it be—in pre-
ference to turning to Mr. Yes-No Reid and his
Freetection system of throwing all the poor little
dogs that can’t swim into the sea and letting the
big and powerful ones stay on land. Provided
that the Barton Government doesn’t commit the
irremediable error of fathering the preference-to-
England idea, it will probably have the support
of Protectionists, in the hope of a better tariff
some day, when the initial work of union has been
completed and the Foreign-trade party in the
Legislature has been lessened. Still, the Govern-
ment must have some progressive planks in the
platform on which it goes to the country ; it can-
not expect any Nationalist enthusiasm over a
timorous policy of letting things be; in some
other respect, if not as regards the tariff, it will
have to announce for national progress.
• • » •
That could be done in no more obvious and
natural manner than by a declaration in favor of
a closer, completer union—not necessarily by any
immediate drastic change in the Constitution, but
by a steady effort towards a further assumption
of the powers already given by the Constitution,
and of a settled policy of extending and liberally
interpreting those powers. Nor would it be well
to shrink from a definite forewarning of the
dangers incurred by the jealous limitation of the
Federal power under the present Federal Consti-
tution. That instrument of government, like the
tariff, was largely a matter of compromise. Like
the tariff, it had to encounter throughout the
bitter opposition of the Foreign - trade party
in New South Wales and of all those in the
other States who look to Downing-street and to
Linendrapers’ Hall, London, for light and
guidance. Therefore, like the tariff, it makes
concessions to the enemies of Australian unity
which, however expedient and necessary at the
time, should be done away with at the first oppor-
tunity as obstacles to the fulfilment of the national
ideal.
* « • •
The framers of the Australian Constitution had
two models on which to pattern their work—the
constitutions of the United States and of Canada.
The first was a union among States which had
been populated by vastly different types of
colonists; by mutually antagonistic Puritans,
Quakers, Cavaliers, and Roman Catholics; by
States which had been accustomed to act, in peace
and war, quite independently of one another ;
which had, in fact, actually, in some cases,
waged war on one another; States which,
forced into some sort of unity by the War
of Independence, had promptly broken asunder
afterwards, and which were only forced into
union again by the fear of outside conquest ;
States which represented such opposing ideas of
government as the aristocratism of Washington
and Hamilton, the demagoguey of Jefferson,
and the sansculottism of a strong pro-French
revolutionary party; The second was a union,
peacefully formed an'd carried out under every
circumstance of deliberation, of a set of provinces
which had for many years amicably co-existed,
and which were vexed by only one problem—that
of the reconciliation of the French and English
sections. Naturally the union of the United States
reflected the internecine jealousy of the States, and
hampered the central power as much as possible.
Just- as naturally the Canadian union gave
generous power to the Federal Government
and reduced the status of the provinces to one of
practical dependence In their working, the two
constitutions have shown the results which might
be expected. The stimulus of Republicanism and
of high Protection has given the United States
a magnificent material prosperity, but in matters
of government its Constitution has not proved a
success. Already its tolerance of State rights
and jealousies has caused one cruel civil war, and
it is to-day the cause of ever-recurring
strife, and of unfair and injurious anomalies. The
State of Maine, having passed prohibition laws,
sought to establish the right to control the
Federal post-office so far as to prevent it carrying
parcels of liquor within the State’s borders; and
the contention (whatever side the right was on)
caused grave trouble. The right of the various
States to control divorce laws within their own
boundaries has caused serious scandals. To
quote a local authority :
Dakota newspapers are filled with advertisements by
lawyers offering to obtain divorces “at cut prices.”
“Success certain; no delay.” “ Absolute secrecy,” etc.
There have been hundreds of divorces in which the
divorcees have not known of the fact of the nullification
of their marriage ties until long after the event. Divorce
judges are usually elected through the influence of the
divorce lawyers, who make certain that none but “ safe ”
men are put on the bench. Any cause suffices for the
obtaining of a decree so long as the applicant from
another State has the necessary £5O or £6O.
It is not healthy for a nation that its Constitu-
tion should shelter such abuses. There are many
other respects in which the Federal system of the
T rated States fails, for lack of proper power being
centred in the Union Government—the difficulty
ot maintaining order in a State, the possibilities
or harassing citizens by successive prosecutions
or suits in different States, the tediousness in
securing finality in legal proceedings, etc. On the
other hand, the Canadian Federation has worked
practically without a hitch, by reason of its logical
acceptance of the fact that there must be a
supreme power and that that power is naturally
the Federal one.
* * * *
t Australian Constitution-makers fol-
lowed the United States rather than the Canadian
pattern. .Timorously deferential to the clamor of
“ he .. foreign” sympathisers in the States, and
roolishly regardful of the dignity of petty parochial
they hesitated at every grant of power
to the I ederation and eagerly accepted every pro-
posal to safeguard so-called “State rights.”
Whereas the Canadian Constitution left most
ot the provinces with single Houses of
Parliament, and all of them with “ Lieu-
tenant-Governors mere subordinate officials
appointed by the Governor-General—the Aus-
tralian Constitution preserved the State Parlia-
ments and State Governors in all their pristine
glory. The Canadian Federal Government can
disallow the Acts of the provincial Parliaments :
that power is not given to the Australian Federal
f'Uthority. The Canadian Federation took over
the debts of the provinces and grants them defi-
nite subsidies and per capita allowances out of its
revenues. The Australian Federation was only
given uncertain powers as regards the consoli-
dation of the State debts, and is constitutionally
bound to distribute its customs and excise revenue
after an arbitrary method. Further—and most
important of all—the powers not secured to the
provinces in Canada by the British North America
Act are, by mere reason of that, vested in the
' ederation. In Australia the Federal Govern-
ment has only the powers secured to it by the
Constitution ; all that remain vest in the States.
Canada gave the Federal Government the benefit
of the doubt every time; in Australia the States
have it every time.
* • • «
The short experience which Australia has had
of the Commonwealth Constitution in working
has shown that in every case where the Canadian
model was departed from, as a concession to State
jealousies, a mistake was made. The reform work
which the Australian Government might under-
take is hampered at every turn by the limitations
of the constitution : financial reform, uniform in-
dustrial legislation, a national as opposed to a
dependent defence policy these are ideals
which are at present difficult of attainment
because of constitutional obstacles. A great
deal could be done by a firm and liberal
interpretation of the Federal Constitution, by
treating with a contemptuous resolute “shoo ”
the small yelpings of the small State Premiers—
the Jenkinses and Philps, and also the Sees and
the Irvines. That should be done in the first
instance; and, at the same time, the way prepared
for such amendments of the Constitution as will
allow of the Australian Government undertaking
more completely its natural duties as the supreme
governing power of the continent. More Protec-
tion and more union should be the keynotes of the
National programme ; and, if the first is postponed
for the present, the second should be impressed
with the greater vehemence. The National party
must not go to the polls, like the Yes-No Reid
party, with office-hunger as its sole claim to con-
sideration.
Extracts from Sydney Telegraph’s present
serial story. This is where a Labor candidate
addresses the people :
There are thousands of you who are willing to work
who are out of employment. Igo to the manufacturer,
and I say to him : “ Why don’t you find employment for
your hands?” “ For two reasons,” he answers. “First,
because I have lost my colonial and some of my home
trade through American competition, and, secondly,
because of the universally depressed condition of every
kindred trade throughout the country, which keeps
people poor and prevents their having money. . . . The
furniture and joinery trade of England is bad. There are
thousands of good hands out of employment. They are
out of work because the manufacturer has few or no
orders. I want the immediate cause for that, and Igo to
the manufacturer. I ask him why he has no orders. lie
tells me because every steamer from America is bringing
huge consignments of ready-made office and general fur-
niture, at such prices or such quality that the English
shopkeers prefer to stock them. Consequently trade is
bad with him, and he cannot find employment for his
men.
The Telegraph’s serial story appears to know
a great deal more than the Telegraph’s leading
article.

“ J.E.D.” on Things We Eat:
A ham and beef establishment opened a few doors awav
from my house on March 3, and to-day (March 12) I make
my report, the result of personal observations. A plate
of pressed tongue, which occupied the central position in
the window on opening night, and which has been daily
moved to the various corners of the window, in a futile
effort to disguise its identity, expired on the fifth day ;
at least, it wasn’t in the window nor on the counter
inside. It had been gradually fading, so I fully expected
it to go suddenly, more so on account of the ham and
beef man putting a funeral wreath of parsley around
it the day before —then I knew it was doomed.
Fourteen pigs’ trotters occupied a position to the
left of the tongue on the first day, but they manoeuvred
all round the window for three days, losing seven of their
number. The remaining- seven must have been ambushed
on the fourth day and cut off suddenly. The piece of
corn beef had its back (the fatty part) turned to the
enemy for the first day, but turned its front on the second
day. It kept up this movement till the sixth day when
it was reduced to a skeleton and was bought by a small
boy for a penny. I could swear to that piece of beef, for
it had a skewer mark up one side which it wore to the
last. I noted several marks on the penny pies for future
reference, as I thought they looked like “ stayers,” but
the ordinary penny pie is like a Chinaman, difficult
to swear to. So I reluctantly leave them alone,
but I have seen the ham-and-beef man put fresh ones in
on top of the old ones (which should, by rights, have
first chance), so it would mostly be a matter of luck in
purchasing. The bolognas and a few other accessories
have a familiar look to me, but I am not certain
as to the actual length of their tenancy. I think
the ham and beef man thought I was watching
him, for his efforts to mislead me with differ-
ent plates, different arrangements of the window,
and bits of green parsley, were worthy of all
praise. A bit of parsley is a great thing in a ham and beef
shop. When the germ thinks it is all up with the pressed
tongue, it crawls on to the parsley wreath, and on the
“ dispersal ” of the tongue the wreath is shifted and it
gets a front seat on the corned beef. A cunning germ
who knows the game has a real good time of it in a
suburban ham and beef shop.
A bold correspondent on “ the virtues of the
red-tape worm ” :
Circumlocution is being criticised (with all the bluster-
ing ineffectiveness to which that newspaper has been
latterly prone) by the Melbourne Age. After all, with
all its delays and annoyances, red-tape is and has been
the most effectual safeguard to all but the subjects of an
autocrat from the plunder, favoritism, and malversation
of their public servants, Ministerial and subordinate.
Certainly, the ideal Minister with ideal strength of will,
intellect, and body, could administer public affairs with-
out regulations, or advising bodies, or officers, in a post-
haste and inexpensive manner. But the ideal
Minister has missed this planet in his incar-
nations. Regulations, dry as they are to read, and
meaningless as many of their restrictions appear at first
glance, are, when carefully analysed, among the most
interesting of all public documents. They are a concen-
trate of the official experience of generations, containing
great constitutional safeguards in disguise, but in full
activity. They are the results of bitterly expensive
events in the past. The geological jurist can show you
in them the marks of different strata of political wisdom,
the shells of old forgeries, perjuries, dummyisms,
nepotisms, abscondings, and frauds, and the basic granite
of as solid an equity as the statemanship plus
experience of the past has been able to give us.
There are Land Act regulations in Australia which,
if dissected and historically illustrated, would simply
startle the public by their story of the ways their lands
were filched from them, ways, however, which cannot be
used any more. There are stamp duties and audit regu-
lations which, if similarly illustrated, would give us a
complete Newgate calendar of official embezzlements and
frauds, which are barely possible again. Such red-tape
(and most tape is of the same colour) is simply profiting
by past experience to protect the public against fraud and
the insidious encroachments of the great and powerful.
By all means, let us get rid of mere work-making
regulations ; but it is assuredly not wise, after the race’s
long experience of the consistent fallibility of those to
whom “discretion” is entrusted, to extend that dis-
cretion as is being done nowadays. The red-tape worm
appears, at first sight, as objectionable and useless as
others of that ilk, but on examination his structure and
functions are by no means useless.
The sectarian campaign in N.S.W. is
bearing its bitter fruit. In one town in the South
two homes have been broken up since the visit of
a clerical incendiary. The husbands were Protes-
tants and the wives Catholics; and they had
rubbed along all right in mutual forbearance of
each other’s ignorance until the row was set on
foot. Then came quarrels, and finally separation.
“ Sanitas ” writes :
We, the tree and enlightened people who flourish under
British rule, are rather fond of poking it at slow-going
countries, but in some respects we could copy the cus-
toms of those ignorant foreigners with much profit to
ourselves. Among even the top-shelf families of Brazil,
a man or woman about to marry is bound to produce a
certificate from one or more physicians that he or she is
perfectly free from all diseases that are transmittable to
offspring, and is thoroughly sound in wind and limb.
The Brazilian belles are also compelled to prepare for
matrimonial duties by wearing clothes that will not
distort their bodies or imperil their health, the result
being that Brazil turns out some of the most shapely and
healthy wives in the world. There is none of the stunted
“ pony ” breed there as there is here, puny tom-tits, hip-
less and flat-chested, all hat, heels and husband-beaters
—not as in the old healthy Hawkesbury times- when the
average woman could show cheeks as ruddy as a rose, a
billowy bust, and calves that would do credit to a cham-
pion oarsman. The prosperity of all countries depends in
a great measure on the health and form of its women,
and we might do worse here than offer annual prizes for
the best made women in the State. This is done in
respect to cattle, pigs and poultry. Surely the well-being
of human kind is of more importance than these ?
This is very good in theory, but the writer of this
dejected footnote has looked at the stunted,
under-sized Brazilian, and can’t see where the
above yarn conies in. The Brazilian is the South
American pony, and looks “stiff” at that.
Indigestion Cured.
PERMANENTLY AND FOR GOOD.
“For a period of over four years I was a
sufferer from indigestion,” says Mr. Elliott
Watkins, of Lady Robinson’s Beach, Rockdale,
Sydney. “The symptoms which troubled me
most being a dizziness in the head, accompanied
by a languid feeling and a most perpetual and
intolerable headache. My stomach was distended
after eating, and violent gnawing pains attacked
me in the chest, breathing being, at times, most
distressing. I was also greatly troubled with
fits of vomiting, when I would be utterly prostrated
and temporarily deprived of sight. Bile Beans
were recommended to me, and the contents of the
first box gave me great relief, and, continuing
with them, for a month, I was completely cured.’’
Bile Beans have now a world wide reputation for
curing biliousness, headache, indigestion, con-
stipation, piles, debility, female weaknesses, ner-
vousness, bad blood, pimples and all skin erup-
tions, bad breath, anaemia, loss of appetite, sleep-
lessness, lumbago, rheumatism, in fact all ailments
that owe their origin to defective bile flow,
assimilation and digestion.
The Melbourne papers duly chronicled
the appointment of Bent’s railway scouts, but
they were painfully silent about the adven-
tures of those gentry and the manner of
their leaving the State’s service. The railway
men quickly got to know them, and duly laid
themselves out to lend them every assistance.
One rode up to the porter’s room at Spencer-st.,
stood his bike against the door and strolled round
to the back to listen. He had, of course, been
seen, and the porters’ remarks were of a strictly
Sunday school character, highly eulogistic of
Bent, who, they agreed, was the greatest states-
man in Australia—but when the scout started to
re-mount his bike the tyre was like a wet rag, the
handle-bars suddenly came unscrewed, and the
chain unaccountably slipped off. Another scout
inspected the repairing sheds at Newport. Every-
body was most civil and obliging, but it was truly
surprising the number of small boys who acci-
dentally spilt oil over the visitor’s new pruts, and
how bolts, nuts, and things uni countably
fell from the roof, where titters were replacing
some steam pipes, until, finally, a pot
of red lead fell with a dull thud just behind the
spy, painting his shoes a beautiful pink. Another
scout, overcome by the heat and burden of the
day, slipped into a cool-storage truck for a rest.
Certain evilly-disposed shunters thoughtfully closed
the door so that he shouldn’t be disturbed ; others
ticketed the truck “ Traralgon,”and hitched it on
to a train of empties, bound for Gippsland. The
confinee seems to have entered several strong pro-
tests on the way up, but they were unheeded, and
it was not till he was bumped up alongside the
platform at Traralgon that the stationmaster
opened the door and dragged him out dirty,
bruised and disgusted. That official promptly
sent for the local constable, and it was only after
much hard protestation and the despatch of
several telegrams that he wasn’t arrested for riding
without a ticket.

POLITICAL

N.S.W. evidently needs a short Act invali-
dating any evidence against the Crown by an ex-
Minister in a case arising from his own executive
acts.
Bully Bent is mad because some Vic.
railway men give “ wage reduction ” as a cause of
their insolvency. “ There has been no reduction,”
yells Mr. Bent. He is, in a way, right. Robbery
ia the correct term.
The Victorian railways have fallen into
such utter chaos that lately the department
couldn’t find trucks to shift its own coal. This at
a time when the Gippsland coal strike and the
failure of the wheat and wool crops have reduced
the demand for trucks to zero.
Mr. Owe’Sullivan, at Temora :
No doubt, with a few years’ development, the land
would be covered with the homes of brave and industri-
ous yeomen.
Doubtless “bones” was the word Owe’Sullivan
used. The fiendish compositor again.
Reid’s statement that Kingston was in his
wrong place as Minister of Customs brought forth
the prompt reply that Reid was in his right place
as the man who was out of office, but was trying
to get into his wrong place as Premier. Reid has
been ever since trying to think out a retort.
