m Sammy’s electorate who have hopes that their
\otes will be sufficient to eject Sammy next
election.
TEN DRINKS.
“ Wot was the accident, Bill!” “ Old gentleman gave me ’arf-a-crown in mistake for a penny.”
The King has increased the numbers *of
Knights and Companions of St. M. and St. G. —not
that the Companions matter much. Only Mayors
and such are likely to be caught by C.M.G. fodder.
Oughtn’t Australian radicalism to be rather shy
of the erection of Courts of Investiture on
this side of the world? We will have the Gov-
ernor-General be-titling people by accolade next,
and after a banquet he might miss the political
flunkey and strike a waiter instead. That would
bq awful!
The cable informs white Australia that it
has been arranged to attach British officers for
duty with the Japanese army, and, later, to simi-
larly attach some little brown Jap officers to the
British forces. A truly beautiful moral lies in
this grafting of the Rose and the Peach Blossom.
Australia may yet have to take care that it doesn’t
find an undesirable immigrant appointed com-
mander of its forces. Also, the unsophisticated
little brown “ officer and gentleman ” will now be
able to learn, at first hand, how to correct his
brother “officer and gentleman ” by making him
doff his pants, and belaboring him on his sitting-
down parts with a waddy.
The most wearying item in the daily papers
nowadays is that perpetual headline “Australia s
Need of Population.” When Australia makes
such a lamentable hash of the job of finding work
for the population it has got, the less said about
the need of more population the better.
It is a bitter commentary on the N.S.W -
Freetrade idea to see in a Sydney provision shop
the notice: —
Fine Canadian Geese and Turkeys.
N.S.W. has had the importing disease so severely
that it has got out of the way of even keeping its
own poultry.
The Reform Bill Conference between
members of the two Victorian Houses made no
approach to a settlement till it decided to meet in
secret. One secret meeting did it. There are
many persuasive things which one man can say to
another when no third man is listening, but which
he can’t say otherwise.
It came out in evidence during the
hearing of the master tailors’ dispute in the
Arbitration Court, that a Sydney employer
worked a coat hand 16 hours a day for a weekly
wage of 155., and that his mate, on a similar
salary, sometimes fainted at his machine. And
this is only one incident selected at random from
a list of sweating horrors.
In a recent murder case the foreman of a
Westralian jury so successfully kicked against its
recommendation to mercy being disregarded that,
at latest, the execution had been delayed for a
week to allow of further inquiries. Yet it ought
not to be necessary for any further inquiries to be
made to at least save the man’s neck, since the
jury’s recommendation can’t possibly mean less
than that.
In a recent letter to the Age, the chair-
man and secretary of the Kyabram Reform
League urged the various league branches to
refrain from making personal remarks about
either House of Parliament. The reformer who
doesn’t make unpleasant remarks might as well
retire from the business. The first beginning of
reform lies in showing that the unreformed thing
is an utter hog.
The petition to upset the Bruce (M.L.)
local option poll (prohibition was carried) on the
grounds of irregularities, was heard some time
ago, but the magistrate reserved his decision. On
a recent Saturday it was announced that he would
give his decision on the following Monday, and,
on the intervening Sunday, prayers were offered
up by all the teetotal parsons “ that the magis-
trate might have Divine guidance in arriving at
his decision.” On Monday he declared the poll
null and void, and left the parsons considering
whether to say that their prayers were of no avail,
or that the Lord guided the magistrate to decide
against them.
A weak point in the Maoriland Arbitration
law is that the contractor has little or no pro-
tection under it; and probably half of the miners,
to name only one class, are contractors—the mine-
owner lets contracts instead of putting men on
wages, expressly to dodge the law. Almost the
last case in the long list lately wiped out at Auck-
land ended in a judgment that “if this class of
workmen is to be brought within the law it must
be by distinct enactment.” That distinct enact-
ment had better hurry.
There should really be some means of
making Parliament responsible for neglectful
work. Take the Early Closing enactment in
New South Wales. Parliament, philanthropically
inclined, fixed an hour for closing shops in the
large centres, which was all right, but no provi-
sion was made for the opening hour. The result
is that an oystermonger, who shut up by law at
midnight and opened again almost immediately,
escaped penalties, the magistrate declaring that
the Act is badly constructed.
Turley .and Givens, two solid and reliable
Queensland Labor men, are mentioned as Labor
senatorial candidates to run with Stewart, the
retiring Labor Senator. At last State election the
property vote and energetic bulcocking of the
roles only kept Givens out at Cairns by 95 votes,
and Turley caine within 112 votes of Minister
Fox ton at Carnarvon. And the Federal rolls
aren’t bulcoeked.
Premier Seddon adds four members to his
Legislative Council; and after every name there
has been a chorus of “Whaffor”? Two of the
four are amiable politicians who have failed ; the
other two have nothing beyond the reputation of
being men who have looked well after the local
drain and the wandering local cow. Certainly a
Council built of this sort of material may not stick
out against reform like an elective body; but
that’s a very negative virtue, and there isn t even
standing room for a negative virtue in politics it
is all required for the positives.
The Auckland (M.L.) furniture-makers
who decided to close their factories and start as
importers’ houses rather than obey the Arbitration
Court’s order to pay journeymen £3
per week may yet find themselves
in the soup. Premier Seddon has
decided to hold a searching investi-
gation into the trade, and if it is
proved that the rate of £3 per week
does not prevent the local article
under-selling the imported clothes-
horse and wardrobe, then the em-
ployers’ union will be prosecuted
for contempt of court and perhaps
for conspiracy. Its case looks
rather bad in the light of the
fact that the Southern employers
are paying the rate awarded, and
apparently not being ruined there-
by. The Auckland employers have
always borne a bad name, and the worst
exposures of sweating in Maoriland have invari-
ably happened in the Northern ex-capital.
Cairns Argus, published in that Queens-
land town where a white man feels proud all over
when a Chinaman nods to him, says that Joe
Chamberlain “stands unchallenged, the foremost
man not only in Great Britain but in the whole
civilised world.” Only in the civilised world, you
see. Even in his wildest moment the Cairns editor
won’t offend his beloved Chinaman by putting
Chamberlain above him.

row with the Upper House, allowed that body to
persuade him to have eight Ministers instead of

a billet for Sachse, M.L.C., who was feeling a

Education portfolio when that calico patriot found
that too much of his time would be taken up
arguing matters out with Kingston.
A vacancy having occurred in the Victorian
House of Rich Landlords an “ election ” has just
been held with the usual results. Only one per-
son was found in the district rich enough to stand,
and he was returned unopposed. Hopes are
entertained that when the scrap of reform that
Premier Irvine has in hand is secured, there will
occasionally be a contest for the Council. We
are a sporting people, sir, and this walk-over
business palls.
Lord Onslow, who was Governor of Maori-
land for three years ever so long ago, now poses in
Britain as an authority on Australasian affairs
generally, and the other day he explained to a
startled House of Coronets that a certain cable
from Queensland meant that the Federal
authorities were shipping Kanakas home to their
islands, where they would be promptly killed,
cooked and eaten by their loving relatives. Did
Onslow ever see a Kanaka ?

breaking out in fresh places. Among the appli-

toria there turned up recently a pathetic and
broken-hearted individual, whose tale of woe
almost broke the Commissioner’s heart, till it
transpired that he owned .£4500 worth of land in
a well-watered part of the State, with only £640
mortgage on it. He came from up Kyabram way,
as usual —ever begging, ever cadging Kyabram.

Dodgers’-Union-cwm-People’s-Reform-League, got

worth election. The Government flouted it, and

little.
Minister Kingston “ rubbing it in ” to the
dishonest importer:
“ Some merchants plead that they make mistakes, that
the clerk was hurried. Well, employ more clerks. Don’t
sweat ’em more. They say, ‘ Oh, the clerk was young.’
Well, pay older men. You have to pay more wages, but
you get better service, and avoid fines. They say it was
written in French. Well, employ Frenchmen to translate
for them. They send up boys in knickerbockers, boys
not up to my shoulder, to see my chief officer. Well,
they should send more experienced persons. Some mer-
chants complained that they were brought before the
police court. It was not in attending the police court
that there was anything wrong—it was in the offence.”
The decision of the Brisbane jury that the
great softgoods importing firm of Robert Reid
(alleged Senator Reid, late Vic. Minister of Edu-
cation) and Co. intentionally defrauded the Cus-
toms revenue with doctored invoices will, per-
haps, be appealed against on all manner of legal
points. But since the unanimous verdict of a
long-considering jury on a multitude of points
there hasn’t been so much talk of appeal.
Anyhow, the episode tends to sink public
opinion of the importing clan a little lower,
and it was so low before that it is likely
soon to touch bottom. The adventures of Leah
Abrahams, of Melbourne, are alone sufficient to
bring importing into disrepute. That lady ap-
pears in a fair way to put up a record as the most-
fined person in Australia.
The great object of the present Victorian
Government is, apparently, to undermine demo-
cratic laws by underhand means. Bent’s Railway
Department now calls for tenders with a minimum
wage clause in the conditions, and then gives the.
work to foreign sweaters because, not being subject
to the minimum wage, they can do it a shade
cheaper. The law that was meant to secure the
Victorian a decent wage is thus distorted into a
means of depriving him of any wage at all.
The Victorian Government presumes pretty
far on the elasticity of the public service. Clerks
of courts have just received an intimation from
the Attorney-General that they are to obtain
copies of the voters’ rolls and tick off thereon the
names of all parties whom they believe to be in
receipt of an income of £l5O a-year and upwards.
One of- these days the public
servant will be strained beyond
the “limit of perfect recovery,”
as the engineers say, and
snap like over - stretched
rubber.
THE CONFIDENCE MAN.
“With sound finance, economy, return of good season*
and more enlightened (!) views on commerce, &c., we may
expect a return of the confidence of our creditors.”—
AROUS.
COHEN (of London) : " As soon as you chuck up
Protection and Labor Politics, Pm ready to
show you the three-card trick again.”
In reply to Brisbane Courier, which
asserted that Bishop Webber’s cathedral-cadging
tour was injuring Queensland’s financial credit, a
Brisbane clergyman, Archdeacon David, replies
husly :
If all the Bishops in Queensland, Anglican and Roman.
Catholic, were to make a simultaneous appeal for help,
their united efforts could not do one tithe of the injury
to public credit accomplished by the issue of Treasury
Bills for the purpose of meeting deficits in current
revenue. English .financiers regard this method of bor-
rowing as illegitimate. Ihe State is in the position of a
man who, when the bank has refused a further advance,,
has to raise money by promissory notes.
The Yankees don’t seem to have any over-
whelming regard for our trade. A recent ship-
ment of fruit from Sydney by the Sierra was held
up by the California State authorities till the
Legislature could pass a Bill authorising its des-
truction at the garbage crematory —and then it
was landed and reduced to ashes with almighty
celerity. The ostensible reason was that a fruit
S3Bt, which this country originally imported from
alifornia, might be imported back into America.
Exports are always liable to strike snags like that.
Better devote ourselves to supplying the local
market.
The Labor candidate at Tamworth holds
that “State borrowing should be gradually re-
duced.” This is consoling in a way, but it would
be pleasing to know how much he is prepared to
reduce it to, and how gradually he proposes to
arrive at that point, whatever it may be. In the
absence of definite information on this matter the
people should gradually vote for him and every
other man of his way of thinking —very gradually.
Asa rule this vague sort of aspiration after better
things means that this generation should make
good resolutions and the next one should do the.
actual swearing off.
If, as has been suggested, the Sydney
Municipal Council can take over the Resumed
Rocks Area in sections from the State and re-
build it, a solution would be found for a serious
difficulty. It is quite evident that the State
Government, unless it cuts down expenditure so
as to have some revenue money available, will not
be able to do anything to carry out its finely-
conceived but miserably-managed resumptions
policy for many years to come ; but the Municipal
Council, with better credit and a better chance of
economical management, might carry a splendid
project to a successful conclusion.
“ M.” writes :
The “ slight unpleasantness” referred to in your parrs
the resignation of Maoriland Police Commissioner Tun-
bridge (B. 28/2/’O3) was slight to the extent of being the
biggest scandal that the force in Maoriland ever knew.
A police sergeant and two constables in the execution of
their duty found it necessary to arrest some gilded youth*
of Nelson. Their fathers “had influence,” which
secured the dismissal of the officers in question.
An inquiry was urged by the Opposition press, and the
folks of Sleepy Hollow kept holding indignation meetings
till they got the investigation the}' wanted. Tunbridge
exonerated the men and recommended their reinstate-
ment, but his decision was calmly overridden. The
affair is going to cause Seddon some trouble when
Parliament meets in July.
While Premier Irvine vaunts the “finality
provisions of the Reform Compromise, he would
do well to consider a specific instance of their
effect. The Victorian Factories Act comes up
next October for re-enactment. The newly-
elected Council, knowing its power, will probably
so amend this as to make it utterly useless. The
matter then proceeds after this fashion : Amend-
ments'rejected. Bill then laid aside by Council.
Assembly dissolved and Bill re-passed. Former
amendments insisted on by House of 35 Moder-
ately Bloated Landlords. Assembly returns re-
inforced. Council, chosen from the select coterie
of large landowners, also returns re-in forced
opinion, and once more rejects the Bill. What
then ? There is no joint sitting, no referendum,
nothing. Where is the finality ?

N.S.W. Acting Chief Justice was surprised, the
other day, by the statement that a public work
costing £100,000 can be carried out without
authority of Parliament. How innocent some
Judges are ! To carry out a work in sections,
each section costing something less than
£20,000 (all works costing over £20,000 must
be submitted to the Works Committee)
has for years been the commonest of
administrative tricks. There is probably not a
roads and bridges member, and there has not been
one for the last ten years, who has not besought
some Minister or other to practise it. “Joey”
Carruthkrs, the alleged financial purist of to-
day, was about the first to do it on a large scale
when he was at the Education office ; and the
vicious system has drifted on until nowadays the
healthy provision of submitting every large
works proposal to the semi-judicial Works Com-
mittee, and through that body to Parliament,
is very nearly an expensive and elaborate
farce. The law has been in this way dodged and
defeated with regard to public buildings, sewer-
age and similar works, telephone tunnels, tram-
wavs, and half a dozen other things. Only a few
weeks ago the start of a work, ultimately to cost
at least £200,000, was found to run into £21,000,
but the difficulty was soon got over—three or
four feet were temporarily knocked off the top of
a proposed wall, and, the estimated cost being
thus squeezed below £20,000, the Minister bowed,
and the importuning M.P. went away happy.

Business, Robbery, etc.
“0.0.”: Maybe I got the impression from
reading the Owe'Sulli van’s speeches, but my idea of a
contractor was a soft-handed gentleman, with a bay-
window on his front elevation, who merely waved his
hand at his sweated employees, and said, “Make this
building arise!” and it rose, and great was the profit
thereof. Since I let a building contract my ideas on the
subject have altered. The first man on the job arrived
about daybreak, and had put in a couple of hours’ work
upon the foundations before the rest of the men arrived.
Two of them rode bikes, and another, whom I took to be
the contractor, arrived in a sulky. They greeted the
first arrival as Bill, and then leisurely peeled off, and
started laying bricks. I noticed that Bill, in addition to
his own work, had to “ chuck a few more bricks here,”
and “another barrow-load o’ concrete there,” for his
mates, while they pulled their pipes. I concluded he was
the slave and derelict of the party. At 6, after all the
others had ridden or driven away with a “S’long Bill,”
that tireless individual was still working, and, when I
inquired why he didn’t leave with the rest, he staggered
me by replying : “In these times a contractor has to
take advantage of all the daylight he can get. I can’t
afford to knock off with my men.” And it was dark when
Bill trudged off to town in the tracks made by his “ sweated
workmen” on wheels. I never met another contractor
like Bill; has anyone else encountered him or his dupli-
cate?
* * • *
At Warrnambool (Vic.) Insolvency Court an
insolvent groom lately applied for a discharge. The debts
amounted to only £l2, and the smallness of the amount
seemed to work the Judge into a fury. “He had never
heard of a man going insolvent for such a sum, and he
would not grant a certificate until the insolvent had paid
20s. in the £. If the practice of going insolvent for small
gums were to be allowed, the courts would be swarming
with these absurd insolvencies.” Of course, a man who
owes £l2 would deserve better of the community if he
paid his debts instead of “finking.” But the effect of
the Judge’s remarks will be to create the impression that
while he will receive with open arms the shark who finks
for a sum right up to the capacity of the court, he
despises the mere amateur linker who cannot beat his
creditors for a bigger amount than £l2. And the
prospective insolvent, more logical than the Judge, will
decide to owe more than £l2.
* • • •
“C.W.R.”: yRe the fiscal atrocities placed to
the discredit of Minister Kingston. Some years ago I
held the exalted position of invoice clerk to a firm of
importers. We generally received an invoice for customs’
purposes; another for ledger entry. They used to
vary considerably, the customs passes being 50 per
cent, below our ledger entry for the same goods.
It was none of my business to inquire into the
anomaly. 1 only learnt the truth, over beer, from the
customs clerk, who was daily passing bogus invoices
which never reached the ordinary clerical staff.
The trick must be now known to Minister Kingston and
his officers. It is simple. The exporter sends an invoice
specially prepared for customs purposes. In it he states
that so many yards of calico are l£d. per yard, and so many
yards of velveteen are lid. per yard. In the genuine
invoice, which is never seen by the customs authorities,
the same goods appear as linen, 2s. per yard ; velvet, ss.
ditto. Take it from me, the biggest swindlers in the
community are those smug, suave tricksters who hold up
their hands in horror-stricken amazement when some
poor wretch is found guilty of purloining a pound of
steak or a loaf of bread. Kingston is doing for composite
Australia what separate Australia neglected to do for
itself. All honor to him.
* * * •
“J.F.D.”: The small tailor who has got an
order from out back and a letter thanking him for the
fit and style of the garments is unscrupulous in dealing
with his correspondence. A Sydney snip displays a
letter in his window wherein a grammarless gent from
Cooney’s.Creek way, after thanking him for the suit, tells
him confidentially that he proposed to his girl the first
day he wore it, and was accepted. And how many of the
fools who write to the “specks before the eyes” man
know that their letters are often exhibited, with full
names and addresses in the specialist’s window. Knew a
man who was running a new strength-giving medicine,
one bottle of which enabled you to lift two tons. Ha
went round wherever a specialist exhibited his correspon-
dence and took all the names and addresses, and then
deluged the “ completely cured” and “ restored to life "
crowd with his advertising pamphlets. He got much
business that way, too. When once a man gets the habit
of being completely cured he keeps on at it.
“ Adelaide ” : Re recent discussion in Bulletin
as to weighing paper with what is wrapped in it, the
English Weights and Measures Act expressly deals with
that practice. A recent mail shows that a tea co. in
Wolverhampton was fined £4, the manager £2, and
the assistant manager £l, for “ fraud, by weighing sugar
in bags of a very heavy nature.” It was a special case,
the magistrate saying that mineral matter was worked
into the paper solely to increase the weight.
* • • *
A Bulletin contributor gives a personal ex-
perience of the mone3 r -lender. “Pressed for money I
interviewed a financier and negotiated a loan of £5 —
security a sale note transferring about £l5O worth of
household furniture to lender. This note was designated
“ a mere matter of form.” Interest was fixed at £1 for
30 days. At end of month I renewed the loan and paid
the interest £l. Meantime I was sued for an
old-standing disputed claim, and verdict for £l4
15s. was recorded against me. After interval of
24 hours an unsavory bailiff took possession of
my front parlor. In order to remove him I was
compelled to borrow £l4 15s. on a bill of sale, from
money-lender No. 2; interest £4 ; debt to be repaid at
the rate of 10s. per week. On registration of bill-of-sale,
financier No. 1 pounced down upon me and threatened
gaol for mortgaging his property Gave me 24 hours to
pay £5 (original amount borrowed), £1 interest, and £1
10s. for disturbing his peace of mind—or some-
thing of that sort. Situation looking desperate,
I compromised, and gave No. 1 £1 in cash
and the actual possession of about £2O worth
of books, a £23 bicycle, and a marble timepiece-
same to be redeemed within a month on payment of £8
10s ; if not redeemed, to be sold, and proeeeds appro-
priated by lender. When 1 called at the end of the
month, I was told that the property had been disposed
of for £4, leaving a balance of £4 10s. to be paid I 1 was
asked what / intended to do. Pent-up wrath supplied
the answer, and physical violence raised two lumps on
No. l’s countenance. I have not heard from him since,
although I expect to receive a doctor’s bill one of these
days, with compound interest added, and the sum of £4
10s. interest “ brought forward.” The matter there-
fore stands thus: I borrowed £5, and within four
months paid £2 in cash and sacrificed fully £5O worth of
property, and I still owe a balance of £4 10s. ! At pre-
sent I am wrestling with No. 2, who lent the £l4 15s. He
seems likely to go on for ever.

“ Petrainos ”: Know an Adelaide chap who,
though only 19, is manager of a department in a big soft-
goods warehouse. Firm is short of hands and he does
considerable overtime. Been with the firm four years
and gets 15s. a week and an occasional tea-ticket. Also,
instancing the benefits of education, am acquainted with
a University fellow holding a responsible position in
an office who ekes out 10s. per week. Could name
quite half-a-dozen fellows over 21 who earn from 10s. to
20s. weekly in one of the biggest businesses of its kind in
Australasia.

“ E.”: Buying and selling businesses is not a bad
game if you have some luck. Know a man who has started
seven shops in the last nine months and sold them all as
going businesses, getting a reasonable sum for goodwill.
He deals in businesses for sale, and starts shops as a side
line.

“Eleda”: During height of land boom (Vic.)
was travelling by rail N.S.W.-wards, when a canny old
Scot confidingly told me that he had just sold his sheep
run at Donnybrook to a Melbourne syndicate, which pro-
posed laying out a township there, as it was then ex-
pected that D. would shortly become an important rail-
way junction. The terms were a small cash deposit, and
the balance by bills over several years. Being about the
only boom sceptic I ever heard of, I asked the ancient if
there was any doubt as to his obtaining the balance in the
event of the scheme proving a failure. “Na, na, mon,”
he chuckled, confidently ; “why the chief mon on the
syndicate’s B. J. Fink.”

BUCHANAN WHISKY.
In these times of drought and financial depres-
sion it is like an oasis in the wilderness to come across an
Australian manufacturing firm that is not only holding
its own but extending its business by leaps and bounds.
This happy position is the envied one of the Dunlop Tyre
Co., who, despite working double shifts at their big Mel-
bourne mills, are still unable to cope with the demand for
their goods. During the past few months the output of
Dunlop cycle and vehicle tyres and general rubber goods
has increased 40 per cent.—a result that speaks volumes
for the material and workmanship embodied in the
Company’s goods.*
* m * m

has increased 40 per cent.—a result that speaks volumes
for the material and workmanship embodied in the com-
pany’s goods.*
A Sceptic Convinced.
Doubt Turned to Faith.
The sceptical unbeliever is a man often to be
shunned. Without faith he goes through the
world endeavouring to combat truths which he
cairtiot understand, and often throwing cold
water upon others’ firm convictions. Mr. J. H.
King, of 11, Jersey Terrace, Sydney, was once
a sceptic. He doubted that years of agony and
pain could be turned into joy and pleasure.
To-day he is a firm believer in the truth of those
things he once so persistently doubted. Read
what he writes:—“On the recommendation of
a friend, I purchased a bottle of Dr. Morse’s
Indian Root Pills. I put little or no faith in
them, but in a few days I was surprised and
pleased to find that my digestion was decidedly
improved. For me this was something to be
more than thankful for. Many years I had
suffered from chronic dyspepsia. Years of pain
had undoubtedly dulled my senses to the fact
that a cure for me somewhere did exist, but
year in year out I suffered from this dire
complaint, and try what doctors or medicine I
could, no change for the better resulted. Purely
in the spirit of curiosity, at last I took Dr.
Morse’s Indian Hoot Pills. They did in a few
days what years of other medicines and doctors
had failed to do. lam now entirely free from
any trace of my old complaint. My appetite is
good, and I can digest with ease and comfort all
kinds of food, and it is with feelings of the
deepest gratitude that I thank Dr. Morse’s
Indian Root Pills for my complete and absolute
recovery to perfect health.” Dr. Morse’s Indian
Root Pills are a perfect blood purifier, and a
positive cure for biliousness, indigestion, con-
stipation, headaches, sallow complexion, liver
and kidney troubles, piles, pimples and blotches,
and for female ailments. Put up in amber
bottles and the full name blown thereon.*

SPORTING NOTIONS.
A. J.C. has at last shifted from that ridiculous
position taken up with regard to places where members
may bet. Hitherto, registered Noses were debarred
from betting at coursing, trotting and other meetings
taking place on vetoed grounds, but the absurdity of this
attitude was driven home by deputations armed with
invincible arguments, and now the raucous-voiced may
roar their luugs out anywhere and at any thing—bar pony
meetings.
• • • •
Acrasia (8.9) and Street Arab (6.13) had their
third tussle in Rosehill Handicap, Saturday. Prior to
former visiting Melbourne for Australian Cup, Street
Arab led her in Warwick Farm Handicap, but she battled
up in last few strides and won. When Acrasia came back
•he met Street Arab again at Rosehill, and he made the
running to the distance, but she beat him easily after-
wards. Latest occasion saw the tables turned. Race
was between them near the close. S.A. rounded into the
straight with a telling lead, and Acrasia went after him,
but there was a much bigger difference in the weights
this time, and the Jeweller horse got the money by
lengths.
• ■ • • •
Saturday week, in Rawson Stakes at Rosehill,
punters laid odds on Great Scot winning, but Cyanide
proved too good. Saturday last Cyanide was absent, and
Abundance went out at 6 to 4, while 5 to 2 could be had
about Great Scot, who, with Barden up, went to the front
from flag fall and held a good lead to the finish. Abun-
dance was last into the straight, and then, after swinging
right out, made a brilliant effort from the half distance—
too late ; he just beat Purser out of second place. Rose
Petal also shaped well.
• * • •
To punters who fancy The Idler for Doncaster
Handicap this scribe’s earnest advice is—wait until the
day and see how the gelding looks. If The Idler looks
rather fat and lolly, and the money is on, he’s a fair
investment. If he looks “ trained to the hour” the race
will probably go to a less uncertain beast. The stable
reckoned that the son of Far Niente could win last Caul-
field Cup when he was apparently in good condition, but
he either couldn’t or wouldn’t run up to his best public
“form,” not to mention private trials. Three days
previously he had made a bold show in the Coongy
Handicap, finishing fourth, and between the two races
The Idler must have got out of sorts.
• • • •
ProbabilifV is that nine left in Tsoming A. J.C.
St. Leger after first forfeit Monday week will be reduced
to Great Scot, Abundance, Chantress, and Balfour, and
at that the race is sure to prove inte esting. Though
Abundance has been doing well on the track, his showing
at Rosehill Saturday wasn’t too taking, while Great Scot’s
success in same race brought back troops of friends who
had deserted him through his downfall on same course a
week before. But for doubt as to whether Chantress can
get the distance, she would be given a good “ look in,”
though fact of her being among Cumberland Stakes and
A. J.C. Plate entrants would seem to indicate that the
“ stable ” is satisfied on that point. Manner of Chantress’
vanquishment of Wakeful over a mile suggests that if first
part of the Leger isn’t over fast she’ll probably be close
up at the end, but ’tis odds on that Great Scot will make
the pace a cracker all the journey, in which event
Chantress must be an out-and-out stayer to live.
Bananalander Balfour will go from end to end, but has
he the necessary foot? Since his advent at Randwick he
has done nothing approaching watch-breaking.
• • * *
Australia’s win of Carlingford Mile at Rosehill,
Saturday, carrying top weight (10.4) in a big field, makes
her look worth an investment for A. J.C. Doncaster Mile,
wherein she will hump only 7.4—35 t. less than in her
latest success. T’other day big bookie Oxenham is said
to have laid £2OOO to £lB the double—Australia and
Patronage—for Doncaster and Sydney Cun ; and former’s
trainer prepares Oxenham’s nags. Australia cost a big
sum as a yearling, but, though four years old now, she
has only secured three races- Filly Stakes at Flemington,
a six-furlong handicap at Maribyrnong, and last Satur-
day’s success. Previous question as to her ability to stay
a solidly-run mile should be settled now. Australia’s
speedy sister Carinthia hasn’t much liking for distance,
but a half-sister (Eleanor) sees a lengthy trip out well.
This scribe’s view is that what beats Australia will scoop
the coin.
• • • *
Hurdle Race at Rosehill, Saturday, saw two
nags come croppers—Snowstorm (with M’Kenna up) and
Bengal (ridden by A. Colley). M’Kenna had a forearm
broken, and Colley looked more seriously hurt than was
revealed by a surgical examination. Bengal had to be
shot. Such happenings are treated as common every-
day occurrences, but if the blood comes from a man’s
nose in a boxing contest, all Methodism is up in arms and
one of the great Sydney dailies sermonises over 1J cols, of
space in the best part of the paper. The ways of some
people are indeed inscrutable.

at ’Ome. Late in February he finished second (unbacked)
for Handicap Steeple at Manchester.
• * * •
“Le Chasse ”: The horse high-jumping at Ade-
laide’s March show (21/3/’O3) recalls the fact that a geld-
ing called Canary, once owned by old-time Paddy Pigott,
jumped 6ft. 10in., clear, in Kirk’s Bazaar, Melbourne,
Jan. 24,1870. Also, a saucy Scotch lassie, Miss M’Kelvie,
while following the Rangitiki (M.L.) hounds, during a
meet in 1883, cleared an iron gate sft. 6in. in height, the
distance from take-off to landing being 22ft. “ Yon was
a braw skip,” was her comment on looking back at the
gate ; and the red-coats heartily endorsed the remark.
• * * *
A smart sporting* publican in a somewhat
•portive northern town recently picked up a slippy mare,
and after a quiet private trial decided to run a little
weight off and then “ go for the gloves” at the next big
meeting. Accordingly he entered her for a small stake at
some races held on a neighboring course, and gave the
mount to his black-boy with strict orders to take hold of
her head at the start and hang on to it hard, so that he
could drive his field home before him. But the mare shot
out of the barrier like a rocket, took possession of affairs
(and her tiny rider also), and to the unspeakable disgust
of her owner, won by the length of a street. The poor
little nigger was so excited and fearful of getting a dose
of ‘ stirrup-oil” when he got home, that as soon as he
rode into the weighing-yard and saw his angry boss he
kept shouting at the top of his voice : “ Me couldn’t
‘stiff’ her, Bill, like you tole me to ; she got it bit in her
teeth an’ baal me able to ‘stiff’ her at all!” As the
racing officials were close handy, Bill’s feelings may be
better imagined than described.
* • • *
“Le Chasse”: Concerning the youthfulness
of old Byron Moore (B. 28/3/’O3). What about the age and
racing record of T. S. Olibborn who was appointed secre-
tary of the A.J.O. as far back as 1873? Randwick course
was opened with Sydney Subscription Races, April 30,
1834, when Bayley’s Whisker won the double, Town Plate,
6080v5., and Ladies’ Purse, £25. Whisker died about a
week afterwards, his owner having refused £I4OO for him
only three days before. Nothing seems more fatal to a
good horse, bull, or ram than the refusal by its owner of
a good price.
* m m #
S. E. Gregory, in his insolvency examination,
announced that his various trips with Australian Elevens
panned out thusly : 1890, £400; 1893, nil; 1896, £500;
1899, £7OO ; 1902, £BOO ; which seems to indicate that the
cricket touring business is an expanding one. The in-
crease in the trip just finished was due to the takings in
South Africa, and as this field is likely to improve, the
competition for places in the 1905 team is to be keener
than ever. Trumble, Darling, Howell, Carter, and
Gregory himself may already be considered back numbers.
So there is a fine chance for a few energetic pushing
young men (with a knowledge of bowling preferred) to
acquire a lucrative job in 1905.
• • • *
On performances the stolid, steady-going R. A.
Duff has easily eclipsed the dashing public idol, his first
wicket companion, Victor Trumper. Only on one
occasion in a big match this season has he missed the
century, the time he made 94 against S. Australia; co-
incidently, including the match against Warner and Cos.
Duff’s average for this past season is 94, and his best
score 194.
•'• • •
Jack Reedman, who has played cricket con-
tinuously in the S.A. XI. sinoe 1890, and has been a
member of the North Adelaide football club all the time,
is to be given a “ benefit ”by his club. Reedman, like
Giffen, is employed in the G.P.0., but has not nearly so
good a position. He got into a Combined Australian XI.
just once—the first match against Stoddart’s first team
in 1894.
• • • •
Except for the one good (on paper) bowling
performance when he got N.S.W. on a bad wicket,
Albert Trott’s return to Australia with the team-which-
isn’t-Lord-Hawke’s must be described as disappointing.
Warner relied on him for any chance of success against
N.S.W. and Victoria, and in each match, when success
seemed certain, the utter failure of the side’s bowling
meant defeat.

Dear B. —Warner, the captain of the departing
English cricket team, was “sounded” on the chance of
his bringing out a representative side next season. He
seemed to share the general impression that Maclaren
had a mortgage on the job, though why, beyond the fact
that Maclaren claims it, heaven only knows. Your
Society for Getting Rather Tired of Shakespeare might
be well adapted to the arrogant Lancashire gentleman-
professional.—X.B.

Decadence of S.A. cricket. Lord Hawke
team’s 553, Saturday, tops everything they have done in
Australasia ; previous best in an XI. a-side match was 380,
opposed to M.L. Burnup’s 103 is his highest, and
Taylor’s 105 equals figures credited to the same bat in
South Island of M.L. match.
• * • *
Recent match —Lord Hawke’s team v. N.S.W.—
brought a £6OO gate during the three days it lasted.
M.L. Cricket Council gets half this and pays exs. of the
amateur section of the visiting combination. This sum
verged on twice as much as the Melbourne “gate,” and
the Sydney crowd, second day (13,000), was the biggest
the Britishers had seen in any part of the continent.
* « * •
“M.” : There are many shining lights of
English cricket who are thirsting for a smack at Aus-
tralians on their own hill—and consequently Maclaren’s
refusal to bring out a team has turned the search for a
leader in other directions. Amongst those mentioned
is smiter Jessop, captain of Gloucestershire. Jessop is a
picturesque player—when he’s knocking the runs up, it
is just lovely to watch him, and his fielding is an object-
lesson. But, all the same, Maclaren’s yarn is a very lame
one—that he can’t get bowlers sufficiently strong and
good enough to undertake the trip. If the idea is to wait
until such trundlers are available, then Australia will
never see another English team—since champion county
Yorkshire won’t listen to the attempt to bring with the
team any of its professional players.
• * • •
Sydney Cricket season closed Saturday, when
Waverley easily beat Redfern. Sydney settled North
Sydney. Waverley succeeded to the premiership with six
triumphs, two beatings, and one drawn game. Lowest
compilation by the side in any match was 141. Eight
times runs notched exceeded 200. six times they were
above 300, and once beyond 400—6 wickets for 495.
Skipper A. O. K. Mackenzie did best individual work, he
made 3 “hundreds,” and missed getting another by 2
runs only ; his aggregate was 626—134 better than the
next man’s, O. Gregory. In the second grade Redfern
against ’Varsity hit up 729 for 8 wickets, a record for the
grade.
» * • m
A Queenslander’s remarks ononeform of suicide:
The Victorian football game has certain advantages. It is
fast, and easy for spectators to follow. Its disadvantages
are fairly numerous. It requires a full-sized cricket
ground, and a central umpire of great speed. In Victoria
the umpires do not often ppve satisfaction. Their atten-
tion is fully occupied with keeping on the ball, which
they have to throw in as often as it goes out of bounds.
This leaves chances, frequently taken, for players to deal
with too prominent opponents. The rules themselves add
to the difficulties of the umpire. Forinstance, amanwith
the ball must drop it on being tackled ; if not, free kick
against him. But if he drop it and the other does not im-
mediately let him go, free kick for him. No other game
has a Morton’s fork like this. Who can tell if either
broke a rule in a question of seconds ; yet, strictly, one
must have done so. A biassed umpire at Rugb3" can give
or withhold free kicks, but he cannot take them from one
side and give them to the other, which happens in the
Victorian game. Too much discretion is left to the
umpire in other ways, notably in the matter of rough
play. To know the umpire is worth many points in
deciding a team’s tactics. If the game were played for
its own sake it would be perfect. Played simply to win,
as it always is, it compares badly with Rugby.
* * * m
Encouraged by nearly £SOO profit on last year’s
transactions, Sydney Metropolitan Rugby Union intends
establishing a medical fund for the benefit of injured
players taking part in club competitions. It has long
been the custom of the Union to insure players, but the
scheme hasn’t worked smoothly. The proposed idea
is very puny, only £l5O being endowed, added to which
will be 5 per cent, of gross receipts of all matches. Still,
it may fulfil all requirements for years, bub it mostly
depends upon how the public roll up. The medical fund
won’t be an enormous one, and a few bad smashes on the
field will well-nigh break the bank.
• • * *
At the Glenelg (S.A.) sea-baths the manager
has trained a young bulldog to rescue-work, and his per-
formance is now a feature of all aquatic sports. At the
ringing of a bell he jumps up from his sleep on the shore,
runs 100yds. or so along the platforms to deep water, and
then goes for his drowning man. The “man” is gen-
erally a dummy, it having been found that the faithful
hound, in his anxiety to save life, is not too particular
where he takes hold. His average time for rescue, from
the bell, is well under 90 seconds.
* » 9 *
“Dudley”: Re modesty of Australian women
(8., 7/3/’O3). At the Br ghton (Vic.) Ladies’ Swimming
Club races, the masculine element was rigorously ex-
cluded. Some difficulty in arranging a site for the band
resulted in it being requested to make its noise outside
the baths ; but said band took this as an insult, and
“ regretted,’’ &c. At this awkward juncture the resource-
ful secretaryess engaged the Blind Asylum band. And
even then some dainty, dripping little swimmer would
hurry past the musicians, blushing right down to her
feet, in her scrap of pale-blue clothing. I don’t think
there was one who didn’t run for her life as she passed
the blind music-makers.
m m * m
Popular notions of the diving capabilities of
South Sea natives were completely shattered at a Sydney
swimming gala, Saturday, when seven much-advertised
Solomon Islanders gave a display which hadn’t the merit
of being passable, though it was funny frequently—
mainly through the state of fear and trembling they
were all in on the perch, perhaps 30ft. above the water.
One of them point-blank refused to jump when he looked
at the water, and went back down the ladder. All but
one leaped feet first, and their flight through space was
mostly helpless ; they reached the water back first,
stomach first, or anyhow. There are scores of Sydney
schoolboys who would do the thing, and do it well, any-
time, for a bit of fun. After this the man who says South
Sea Islanders can dive ought to be flattened out without
ceremony of any kind.
That human fish, Dick Cavill, beat his own
previous world’s record (59sec.) at Fort-street (Sydney)
School swimming club gala, Saturday, by l-ssec.,
which is just that fraction less than his 58 3-5 accom-
plished in England last year, and which sapient ’Ome
swimming authorities disallowed because'twas done in a
handicap though Cavill swam from scratch. This couldn’t
occur in any other country nor in any other branch of
athletics in England. Cavill finishes up the season the
biggest swimming wonder the world has known through
proving a top-notcher over all distances from 25 yards to
a mile.
■ • ■ •
Hopper, of Sydney Thousand fame, came here
from Yankeedom with Plugger Bill Martin, Palmer, and
Beauchamp last November. Ranked high as an amateur
in the U.S., Martin fixed him a third-class man. Sydney
carnival tempted Hopper here, and he waited patiently
for it. Though shaping well from the outset, he was
mostly beaten out of prize money, and only £3 came to
him till the big coup, for which he was a strong tip
before leaving Melbourne through coming on considerably
soon after the handicaps were published.
■ • * •
When it was given out to the Australian public
that the Sydney Thousand was the largest prize-money
ever offered for a bicycle race, many imagined the state-
ment included all kinds of motoring as well. _ Such is not
the case, however, for the biggest road race in the world
will be run in France during June and July. It will be
run in five heats over 1367 miles, and the prize-money
amounts to £4OOO. The mammoth contest is under the
auspices of the Auto journal (owned by Count Dion, of
De Dion Bouton motor fame).
* • » »
Looking through the latest catalogue of
“ bicycle accessories,” one is inclined to wonder if there
can be space enough on an average-sized machine to
hold them all, and what a jigger will look like when
completely fitted out with every invention which makers
declare to be a boon and a blessing to the cyclist. The
latest accessory is a self-registering attachment, which
delineates automatically every rise and fall of the road
passed over. How this is done is a mystery, but the
result is a long strip of paper on which is recorded what
looks to the uninitiated like a pen and ink sketch of a lot
of scalded Cochin China chickens chasing a herd
of “Daddy-long-legs” through a stretch of Bathurst
burrs. This the inventor declares to be a true record of
every hill and hollow, and the steepness of every gradient.
It is further claimed that this new idea will be of great
value in military matters, owing to the rapidity with
which a road may be surveyed, nothing more being
necessary for the surveyor than to do a ride along the
road in question. He propels the bike; the machine
does the rest.
• • • •
S.A. bike League’s meet —the biggest thing
it has tackled yet—opened Saturday, before, ’tis stated,
the largest crowd that ever looked at cycling in Adelaide.
Gate panned out £42o—considerably less than that £550
of the “ Lesna” meeting in ’97, which means the latter
mentioned was the more largely attended or higher
priced. Negro Taylor, whose presence cost £l6O,
was the star attraction, and so good were the
results that the full cost of the three days’
show is already nearly covered. Taylor was in
capital form, and got home first in every race
easily, bar the seventh heat of the Walne Stakes, when
Thorn pipped him on the post, but the black (qualifying
through being second in the fastest heat) got even in the
final by several lengths from Walker, who squelched
Thorn handily. Sir E. T. Smith Stakes were Taylor’s all
the way, and Lady Smith pinned the blue ribbon on his
coal-hued chest. Subsequently, Taylor was presented to
Lieut.-Governor Samuel Way and Mrs. Way. Wonder
how America will look to the despised “ cullud pusson ”
after this ?
• * • »
Negotiations are in progress to bring the fastest
cyclist in Europe to Australia next season. Ellegaard is
most likely to be the choice, and since he has scooped in
the world’s championships for the last two years, he is
certain to be as great a draw as imported Taylor.
• • • *
S.A. League of Wheelmen intends to found
a fund for injured cyclists, and will donate 5 per cent, of
the total profits of race meetings to the project.
Hitherto, the fines and fees —a very trifling amount —
have been used for the purpose, and have been insufficient
for requirements. The idea is worthy of adoption by the
leagues of the various States, more especially since motor-
racing has become a feature of bicycle-track race meetings
nowadays.
• * • •
Evidence of the popularity of bowls is the fact
that Victoria is sending representative teams to N.S.W.,
S.A., and Tas. next week, and a Ballarat combination is
also coming to Sydney. Strongest lot go to S.A., where
they are least wanted, as that section’s bowlers are only
just “ coming on.” Most important tussle is with N.S. W.,
wherein, owing to errors of judgment in selection and
through other causes, neither State will be fully repre-
sentative. Both teams are weak.
• * • »
Yic. bowling champion of champions final is
down for decision Thursday, this week. Individual cham-
pion and once fore-front cricketer, W. H. Cooper, went
under to Hardham with unexpected ease in semi-finals.
Cooper—consistent usually—was not so this time. Hard-
ham is among the most finished rollers of the “woods”
Victoria boasts. Old-hand Cuddon had a tough time
with comparatively unknown quantity Barr. Latter
went away, holding a big lead, then they were “ 17 all.”
Thirty ends saw scores—Cuddon 20, Barr 26. Close play
brought 29-28 in favor of Cuddon, but Barr got 3 next
end and won the best bowling match seen in Victoria
this season.
• * • *
Rawson Challenge Cup competition, engaged
in by Sydney bowling clubs and played on the same lines
as pennant matches (excepting that one defeat puts a
team out) has been fined down to St. George and Peter-
sham. Petersham has already secured the pennant and
“B” events this season, while St. George got into the
semi-finals of each, but no further. Petersham and St.
George never met in either event named, which fact in-
vests Rawson Cup final with more than ordinary interest.
Singular that in all the big bowling things of present
season (Individual Championship, Champion Rink, Pen-
nant and “B ” competitions) Petersham got the bye in
the penultimate stage.
* • • •
In these Federal times the game laws are pecu-
liar. Game may be shot with impunity S.A. or N.S.W.
side of the Murray, but woe betide the sportsman (if he
is caught) who tries to get his “bag” on the opposite
bank. Heaps of ducks are offered for sale in Melbourne
during the close season—all shot across the dividing
stream, of course. Why not Federal game laws—not
uniform, but marked by climatic limits to meet the dif-
ference in the breeding seasons?
* * * •
Bruise for N.S. W. bantam supremacy at Sydney
Golden Gate A.O. Saturday night ’tween Peter Toohey
and Ted Green was warm from round four. Green forced
and Toohey responded. They jostled and clashed like an
ice pack. Green was best after fierce fighting in the
fourth round, but things were more willing in the next,
and they were equal on points. Thence on ’twas Green
mostly—though not by much, till the 14th, when a right
swing sent Toohey with a heavy bump to the boards,
whence he rose dazed and weak but came again. Subse-
quent rounds showed Toohey never quite got over the
knock down thump. At the close of the 20th round
Green got the verdict amid great cheering. This was
their third fight; Toohey won the other two. Probablv
they’ll get together again and Green will most likelv
repeat his success. v
• • • •
One of the most skilful and vimful fights seen in
Sydney for long was that between bantams Bert Weel™.
and Dibley, at Sydney Gaiety A. 0., Monday nilhf
Weekes especially shone, his blocking and ducking bein£
high class. Often the pair stood toe to toe and punched*
with loads of weight behind the thumps, and the crowd
cheered incessantly. All manner of blows delivered in a
way reminiscent of the best days of Sydney’s boxing-i*
or 13 years ago-were seen. Never did excitement fW
once, neither was there any let up in vigor. Ultimately*
in the midst of hard fighting, Weekes landed a left swim?
on the jaw, sending Dibley to the boards for Bsec. Some
confusion here caused a cessation of hostilities till the
minute's spell ended, then Weekes got to work all life
aud soon had his man counted out while the building
rang with cheers. K
• * • *
Fifteen hundred people watched Lenton and
Truscott fight 20 gritty rounds at Sydney National S 0
last week. The pair stripped excellently, after weeks’of
thorough preparation out of town. Truscott stood un
better and reached longer than his sturdier antagonist
Lenton rushed the fighting, and scored well in the first
few rounds, while partisan shouts greeted every good
punch. Truscott—cool, clever and strong—waited and
watched, and punched a hard one in now and again
When he copped anything severe his “ devil ” got loose
and he made things buzz until the fit went off, then
Lenton notched again. So it went on, with Truscott
nearly always in the lead, and Lenton two or three times
apparently done. Grit and remarkable recuperative
power kept him going under a heavy pummelling till the
20th round closed, and then the referee declared Truscott
best on points amid approving applause.

Heavyweight pug. supremacy of Tasmania was
contested at Queenstown, t’other day, ’tween Zeehan’g
production Alf. James and Falstatfian-bellied Fred
Preston, of Melbourne, said to fight better that way
James was best from go, and beefy Fred, threw it up
midway through the fifth round. Attendance was
enormous, and at 10s. 6d., 55., and 4s. must have
brought over and above the £l3O offered as inducement
James subsequently stated that he purposed meeting
Felix or Doherty for Australasian supremacy. Where the
matter of premierships comes in with either of the two
is puzzling, as both Creedon and M’Call (the champion)
beat Doherty, who previously defeated Felix for the title
and M’Call has since laid Creedon low. If Tassy’s fore-
fronter wants the top-notch title he’ll have to treat with
the winner of coming Scanlan-M’Call battle, which even-
tuates at the Sydney National S.C., May 5.

N.S.W. Treasurer Waddell has stepped to
the front in the new role of calamity howler. He
says that, “in view of the terrible times,’’ some-
thing must be done, and hints at reducing the
wages of railway men, which were increased so
gaily a year or so ago, and cutting down the staff.
But seeing that Treasurer Waddell is enjoying the
largest revenue ever handled by any N.S.W.
Treasurer, and that he has Owe’Sullivan’s assur-
ance that the new works built by the Owe’ person
with the Owe’ money are bringing in 4 and 5 or
even 6 per cent. —which must mean a lot more
revenue in the near future —what is Waddell
calamity-howling about ?
Herbert Nicholls, M.H.A. (Tasmania), on
the poor tawdry ham-frills of State Governorship :
We can’t afford to pay for mere ornament. We don’t
want to set up a court; we want a man to carry on the
constitutional functions of the Government, and not to
set up a small and poor imitation of Windsor. We could
afford very well to govern this State on the lines of the
small Canadian provinces, where the Governor is Mr.
Jones—that is the name of one of them—with a salary of
£1200 per year. I have not heard anyone say that Mr.
Jones is inefficient, and that they require in his place a
viscount or someone with a distinguished career in the
English service. They don’t want anything of that sort,
and when we get rid of that sort of ornament it will not
be very long before we shall be very glad that we have
done it, and shall wonder how we endured the thing so
long.
It looks as though a whole lot of trouble
was being taken to save from prosecution one of
the very biggest of Westralian firmsj—a firm, too,
with the name of the very best known of Wes-
tralian families at its head. The charge is that,
by a series of very remarkable misunderstandings,
15 cattle, obviously suffering from pleuro, were got
through to the goldfields boxed with a large mob,
and that, by another remarkable mistake, these
15 were dropped at Coolgardie, while the sound
ones went on to Kalgoorlie, where an inspector
was in waiting. The story goes that the Stock
Dept, has been aching to prosecute, and so sift the
allegations, but cannot get authority, although
the Minister has now undertaken to personally
conduct an inquiry. The objection to that
arrangement, though, is that the Minister’s chief
is also the head of the law-firm which is the other
firm’s advisers.
Dill Macky’s careful pouring of oil on the
troubled flames was responsible for a fine flare-up
at Wyalong and another at Temora (N.S.W.) At
Wyalong revolvers were used freely, the Riot Act
read, and one miner is now lying in hospital in a
critical condition. If he dies there will be trial
for murder, and possibly another death. At
Temora there was more trouble, but, fortunately,
only property was injured. Dill Macky can now
lay to his soul the flattering unction that he is a
power in the land.

“ C.H.L.” : For healing caries of the
bone in the living body Professor Mosetig, of
Vienna, has discovered a new method which con-
sists chiefly in filling the cavities of the diseased
bones with a mixture of iodoform, sesame-
oil, and spermaceti. During the last session
of the Vienna Medical Society Dr. Mosetig
showed Rontgen pictures of the bones of patients
who had been thus completely cured.
“Burglar Bill” on a point of gaol eti-
quette : —
A peculiar institution “ inside ” is the “ prisoners’ dele-
gate ” who is sent to the cookhouse to represent the
prisoners and look after their interests. Each prisoner
takes the position in turn (holding it for one day only)
and goes to the gate in the morning to receive the daily
rations of meat, bread, hominy, etc., and to see that the
said rations are fit for consumption. Special rules are
laid down for the delegate’s guidance—in the event of
any part of the rations being, in his opinion, uneatable,
he has to draw the attention of the deputy-governor to
the matter. He is then induced to make a specific
charge against the quality of the ration, which
promptly lands him in No. 36 (the lockup cell).
Ration (or portion of it) in dispute, is placed
before the doctor on his arrival, and if that
important functionary rules that it is eatable, the
delegate is accused of “ making a false charge likely to
create dissatisfaction in gaol,” and before the visiting
“ beak ” may receive three to seven days’ cells, according
to the humor the “ beak ” may be in. The doctor is not
called as evidence, his report being given verbally by the
warder who charged the delegate, so the delegate is con-
victed on the alleged word of a man not in court, whose
alleged statement is retailed by the prosecutor. The
charge-book of one gaol contains numerous charges
against delegates for condemning food which was unfit
to eat, and which the prisoners returned untasted in
their “kits.” I have seen myself 40 tins of sour
“hominy” returned, the delegate being afraid to con-
demn it, and the authorities too negligent to take any
notice of this sign of disapproval. A prison doctor who
can deal with 15 sick prisoners in 2)4 minutes would say
anything was fit to eat.

WARNER’S SAFE CURE
THE KIDNEY AND LIVER SPECIFIC.
ITS POWER EXPLAINED.
It has been a source of wonder to many how any one medicine can possibly cure so many disorders
of apparently diverse origin, but to the scientific mind the curative properties of Warner’s Safe Cure
supply no element of mystery. Warner’s Safe Cure is simply the result of years of earnest research
for a remedy, which possessed the power to restore to health and activity both the kidneys and the
liver. Physicians were aware of the fact that, so long as the kidneys and the liver were working
strongly, nine-tenths of the ailments, with which the human race is afflicted, could not exist. The
difficulty was the finding of a safe and speedy medium which would act directly upon the kidneys and
liver m such a manner that their health and vigor should be restored when once they had become
either weak or diseased. Patient investigation terminated in the discovery of the medicine now
known throughout the civilised world as Warner’s Safe Cure. This remarkable medicine possesses a
curious and stimulating effect upon the kidneys and liver. Not only does it re-impart a healthy
action to those vital organs, but the stimulus is permanent. The importance of this great discovery
will be fully realised if the functions of the kidneys and liver are considered.
THE KIDNEYS
filter from the blood of the average person about three pints of water every day. In this water should
be dissolved about an ounce of urea, ten or twelve grains, in weight, of uric acid, and other animal
mineral matter varying from a third of an ounce to nearly an ounce. If the kidneys are weak or
diseased a piece of this solid refuse matter remains in the system, and becomes actively poisonous.
Minute crystals of uric acid form in the blood and are deposited in the joints and tissues of the body,
causing the acute pain experienced when we have
RHEUMATISM, GOUT,
liumbago, Neuralgia, Sciatica, &c. Sometimes the uric acid crystals aggregate in the kidneys
and form Gravel and Stones, and cause Kidney Colic, and Irritation and Inflammation of
the Bladder.
THE LIVER.
So intimate is the relation between the work done by the liver and that of the kidneys, that Tt is
almost impossible for either of them to be deranged without the other being directly affected. The
liver is an automatic chemical laboratory. In the liver various substances are actually made from the
blood. Two or three pounds of bile are thus made every day. The liver takes sugar from the blood,
converts it into another form, and stores it up so as to be able to again supply it to the blood as the
latter needs enrichment. The liver changes uric acid, which is insoluble, into urea, which is com-
pletely soluble, and the liver also deals with blood corpuscles which have lived their life and are
useful no longer. When the liver is inactive or diseased we suffer from Indigestion, Biliousness,
Jaundice, Anaemia, Sick Headache, Blood Disorders, and Debility.
Warner’s Safe Cure cures all the disorders named safely, surely, completely, and permanently,
simply because it restores to vigorous health and activity the kidneys and liver, and enables them to
efficiently perform their function of removing from the system the uric and biliary poisons which
cause the sickness.
Mrs. May Edwards, of Joseph Street, Balmain, N.S.W., says:—
“ I had kidney disease, which was brought on by a cold, for nearly eight years, causing great pain
across the back and loins, and making me sick at the stomach. I could neither eat nor sleep. I had
swellings in my ankles and pains in my legs, and was so ill that I had to hire a girl to do my work.
Reading in a book accounts of Warner’s Safe Cure, I decided to try the effect of this medicine in my
own case, and found immediate relief. The swelling left my ankles. I could eat well and sleep well
again. I took altogether eight bottles, which I procured from Mr. G. L. Harvison, Chemist, Western
Road. I never suffer at all now, and am really and truly cured. The medicine did me all the good
in the world.”
The commonest first symptoms of kidney disorder is
PAIN IN THE BACK.
A surer test is to place some urine, passed the first thing in the morning, in a covered glass, and
let it stand until the next morning. If it is then cloudy, or there is a brick-dust like sediment, if
particles float about in it, or it is of an unnatural colour, then the kidneys are not healthy and
Warner’s Safe Cure should be instantly resorted to or Bright’s Disease, Diabetes, or some less
serious but more painful illness will surely result.

together with accounts of many remarkable recoveries, will be sent free on application to H. H.
Warner and Co., Limited, Australasian Branch, Melbourne.

The Blot on the Commonwealth.
During the first weeks of the Australian Parlia-
ment a proposal was tabled for the appointment
of a Royal Commission to inquire into the treat-
ment of the Westralian aborigines. The motion
was never reached, and Westralian squatters’
influence was sufficiently strong in the Cabinet to
prevent the Government from acting in the matter
without the spur of a Parliamentary resolution.
Yet the position of the unhappy natives calls for
some immediate relief in view of the innumerable
reports of cruelty which, at any rate, appear to be
well authenticated. The position has not been
made any better by the Westralian Act of 1902
which, among other provisions, enacts that a
single J.P. may try an aborigine and sentence him
to any term of imprisonment up to two years or
to be flogged ; and that
Any aboriginal who neglects or refuses to enter or com-
mence service, deserts, or quits work, without his
master’s consent, or shall commit any other breach of his
contract, is liable to three months’ imprisonment, with
or without hard labor.
The Protector (save the mark !) of the aborigi-
nals pleads that floggings are few and not severe :
As to the use of the cat-o’-nine-tails, about which so
much has been said, I find that the cases where flogging
has been ordered are few. In answer to my close in-
quiries as to the sort of whip used, I find that the lashes
are small cord, about a quarter of an inch in diameter.
But, however few the floggings may be, and what-
ever the dimensions of the implement used, it is a
foul blot on Australia that, in any State, a single
J.P. (in many cases a squatter, and himself
an employer of indentured aboriginal labor) can
order a native to be flogged. It is a Simon
Legree system, with the added infamy that in
Australia Legree needn’t stain his hands as well
as his soul with the whip. He orders, and the
State does the flogging.
4
The chances of dying in N.S.W. keep
pretty good ; 16,646 people passed out last year,
against 16,021 in 1901, and 15,118 the year before
that. Of all mortal diseases cancer makes the
most steady progress along its horrid way ; the
regularity of its movement is scarcely disturbed.
Five years ago it had 544 victims per million
people ; last year, 624. In the last two dry years
pneumonia has bulked largest; in 1902 the start-
ling rate of 977 per million was reached, or an in-
crease of about 70 per cent, on the figures of three
years back. Of the others, phthisis, enteritis and
violence run almost neck and neck, each with
about one victim to every 1200 people ; while the
habit of dying of old age occurs annually pretty
regularly to about 700 people in every million.
The Importer.
Australia, thanks to the honest and rigid
administration of Customs Minister Kingston, is
beginning to size up its old acquaintance, the
softgoods importer. Being sized up he proves, in
a large proportion of instances, to be a conscience-
less swindler, a whited sepulchre, a liar, and a
ir harisee in a bell topper. A great lack of prin-
ciple appears to pervade the importing class in
general, but the softgoods importer stands up
like a tower as. the worst specimen of his
order. The habit of defrauding the revenue
e “Ogus invoices appears to have been
of such long continuance with this class
that it had ceased to regard it as in any
way disgraceful. The softgoods importer is
deafeningly '* loyal ” in a large proportion of
cases, but his scruples about defrauding his
Majesty out of his Majesty’s just and lawful cash
often appear to be nil. Of course, all importers are
not equally blameable even among the swindling
class; some of them started the swindle system,
and some followed suit because the man who
doesn t swindle is at a grievous disadvantage com-
pared with the man who does; but, even allowing
for that, a lamentable moral odor attaches to the
trade. Arid the importer, honest or dishonest, is
by the necessities of his trade, in a great measure
a public enemy. He lives on the absence of local
industries to employ the people. Every new indus-
try that is started for the enrichment of Australia
damages the importers business and his profits. He
wants Australia to have enough money to enable
it to buy his goods—money begged or borrowed,
or money made at any kind of toil, however un-
remunerative, that doesn’t interfere with his im-
porting business. Therefore he constantly im-
presses on Australia that its sole destiny is to
make a miserable living at wresting wheat out of
a barren soil for export to a not too remunerative
market at the far end of the world ; at herding
sheep and cattle on the dry plains; at getting
poisoned in the lead mines of Broken Hill; at
specking for gold ; at tearing the country’s irre-
placeable coal capital out of the ground and selling
it to the foreigner ; at building unprofitable public
works out of loan money. But he doesn’t want
it to fake to the more remunerative manu-
facturing industry, and become rich in a
way that would enable it to dispense
with him. If he had to choose between an
almost starving Australia, which could still buy a
little from him, and a rich Australia, which didn’t
require his goods at all, his interests all lie in
trying to make this country the former kind of an
Australia. So, whenever great new industries are
threatened, he rises, as a matter of sordid necessity,
to point out that such things are disastrous, ruinous,
and impossible, and that though Germans and
Frenchmen and Americans may possess them,
there is some unexplainable reason why the in-
ferior Australian can’t. That he should say such
things isn’t wonderful, for almost anybody will
lie when his living depends on it. The thing that
is wonderful is that Australia should take him
seriously as a reliable witness in such a case—
should listen to him in Parliament when he
assures, the country that the only thing that can ,
save it is not to start new industries and thus dis-
turb his profits, and should buy the papers which he
controls, and which set out, in his interest, that the j
country’s wealth and greatness depend on not
having work for its people, but rather in letting !
them stand idle and hungry on a wharf and watch
the products of other people’s labor being hauled
ashore from other people’s ships. The importers’
journals complain bitterly that the Protectionist
party talks of the importer as if he were a public
enemy. What else can he be ?

A Maoriland correspondent:—
Experience has convinced me that the grant of female
franchise has augmented the political influence of the
parsonical element. The new Parliament contains a con-
siderable proportion of Prohibitionists and lay-preachers,
and in spite of all we hear about the separation of Church
from State and the divorce of politics from religion, it is
mere humbug to say that these ranters don’t blend the
two. The Prohibition crusade is very largely a religious
crusade; in fact, cold-water principles are now prac-
tically dogmas in certain churches, and the ordinary
member “in communion” has a horror of being
seen even in the vicinity of an hotel. I have an
idea that a heavy onslaught will be made next
session on the totalisator, and if the “goody-goodies”
get their way, we ll have no Sunday trains very shortly.
The influence which these water-weepers have gained in
this country is making Maoriland a loveless, joyless land
—a land where the man takes his best girl for a walk in
the cemetery on a wet day, and they sit together on the
tombstone of the late Rev John Howlkr and discourse on
the blessedness of never drinking anything but cold tea
and always being home before 9 p.m.
——4,
Dr. Canute Wilkinson’s notion of a dis-
pensary for the consumptive sick in Sydney is an
excellent one ; such an institution would be a
valuable aid to future hospital organisation. It
would tend to locate consumption cases in then-
earlier stages ; would give opportunities to impress
upon sufferers proper precautionary and remedial
treatment; and would doubtless contribute to
many cures.
“ Darkholer,” or some such name, writes :
“ Burglar Bill’s ” facts (B. 21/2/o’3) from one gaol cannot
warrant an inference of general applicability. I spent
nearly two years in a N.S.W. “ first-timer’s ” gaol. I
listened to many prisoners. Hardly 8 per cent, acknow-
ledged being guilty. Only one admitted to a revolting
crime. He, with another, murdered a hawker. He
declared his motive was to get a silk handkerchief for his
mother ; he considered this an extenuation. A prisoner’s
account of his fall must generally be accepted with a ton
of rock-salt. Truth is little regarded either by prisoners
or warders. I once knew a warder, though, to tell a
noble lie to save a prisoner from the dark cell. Another
generalisation of mine is that convicts and their keepers
have little veneration for an oath. “ The system ” does
not make prisoners truthful or manly, but it tends to
make them more prudent, cautious, dissimulative, and
cynical. “ It’s in gaol you hear the truth about a case,”
says “Bill.” The listener, like the purchaser, must
beware. Here are two instances for a sample : A prisoner
who“smooged” to me for tobacco said he was in for
“passing a cheque”; I discovered it was for crimi-
nal assault on a child. Another admitted “horse-
soldiering”; he was convicted for sneak-theft. The
number system can’t hide a man’s identity for
long. A small percentage of the prisoners are black-
mailers (for tobacco), “blabs,” and official “crows.” I
overheard the chief warder tell a lifer to keep watch on a
new warder, who was recognised as an ex-phrenological
lecturer of Sydney Domain. Next day the lifer told me
the new warder would soon get “shot.” “Why?” I
asked. “Because he don’t rouse on the prisoners
enough,” was the reply. This lifer was untruthful, decep-
tive, and malicious. The warders feared him like corn-
tiers a king’s mistress. “The chief,” the lifer told me,
promised to get him out in about three years if he “saw
that the screws and prisoners did nothing wrong.” The
lifer’s prediction was verified. The new warder got the
sack three days afterwards. The warders know all the
tricks and plants ; but they dislike increasing their dis-
agreeable tasks. A great number of the warders are
easy-going fellows, who want to get their salary with as
little trouble as possible. Generally it is from a prisoner
“crow" that the higher officials'first learn of a new
trick, plant, or “ telegraph.” >'he warder hurries
through the search, and blinks. The foolish prisoner
brags about how smart he was. The “ crow " hears and
reports, and then a new order is issued. “ Bill’s” facts
about bush crimes and bush prisoners don’t agree with
my examination. I found that the towns and settled
farming districts contributed 18 revolting cases out of 22.
I also found the bushmen morally the best fellows in
gaol. But they ware not, a* a rule, “ good prisoners."

REFORM WITHOUT REVOLUTION.—The
great and particular merit of Hunyadi Janos is
that it relieves without discomfort, in other words,
it effects reform without revolution. This is
because it is the best and safest natural aperient.*
Judging from the result of Vic. Reform
Conference the chief factor in winning success—
D.C.L. Whisky—was more honored in the observ-
ance by the Council managers, who won all along
the line, than the Assembly representatives.
Trust Fatman to discover what’s what in drinking
material. To neglect in the smallest degree to
draw true wisdom and strength of purpose in any
time of emergency from the correct brand is to
risk the best chance of achievement —D.C.L. The
Whisky of The Wise.*
A compact and ingenious contrivance is Tre«
whella Brothers’ (Trentharu, Vic.) Patent Log and Stump
Jack, which is so rapidly becoming a favorite for stump-
