MELBOURNE 8 GREATEST EASTER ATT RAO
Maloolm G.M. (W.A.), after its third recon-
struction, has made a fresh start. Co. owns 144 acres at
Mt. Malcolm, on which a main shaft is down 500 ft. In a
west crosscut at this depth the reef has been cut, and
driven on north and south for 150 ft., and a winze sunk
below this level. Going north is a blank, but, in the
south drive, the reef is 3ft. wide and worth loz. per ton.
00. owns a 30-head mill and cyanide plant capable ot
treating 3000 tons monthly. Vigorous development is
now going on under new management, and co. snouia
soon be a regular producer, as the mine is all right, but
has been very badly handled. Co. is in 325,000 shares a
rather tall order for an undeveloped proposition.
• * * *
Hampton Plains Co. (W.A.) is not yet satisfied
that its huge property is a wild cat, and intends giving
it a further test with diamond drills. Directors are to
follow the cue of the Associated Northern, and raise pro-
specting funds to the tune of £25,000 by promoting a
syndicate in Is. shares. At present work is confined to a
very big formation on Block 50, known as Hampton
Boulder. The ore only averages sdwt. per ton, but this
is alleged to more than cover costs. The area of the estate
is so extensive—l6,ooo acres-that no single co. could
possibly prospect it thoroughly, and co.’s only chance i
to give fossickers exceptionally tempting terms to induce
them to co-operate. Instead of doing so they have (until
quite recently, when it was too late) offered worse terms
than Government. Co. is badly handicapped by not
having a railway to its property. This was projected,
but the Labor party blocked the Bill in Parliament
• * • *
Great Fingall (W.A.), is evidently going to be
run for all it is worth by Bewick, Moreing and Co.
Two years ago, in order to afford their employees no
excuse for gambling in scrip, the general meeting,
on the proposal of the late secretary m his
capacity of director, agreed to allow the stan
two per cent, on all divg. declared. Yet the trouble
came just the same—likewise the bogus scrip, the
subsequent proposal to issue 30,000 additional
shares at £5 each, probably to meet shortages, was
bad enough, but the suggested purchase of the West
Fin gall for £lOO,OOO cash is perfectly scandalous, me
property consists of some leases pegged out h.V B.M. and
Co., and sold to the Great Fingall 00. for 5000
shares. Two years ago they were floated off as west
Fingall, a co. of 250,000 shares. Of these the Great Un-
gall got 100,000 shares, and B.M. and Co., for putting
through the deal, got a similar quantity. At time of
flotation it was specially' stipulated that the underlay oj
the reef, if in the West’s property, still belonged to, and
could be followed by, the Great Fingall co. Two y earB
prospecting by diamond-drilling has proved these west
blocks practically worthless.
* • * •
South Kalgurli (W.A.), has now expended
£188,500 of Bull’s hard cash, in addition to another
£220,000, proceeds of gold w’on, and seems as far off the
dividend stage as ever. To instal the Merton s furnaces
and other treatment plant recommended by Boulder
Hamilton, it is proposed to raise more capital. Some of
the long-suffering shareholders object to this, and are
taking active steps to have somebody fired out first. The
costs, which were estimated by the management at 405.,
have been 625. per ton, and the grumblers want to know
the reason why. The mine is in a dicky' state, and it is
taking the management all its time to maintain a monthly
return of any sort. A serious collapse in output may
occur at any moment. The nest-egg is entirely' exhausted,
so that future monthly returns are bound to be more or
less fluctuating. For the amount of money spent, the
mine and its equipment make about the poorest showing
of any mine on earth. The recent rise in scrip is due to a
bear squeeze.
• * • ■
North Kalgurli (W.A.) has decided to erect an
up-to-date 20-head battery of 12001 b. stamps, capable of
treating 2000 tons of ore monthly, and supplemented by
filter presses to treat the slimes. This is a much more
extensive plant than the manager recommended, as he
limited the expend ture to £5OOO, and the public should
not jump to the conclusion that, because such a plant is
to be ordered, the present position of the mine in any
way warrants it. Where the purchase price of the
machinery is to come from is not stated. As here re-
marked, the lodes in the mine are very much faulted and
erratic in values, more especially in the lower levels, the
longest pay chute being only about 200 ft. As the lodes
on the field are, in places at least, much wider
than the drives, breaking down ore for the battery
often discloses unexpected treasures. This happened in
the case of the adjoining Hainault, and may occur with
this mine. Some 2000 tons of pay-ore are already on the
dump, in addition to the amount blocked out below, so
that the battery is pretty safe for a 12 months’run, at
least, on 15dwt. ore. The purchase and erection of bat-
tery will more than exhaust all available funds, and co.
will have to depend on revenue to keep things going—a
rather unsatisfactory prospect for such a patchy proposi-
tion. The erection of the battery is bound to boom scrip
and give sensible shareholders a long-looked-for chance
of passing on the baby to others to nurse.
• • * *
Kalgoorlie Amalgamated (W.A.), with its
£50,000 fresh capital, is busy diamond-drilling on its ex-
tensive propertv. Two bores have been put down to
1000 ft. each, a third to 600 ft., and a fourth to 400 ft. All
these bores have passed through lodes of sorts, but none
of them showed payable results. 00. has secured some
further blocks in the same neighbourhood as its Block 45,
and now owns 142 acres in all. The most promising pro-
perty is the Hannan’s Croesus, on which a fairly good lode
is beino' worked. The True Blue West, a small lease,
adjoining the Brown Hill Co. on the west, may be worth
something as a position block. Tributors have done well
on several of the leases, and are very anxious to resume
work if allowed.
• • • •
Westralia is just now getting a clear view of the
worm which is at the root of all efforts to fix wages. The
“ boss,” when his business isn’t too flourishing, points to
his profits, or the place where the profits ought to be,
and demands that the wages shall be low accordingly ;
and the workman in that case points to the cost of decent
living and asks us to glue our eye to that. In the
Westralian case the court has said that Kalgoorlie must
pay its miners 11s. Bd. per shift. The workman, taking
that 11s. Bd. as a standard, says that the cost of living is
much higher on the out-fields, wherefore the Peak Hill
miner must have 14s. fid. ; while the “ boss ” points to the
fact that Kalgoorlie averages 1‘420z. gold per ton of stone
against Peak Hill’s '840z., wherefore the Peak Hill miner
ought not to be paid as much as the other. Of course,
the worm is there, no matter how the fixing is done ; so
its appearance in this case is no argument against the
Arbitration Court. It rather illustrates the difficulties of
arbitration and the hopelessness of laying down hard-and-
fast rules and governing principles.
* * * •
Queensland Local Govt. Act, in force from the
end of March, deals with the hotlv-dehated question of mine
rates in a fashion which will not yield much revenue to
the interested municipalities. The value of a gold mine,
is deemed to be 20 times the rent of the lease; other
mining lands are deemed to be worth as much as any
other land, unimproved, in the neighbourhood—and land
in the neighborhood of mines is generally a wilderness of
rocks and jam tins, worth half-a-crown or so an acre. It
isn’t clear, in any case, why a mostly-absentee mining co.
should not pay something 'substantial outof its profits to
improve the odious natural conditions of the district
where the miners have to put in their live*.

The fire which started in the Broken Hill Pro-
prietary mine about six years ago is still smouldering;
and whenever a crack occurs in the loose wall which
they have built around it a sickening gas pourß through,
and the miners have a worse time than usual. While it
stays where it is the fire is doing little harm ; but it has
to be constantly watched, and there is, besides, the
gnawing expense of keeping the neighboring workings
reasonably clear of the leaking gas.
At Poverty Point.
An Adelaide Waxworks show advertises: “Just landed.
Now on view. Mrs. Sangal’s baby.” What a life’s
beginning!
A comedy company with the capable Harry Plimmer in
command, and Miss Florence Seymour for leading lady, is
going to play some of Brough’s old pieces. The tour
starts at Auckland in May or June, and three fine selec-
tions from the Brough repertoire will be staged—
“ Second Mrs. Tanqueray,” “The Village Priest,” and
“ The Tyranny of Tears.” Miss Seymour and Plimmer
should make an interesting Tanqueray couple, anyhow.
Latest Chicago (U.S.A.) theatrical attraction is an
actor who takes fits in a masterly and horrible fashion.
He had to simulate an epileptic in a yellow drama, and
his realistic fits were the main draw of the piece. Now
he is in great demand for music-halls, afternoon teas and
“at homes”—getting huge fees for throwing a fit in a
life-like fashion.
The tremendous Augustus Glover, who died in the
hospital at Napier (M.L.), lies buried in the cemetery of
that pleasant, sleepy little town. There are few quieter,
cooler, greener places to die in than Napier, and when a
man goes to sleep permanently in a town that never was
quite awake he has the satisfaction of knowing that he
isn’t so widely different from the rest of the inhabitants
after all.
Said that J. C. Williamson is going to introduce the
haka dance into the production of his Maori opera. The
haka, carefully sandpapered, bevelled at the edges,
varnished and polished, and properly clothed with as
much rag as the average ballet, is a thing to make the
hair of the front stall Johnnies stand up like a church
tower. But if the Maoris, who are said to be specially
engaged for the show, get a few drinks in some night,
and the music gets to their heads, the mats will be
whirled off, and the audience will pour out in wild haste
to bring in a medical man to help some old Maori wahine
to get her stomach back from over her shoulder. The
few hakas this writer saw danced by the natives in a
certain portion of the King country were marvels of con-
tortion and dislocation.
A well-known theatrical manager is said to have
recovered a large sum of money from an employee whose
defalcations amounted to an act of madness.
Cremated on the 18th March, at Sydney, in the presence
of a good many pets of her profession, Milly Adams, a
once popular actress. She was cabled for to Shanghai,
where a sister lay very ill, and, on arrival, was herself
stricken down by typhoid. By her own wish her body
was brought to Australia and given to the flames.
Melba, booked to make her farewell appearance at the
Melbourne Exhibition Building on Saturday night last,
finished up her Australian tour in characteristic Nellie
Melba fashion—had a royal row with manager Musgrove,
and absolutely refused to do any farewelling at all.
Hardly any publicity was given to the abandonment, and
the first that wrathful ticket-holders knew of it was
when they rolled up for their seats and were glumly told
that the diva wasn’t on tap just then. The harassed
Musgrove states that, he being responsible to the public,
he wanted the farewelling done according to his ideas
(whatever they were), but that Melba wanted them run
on her lines (whatever they were); the result being that
the well-advertised “absolutely last appearance” wasn't
an appearance at all. But what was the difference of
opinion between the two? What did Musgrove want
done that Melba wouldn’t? And what did Melba want to
do which Musgrove wouldn't ?
“A.H.”: Apropos of Tub Bulletin’s complaint that
Grundy, in “Mrs. Hilary Regrets,” gives his “county
family ” people the manners of the kitchen and stable, it
would seem a habit of some English writers to discourse
of “high life” in absolute ignorance of its customs.
Arabella Kenealy, in the much-boomed “ A Semi-detached
Marriage,” makes the bridegroom, a wealthy man of title,
stipulate for the semi-detachment on the ground that
“ no one could continue to love a woman after seeing her
clean her teeth.” The absurd would-be epigram showed
complete ignorance of the ways of civilised life. If
dressing-rooms are not a matter of course in well-to-do
homes, a bathroom, at least, may be presumed. George
Moore, from whom Arabella plagiarises, shows the same
simplicity when he lets his hero declare that “ No man’s
love survives the sight of his bride washing over a hand-
basin.” By the way, about 15 years ago a London society
paper stated, as proof of the alleged uncivilised habits of
Scotchmen, that even wealthy commercial men in that
country habitually washed their faces at the kitchen sink.
“ Melb.” : “ Midsummer Night’s Dream ” is in rehearsal
at Melb. Princess’s. Up till a comparatively recent time,
the dictum was that this piece could not be staged, but
Shakespeare knew what he was about. You will not find
a notable production of “Midsummer Night’s Dream”
till Phelps gave it at Sadler’s Wells, with Rose Edouin,
our Mrs. G. B. W. Lewis, as Puck, and himself as
an unapproachable Bottom the Weaver. G. Kean
brought it out in rivalry at the Princess, with Harley
as Bottom. Douglas Jerrold hated Kean, and there is
hardly such a glowing dramatic criticism anywhere as
Douglas’s account of the Sadler’s Wells production.
The piece has recently been done in London severally by
Beerbohm Tree and Benson. There have been two pro-
ductions in Melbourne. The last was Rignold’s. The
other was at the former Princess’s, with Jefferson, Heir,
Lambert, Stewart, Mrs. Heir, Rosa Dunn, Emma Neville,
and Julia Matthews.
John Tait achieved a satisfactory settlement of his
action against Melba for breach of a verbal contract made
by her brother-in-law, Patterson. Plaintiff, who had been
promised the management of Melba’s run through Maori-
land, got £125 and costs. The four Melbourne brothers
Tait—young in fact, old in tact, and mere boys in appear-
ance—are a smart quartette of musical and theatrical
business men. Charles Tait, the eldest, is identified
with Allan’s music warehouse, and all concert arrange-
ments appertaining thereto. He has also had experience
on tour. It was the astute Charlie who went to Westralia
with the Italian co. last year. E. J. Tait, apparently the
youngest brother, acts as treasurer at the Williamson
theatre in Melbourne. James Nevin Tait takes charge of
the Dolores tour in America, and John, the lawyer of the
family, takes compensation as aforesaid for his disappoint-
ment in the Melba matter. The four of them are so much
alike that they advertise one another. To meet one
brother suddenly is to think of the other three, and
wonder which Tait he isn’t.
“Haresfoot”: Re the late Augustus Glover: In the
London AniEN.tuM, for 1867, he is referred to as a
promising actor, in one important part. Pascoe’s
Dramatic List, 1879, says Glover’s real name was De
Fivas. He had a rather foreign appearance. What a
finely set-up, stalwart, well-preserved man! Had the
school of Macready, Phelps, Brooke, and Creswick con-
tinued to flourish, he would have attained a high position.
He might have been as good as James Anderson in such
parts as Ingomar or Macbeth. But he did not get the
practice to keep him out of stereotyped melodrama.
The son of a well-known Mount Morgan-
aire, whose death was mentioned in this paper
last week, writes to point out that the deceased
was Thomas Squire Morgan, not James Morgan,
as printed.
Pastor Bailhache, Church of England, for-
merly a fashionable London Baptist preacher, is
dead. Came to Melbourne in 1875 to replace
Chas. Clark. The church proved a frost, and
Bishop Moorhouse converted him to a better-paid
faith at the same time with Congregational
Pastor S. C. Kent, formerly of Sydney.
Melbourne Age lost no time in notifying
the conviction of Robert Reid’s firm for defraud-
ing the customs. Half-an-hour after the news
came through, the block strollers were enticed
across Collins-st. by a large placard, only to learn
that their softgoods joss had fallen in on every
charge and been fined £5O, just like a common
drunk or vagrant. The foreign-trade Argus was
discreetly silent on the matter.
People remember now with some interest
that in 1901, before the new Australian Govern-
ment had fully taken charge of the customs,
alleged Senator Robert Reid and some other im-
porters formed an influential deputation to pro-
test against customs leakages and the sinful,
wholesale way other importers swindled the cus-
toms, especially at Brisbane. The investigation,
which Mr. Kingston started on hearing from
these importers what a darned bad, fraudulent lot
importers were, led to many things—the prosecu-
tion of the principal deputationist for one.
Victorian Labor-member Bromley has ob-
tained a verdict for £5OO damages from Melbourne
Table Talk, which published an article insinuat-
ing that he had stolen money from his union (the
Tinsmiths). There was not one atom of fact on
which to base such an insinuation ; but the news-
paper, possibly, thought that a libel, if insinuated
only and not said straight out, would be safe.
There could be no greater mistake. Mr. J. Woolf
had charge of Mr. Bromley’s case, and this vic-
tory, following on that in the Trenwith case,
makes him a proud Woolf indeed.
Next Wednesday (April 8) will witness the
opening of the N.S.W. Agricultural Society’s
great exhibition, and it is expected that the
carnival will he one of the most successful yet
promoted. The entries far outnumber those
received for any previous show. The jumping dis-
plays are expected to be especially good, the
nominations including all the State’s best timber-
topping talent. Trotting and motor-car parades
and races will be held daily, and many innovations
are promised.
“ Melb.” : Last night I went to our suburb
of Newmarket, and heard Kingston for the first
time, outside the House of Representatives. He
spoke at a meeting of Manger’s, the attendance
about 1200. This is Kingston’s trial gallop, before
his Queensland campaign. His first Victorian
meeting, and a memorable occasion. Seldom have
I known a speaker sit down with such a fusillade
of cheering. He gripped the people all along,
with a style utterly different from that of any
other man they ever heard. His earnestness was
tremendous and overwhelming. Watch him sweep
the board in Queensland. What a contrast to
Reid s funnyisms, and Barton’s nonchalance !
The brassy Law of Balmain (N.S.W.)
doesn’t now conceal the fact that he is a rene-
gade from the Labor party, and he is stumping
the Tamworth electorate in favor of Garland, the
candidate who is being championed as the one
man whose election will please the London Cohens.
And the unspeakably brassy Law is talking about
the Friedman case:
He believed that he uttered the Gospel truth when he
said that the Government had committed a great political
crime in polluting the fountain of justice over the Fried-
man case.
Yet this same Mr. Law voted with the Govern-
ment on a definite vote of censure on the Friedman
case. Later, he failed to get £75 for the Balmain
Choral Union, and then things began to drop.
A Brisbane correspondent:—
One of the most remarkable figures that Brisbane h.»
known was Mr. Entwhistle. who has just left for M L on
a strange mission. He is now in his 85th year ’ /ft,
much active service as a soldier in India he emigrated tl,
M. L. in '7O, and went thence to Melbourne, where he
mainly instrumental in the formation of the Victoria
Electric Light Company, which first installed electricity,
at Paddy’s Market. After roaming Australia he settles
in Brisbane in 1902. Entwhistle’* experience of marr ed
life has been curious. He was the father of five childreT
two years after his first marriage. He has buried seven
wives, and has 27 children. Some time ago his tenth son
disappeared mysteriously in M.L., and has not been heard
of since. The old man, therefore, a few weeks threw
up a permanency of £4 a week, and is now traversing
Maoriland in a lingering hope of finding and giving the
last resting place to the bones of a favorite boy. b

“ Burglar Bill ” asks a question :
Why don't the authorities provide in every new police
station an entrance for the prison van where that vehicle
can load and unload in seclusion ? At the newly erected
No. 2, in Regent-street, the van loads up in a side street,
to the amusement of a crowd of street arabs and dirty
s'atterns who gather round on its arrival. Passed the
other morning, and an Irish policeman was assuring two
slovenly women “ They’ll be out in a minyet
now.” Generally the unfortunates who are taking
the journey have to stand a few minutes on
the pavement while some small matters are being
attended to, and the slattern with the huge, mouldy
infant, and the pale, fat woman, and the long:, gaun t
woman with the short skirt and the unlaced bo an ."
the innumerable small boy, all collect round. There is
too much publicity about the removal of \\ ith
myself, the worst few minutes of my ‘lagging- \\ere
spent in marching up Sydney platform to catch the Gou -
burn mail. I was offside leader in a team of six, one of the
six being aged 75 and a confirmed jibber, who hung back
in the harness, despite vigorous requests to hurry up-
Mv trousers were 3in. over my boots, and refused to have
anything to do with the vest, and my hat was three sizes too
small, and I carried a bundle, and was handcuffed to the
chain, and held up the string supporting the leg-irons,
and I hadn’t a hand free with which to brain the nearest
of the crowd which ran silently alongside, with its eyes
wide open and its mouth gaping.

Queensland, during the last two or three
months, has lost a good many of its young far-
mers, principally from Darling Downs, who
have emigrated to Canada. 1 he Moana, last
trip, took several families from Warwick,
representing a considerable amount of capi-
tal. The Canadian Government, it is under-
stood .allots 160 acres of _ good country to
each immigrant, and supplies implements, etc., on
long terms. The settler is required to clear and
cultivate 30 acres within three years, when, he wi 1 1
receive the deeds of his holding. Granting the
land is good, and the conditions as simple as
stated, Australia will have to hustle to keep the
cream of their settlers in Debtland. It s the
provident hard-grafter, who has a little means,
who generally responds to offers of this kind, ana
these are the men who can be ill-spared.
THE PIANOLA
Y,*tL
n
f-
v
K.
V -a.
sew:
What
It
Means
To
You.
If you are the owner of a piano which is seldom or never used, little
benefit is derived from it by either you or your family. By the purchase
of a Pianola every member of your family can play the piano, although he
or she may never have played one note in their life.
The striking of the notes of the selection, in proper time and place, is
ao concern of the player. This is correctly done by the Pianola.
The player’s only task is to decide how slow or how fast the notes shall
be played, or how loud or soft he wants them to sound.
Three little levers serve to impart the player’s wishes to the piano.
Should the selection not be familiar, a very simple set of markings is given
on the music-roll which unwinds in front of the player, directing the proper
movement of the levers.
If you will write us, asking for Catalogue B, we will mail you one
free, explaining more fully the Pianola.
The price of the Pianola is £6O prompt cash, or £65 on moderate
monthly payments.

I AYNE’S EXPECTORANT
CURES THE WORSTCOUGHi.
The Downfall of Billie.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
" Who,” said the Man Who Had Endured Much,
"was Billie?”
"Oh!” said the Ordinary Girl, with a start;
"how did you know ?"
"I don’t” (hunting for a match); "but I’m
willing to learn.”
“It’s not particularly interesting,” said the
Girl, with a meditative frown. “ But I ’ll tell
you.” Then thoughtfully. "You see, it hap-
pened while you were away.”
“Things generally do,” murmured the Man,
sadly. The Girl looked reproachful.
“Think of the monotony,” she said.
He who endured tried to smile hypocritically.
“He had such nice manners,” reflected the
Girl; "at least at first,” settling comfortably
into reminiscence. "He reminded me of you,”
meditatively, sinking a dainty chin into a fluffy
pink cushion.
“ Where,” said the Man, sternly, ignoring the
flattery, “ did you run against him ? "
“At the Slommers’ At Home,” moving the
cushion, tentatively. “It was so slow ;it was so
hot; then Bell introduced him just as I was about
to flee, and we grew mutually enthusiastic over
strawberry ice, the general deadliness of ‘at
homes,’ and—and—other things,” vaguely.
"As per usual," said the Man, with his eye on
the oushion.
She smiled guiltily. “ I don’t think it is very
interesting,” appealingly, and moving the oushion.
Silence.
“Oh, well,” replacing it pettishly. "If I can
remember” (wrathfully), “I met him at every-
thing after that” (in a tired voice). “He went
everywhere. He was really an indefatigable
youth ; never dodged duty calls ” (sitting up, with
a stern eye), “ and never pleaded football meet-
ings as excuses for nasty old dinners.”
The shoulders of the delinquent opposite shook
slightly. ...
“He never” (speaking in a stern voice)
"fled gardenwards to avoid Minnie Askew’s
demand for dances ! He did his duty ! ” (loftily).
“He even” (in an awed whisper, clenching the
oushion) “took Mrs. Scoffer down to supper ! ”
“By Jove ! ” said the Man, startled. She sank
back demurely, and continued lazily : “He took
me for drives with the sweetest pony ” (mali-
ciously), “ and the first time I drove, but not the
next ’’ (virtuouslvl.
“Why?” (stiffly).
“Why,” said the Girl, sweetly, with uplifted
brows, “do I always make you take the reins ?
Satan finds some mischief still, etc., applies in
both cases.”
The Man Who Endured grinned dubiously.
“Of course, I lectured him” (in a complacent
tone), “and he seemed very meek, and I really
began to think ’’ (patting the cushion softly) “ he
was awfully nice. Do you know” (breaking off
abruptly) “ when you shut your mouth like that,
you don’t look pretty. Aren’t you interested ?
His real name was Donald,’’ she went on, “ but I
called him Billie. It’s so much shorter and nicer.
We were having a really good time. You see, he
was so jolly, so good-tempered, and never minded
waiting quite half an hour. And then he went
and spoilt it all ” (resignedly). " l was never so
disappointed in anyone.”
Her listener choked : * How did he fall from
grace ? ” he queried.
The Girl shook her head regretfully: “It was
at Alice’s dance, and I was having such a lovely
time ” (in a mournful tone). “How people can
be so selfish, I don’t know. We were sitting out,
for he danced atrociously, and—and —he took my
fan away and said such ridiculous things ” (appeal-
inglv). i .
“At first” (in pretended confusion, with one
cheek buried in the cushion), “I thought lie
was trying to be funny” (lifting innocent
“and—and —and— ”
" And ?” (grimly).
“Oh, I can’t remember anything •
aggrieved voice). “I know I was very angry.’
Silence. >.
“And I told him so” (breaking the silence).
“He was so rude” (opening shocked eyes very
wide) “and said things. I boxed his ears” (blush-
ing just a trifle and hugging the cushion tightly).
" I was so disappointed in him I nearly began to
cry. Then he fled and-sent the Infant frantically
to find me curled up on a seat weeping ; my feel-
ings were so injured. When the Infant came I
howled on his shirt-front and felt so horrid.
Poor Infant” (with a giggle), “he patted me on
the back and besought me to ‘ be a good chap and
not to howl,’ and then he took me home.”
(Heaving a sigh of relief.) “I had such a head-
ache next day ” (gazing up pitifully). It was a
whole week before you came back” (sinking into
a reproachful silence).
The Man Who Endured Much moved uneasily.
“But I suppose”(sighing a little) “a football
tour has more attractions than—than—anything
elBe”(in the softest and most plaintive voice).
The Man who Endured took the cushion.
"Of course” (went on the small voice un-
steadily) “ sport is ever3 r thing, it doesn’t matter
much about —” (stopping uncertainly).
The cushion flew to the other end of the room,
to be joyfully seized by a puppy. Somehow it
and Billie must have been forgotten, for it was
dusk when the Infant put his head in at the door,
to be greeted by a down-covered impudent dach-
shund and two very indifferent people. “You
little animal,” said the Girl, bundling the puppy
into her arms. But she kissed him on the top of
the head all the same.

Quits.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The man who deals with stock is not, as a rule,
the owner of a highly cultivated mind ; and it
generally happens that, the more the bucolic indi-
vidual is successful in his business, the less he
knows or cares for anything else. Hence, in bush
districts, the practical joke is cultivated and
cherished as an expression of the highest form of
wit of which the human mind is capable.
M'Murdo, overseer at an out-station of Glen-
tavish, was quite famous in the district for the
number and variety of successful practical jokes
he had played. A good deal of his spare time
and cash were devoted to literature of the Jack
Harkaway and Deadwood Dick type, and the
fruit was quite worthy of the tree.
The township and district were policed by Ser-
geant Farrell, a clever, hard old Irishman; two
troopers, one named Dennis Burke ; and three
foot-police, including a senior-constable. Trooper
Burke was regarded by the sergeant as one of his
smartest “weaners,” as the old fellow named a
new-chum policeman, and had only recently been
drafted from the depot. He was young, keen
and cocksure about things, and preternaturally
suspicious of everybody out of the force. Thus
he fell easily to M'Murdo when that worthy tried
his irreverent hand en the police; a thing which
only a despot of the calibre of Bonaparte can
afford to do in most countries. M'Murdo found
this out later.
The trooper had been on patrol, and, reaching
M'Murdo’s out-station about sundown, had put-up
there for the night. Several strangers were there
who did not impress the young policeman favor-
ably, and he suspected them of something or
other, in consequence; more especially as
M'Murdo was obviously uneasy. He had supper
and a smoke, and turned in. He awoke suddenly
in the night, and suspicion was changed into
certainty, for he heard M'Murdo say, “Oh, he’s
asleep all right; he got a fair dose in his last pint
of tea, so go ahead.” And they went ahead.
There was to be a raid by two notorious cattle-
stealers on Bardoo, a neighboring cattle station ;
present company assisting. The rendezvous was
carefully named and described ; the names of the
thieves as carefully mentioned ; and the time—
day after to-morrow. Trooper Burke saw glory
and promotion awaiting him.
He slept no more that night, and was up bright
and early next day. M'Murdo said that the visi-
tors had left ; Burke doubted this, for he saw
several horses feeding about which had recently
been ridden hard, and he saw enough saddles to
match them leaning against the wall outside, the
station saddles being still on the pegs under the
verandah. He said nothing, however, but saddled
up and rode into town. Unluckily for him the
sergeant had left leaving word that he
would not be back until night. The senior con-
stable (a footman) was in charge, so youthful
inexperience, ambition and desire for immediate
action had their way, and Burke left for the
cattle-stealers’ rendezvous, hoping that the ser-
geant would be up in time to help him secure his
*The sergeant got home at 5 o’clock, hot
and dusty, and receiving his junior’s report before
he had time to cool, nearly took a fit. He got a
fresh horse and tried to overtake Burke before he
had committed himself too far, but only arrived
in time to receive a share of the contumely thrown
about by a wild lot of youngsters who were assist-
ing at a muster of the Bardoo cattle. M'Murdo
wasn’t there ; but the sergeant knew.
The laugh ran up and down the river at
another of M'Murdo’s successes, and for a long
while the force squirmed at allusions to listeners
and cattle-thieves. The sergeant contented him-
self with slating Burke privately. He wound-up
his lecture by saying : “To let the divils pull yer
leg like that! —we’ll be laughed at until the cows
come home, and ye’ll be axed, till ye die, ‘ Are ye
listenin’, Trooper Burke?’ But wait till I get a
chance at M'Murdo ! Go to yer jooty.”
It had been 116° in Doodney’s bar (the coolest
place in town) during the day, and at 7 o’clock
the sun had gone down, and the wind with it.
The rolling cloud of dust which had been charg-
ing across the surrounding plains, and through
the straggling township, since an hour after sun-
rise was now settling down on everything. The
barman at Doodney’s was wiping the grit out of a
quarter column of glasses—the arrears of a big
shout—with a dry and dusty rag, while he lan-
guidly watched an ancient drunk in the corner
slapping at the walls with his open hands, trying to
kill freckled spiders with blue eyes and with their
red hair done up in a knot at the back of the head.
The coach had just arrived at the Post-office, a
few doors up, an hour late, and the people were
waiting in front of the building for their mail, too
tired or too full of luke-warm whisky even to
talk. v
“Anything stirring up the river, Dave? ” asked
the sergeant.
“Nothing much,” said the driver, “exceptin’l
saw old Smallman, the bush missionary, about five
miles back. He sung out something of a murder as
I passed him.”
At this the sergeant stiffened like a terrier at
a rabbit-hole.
“Murder, Dave? Where?”
“ Dunno. I didn’t stop for his pitch ; late
enough as ’tis. He’ll be here soon, be the way
he’s graftin’on the old grey. See yer later. ” He
reached over for the mail receipt handed to him
by the Post-office official, ari l with a “Right
y’are, sonny! ” gathered up his drooping team
and drove off to the pub. to drop his passengers.
The sergeant got his mail from the police box and
went back to his quarters.
A little later a man rode up to the police-
station. He was tall, bony and elderly, Small-
man by name, a bush missionary, and better
known for his enormous appetite than his piety.
The horse, too, was tall, bony and grey, and
nearly as old as his owner; the turnout being
known locally as the “Woodheap.” He dis-
mounted, and walked over to the sergeant, who
was now leaning on the verandah rail.
“ Ye’re back soon, Mr. Smallman,” he said.
“Yes, sergeant, I am returned much sooner
than I anticipated. lam come to report a dread-
ful murder.”
“S-s-h! Not another word; come inside. Sit
down, me good mahn, and wait a minyut.
Constable Burke, tell the black boy to bring up
Dandy and Spider, and do ye fetch Gaylad, and
get ready for a ride yerself. Now then, Mr.
Smallman, tell the story in yer own way, but be
quick.”
“ As you know, sergeant, I left town to-day on
my monthly circuit, intending to get to Glen-
tavish out-station by evening, and stay there for
the night. I got to the gate leading into the
house-paddock surrounding the huts, when I saw
a man, bareheaded, running hard towards me
from the hut. Presently another came out of the
front door with a gun in his hand. He fired at
the fugitive, who still ran on. The murderer then
dropped on one knee, and, taking careful aim,
fired a,t the man who was running, and dropped
him like a log. The murderer then made over
towards me, and, being unarmed, I turned my
horse and fled, to report the frightful affair to
you.”
“ And did ye re-cognise anny of them ? ”
“ I did, sergeant. The man who was cut off in
the full blossom of his sins I did not know ; but
the murderer I fully recognised as M'Murdo, the
overseer there. I always considered him an evil
man.”
“M'Murdo?” The sergeant was silent for a
little. Then he asked, “ What time was this ? ”
“ About 5 o’clock.”
Had the coach passed before the shooting? ”
“No ! for it passed me at Bardoo waterhole,
five miles back.”
The sergeant went out, and, after a search,
found the coach-driver spooning the freckled bar-
maid from Doodney’s. A few short questions and
replies satisfied the sergeant, and he turned back
to the station. He wasn’t nearly so keen on the
matter as he had been, and walked leisurely on
his return. Suddenly, he hastened his steps, and
entered the office like a man who has thought of
something brilliant, and is in a hurry to carry it
out. He gave instructions to the missionary not
to. leave the town until he had returned, and ten
minutes later was on the road with Burke and a
black boy. They rode silently for a few miles,
and then the sergeant said : “ Would ye like to be
even with M‘Murdo for the 13th of last October,
Dmny ?” And Burke said, fervently, “I would.”
They rode steadily until they reached the gate
of the out-station paddock, and it seemed to Con-
stable Burke that the Sergeant took singularly
few precautions considering that they were come
to arrest an armed murderer, or possibly a mad-
man. He rode up to the huts amid a clamor of
dogs, dismounted, and walked straight into the
front room, where M'Murdo, in pyjamas, was in
the act of lighting a candle; roused out, no doubt,
by the barking of the dogs and the thud of the
approaching horses’ feet.
“ Hello, Sergeant! ” he said ; “ welcome home.
Are you looking for cattle-duffers ? ”
“We’ll see, me boy—put up yer hands. I
arrest ye, James M‘Murdo, in the King’s name,
for murder ; and I warn ye that anything ye may
say will be used against ye at yer trial. Produce
the handcuffs, Constable Burke.”
“ Hold on, Sergeant ; it’s all a joke.
“ Ye’ll find it’s no joke.”
“ But it is, I tell you. One of the station-men
brought me a message from the head station, and
has not long been gone home. We were just
going to have tea when we saw old Smallman, the
bush missionary, at the gate, and as the ration
cart is not due for two days, it meant a famine if
he had three meals here, so, to bluff him off, the
man who was here ran, and I got the gun and
after him. I fired two shots at him, and he fell at the
second, as was arranged. You’d have died to see
the parson and old Woodheap travel away. Of
course, I am sorry you have had a ride for nothing,
but I can put you and Burke up ”
“That will do,” said the sergeant, sternly.
“On yer own showing, yer damned inhospitable,
and we came prepared, and yer a desperate
villain, and I’m going to gather ye in.”
M'Murdo put up a very good fight, but he was
quite overmatched, and at last they had him
handcuffed, leg-ironed, and helpless.
“ To-morrow, ” said the sergeant, in his official
voice, “Constable Burke will search for the corpse
until he finds one, or the remains of one. He
knows ye, and will do his jooty.” M'Murdo
groaned when he remembered that Burke did
know him.
The next day the town turned out to see the
sergeant leading a horse carrying his prisoner,
who was handcuffed and had his feet tied under
the horse’s belly. He came at mid-day past the
school where the children were playing, and down
the main street. They had met the coach opposite
the out-station. It was filled with passengers.
One, a new-chum on his way to take up ;i>,
appointment as jackeroo on a station higher up
the river, commented audibly on the villainous
looks of the prisoner. The coach-driver and the
sergeant solemnly winked at each other. The
sergeant asked after the health of the driver’s
blue-eyed beauty, and allowed no communication
from the prisoner. After leaving the coach the
sergeant did not speak a word, and M'Murdo had
time to think things over. He had read a good
deal of police literature, and had a very fair collec-
tion of Deadwood Dicks at the station, and he
particularly remembered that in one he had
recently read, called “The Sleuth’s Revenge,”
the police had fraudulently produced or pro-
vided a body, to the very great inconvenience
of the hero. So that, by the time he reached
his narrow cell, he was sorry that he had
ever been born. The sergeant told Smallfnan
the prisoner’s version of the affair and it lost
nothing in the telling, so that when the missionary
left the lock-up after the interview allowed by the
sergeant, M'Murdo gathered the impression that
the man of peace would prefer, rather, to swear
his life away for “ the gross insult to a servant of
the Master ” than for a real crime.
It took ten clear days to straighten up matters.
Burke spent that time looking for the body.
During the ten days M'Murdo had an experience
of offended justice in the shape of a revengeful
policeman. The cooking was awful. The worst
drunks were put in his cell and his rest was broken
by policemen pounding on the door.
When he came out, his troubles had not ended,
for when he saw his owner (M'Tavish of that ilk)
he was told that “she hass no use for a man to
play practical jokes in working hours ” (5 a.m. to
la.m.) “ She wass too expensive for a station in
dry times, so she’d better go somewhere else
whateffer, where they did not refuse hospitality
to the man of God also.” A Homily on Hospitality
from M'Tavish broke M'Murdo up completely,
for he was quite dumb, and folding up his things
he stole silently away.
The sergeant said, “Are ye satisfied, Dinny ?”
And policeman Dennis Burke said in a deep voice,
“ Oi am ! ”
TARBOY.
It would be interesting to know in what
position some of the Australian drapery firms
stand in respect to the Copyright Act. In their
various catalogues of fashions they scissor whole-
sale pages of designs that have appeared in the
Ladies’ Pictorial and the Queen and such-like
influential publications. There is no acknow-
ledgment made whatever, the illustration being
boldly claimed as “our own design.” Doubtless
the retail drapers’ safety lies in the fact that none
of these pirated catalogues reach England. Were
the various Art Societies not too busy quarrelling
about subsidies they might devote a little atten-
tion to this question, for it affects them to a very
material extent. The heaviest expenditure in a
fashion catalogue is the artists’ drawings, and if
these can be filched, the dishonest softgoods man
will spend no money among the long-haired fra-
ternity with the paint smudge on its clothes.

"Our Respected Townsman.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mild as the dove of Peace,
I waxed exceeding fat;
I saw my flocks increase,
And thanked my God for that;
And smoked my pipe at evening glow, and softly
spat.
Through green to gold my grain
Eacli year sufficed my soul,
And year by year my brain
Found pleasure in the roll
Of notes that marked my careful progress to the
goal.
Was the earth flat or round ?
It mattered not to me ;
Good friends thereon I found,
Rarely an enemy.
So what to me was time, and what Eternity ?
No bickering kings brought down
The price of greasy wool;
Sunk ship or blazing town—
There still was fruit to pull ;
And kingdoms might go bung—my peaceful cup
was full.
Our Road Board meetings were
My sole affairs of State;
I occupied the chair
And gravely held debate
Of which work should be done next year, and
which should wait.
I saw my sons grow tall,
And marry, and the grace
Of God was over all,
And round about the place,
For when I died they would perpetuate my race.
Wars flamed in alien lands,
And kingdoms rose and fell;
Anarchy burst her bands,
And stalked red-robed from Hell.
But these were far-off, distant things, and all was
well.
No fame I left to fade,
No obloquy to grow,
And in my exit made
No panoply or show,
But peacefully subsided to the worms below.
Now, crawling through my frame,
The worms say, “Peace! Have peace!
Since flesh is more than fame
And glory less than grease.”
And, after all, the final arbiters are these.
M.li
M. CORMAC.
A Woman, Her Property, and
Some Men.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The matrimonial advt. is a thing that is hung
around with mystery and clothed with a faint
aroma of wickedness, and is therefore attractive
to many classes of society. For a month I had
read the column regularly, half inclined to open a
correspondence with some of the advertisers, and
see if there really are men and women who seek
their life-companions in such questionable quarters
as the columns of a daily newspaper.
At last I decided to advertise on my own
account, and I bribed a youth to take my adver-
tisement into a newspaper office. This was how
it read in print:
YOUNG French lady, widow; of refined education ;
possessed of landed property ; desires to meet
with honorable man of loving disposition with means.
Address, “ Ad6le,” G.P.O.
This, I thought, would fetch ’em.
It did. Two days later I called, and a brusque,
unsentimental clerk, slightly bald in front, advised
me to get an express.
“ Are there so many ? ” I asked in astonishment.
“Tons !” he replied, laconically.
I went away, and, returning with a carry-all,
took it home in a cab stuffed with nearly 500
letters. Arrived, I sat down in the middle of the
room and commenced to open and sort out those
whom I would answer.
They were varied, and very hard to choose from,
but I managed to select 16 for a start. A friend
in the city had offered me the use of his office for
interview purposes. It consisted of two rooms, in
one of which I was to receive my would-be wooers,
while in the other my friend was to wait silently
with a club to come to my rescue if necessary. I
wrote asking two to call next evening at 8 and
9.15 p, m. respectively.
• • * *
I had shaded the gas, and placed the other chair
so that the light would fall on my prospective
husband and leave myself in the shade. My
greatest dread was that some scamp of my
acquaintance would walk in and say, “ Blessed if
I didn’t think it was you, and that was why I
answered.”
He arrived at 8 sharp. He knocked lightly,
and came in with the stealthy tread of a man
who knows he is doing wrong, but is determined
to see it through. He was tall, well set-up, and
walked with an easy swing into the light. He
peered at me sitting in the shadow.
“ Won’t you sit down?” I said in my lowest
audible voice.
He threw his hat and stick on the floor and col-
lapsed into the chair, which was a wicker easy
one, and mumbled, “Thanks, awfully!”
I waited for him to begin, but he sat there
silently looking round the room, gaping at a
picture on the wall. The silence was so great that
I could hear my friend next door changing the
position of the club. In desperation I said in my
highest respectable voice:
“ Been a beautiful day, hasn’t it?”
“N.T.8.5.!” lie replied enigmatically.
‘ l Wtiich being translated means —”
“Not too blooming slow!” he continued.
This broke the ice, and he launched out, “I say,
would you mind if I come a bit closer ? ”
Remembering the club, I assented sweetly,
“Not at all!”
He came within reaching distance. Isay, he
began, “ I like you. You know, you’re not half a
bad-looking girl.”
I bowed ; my heart was too full for words.
“Do you really want to get married, or did you
just advertise for a lark ?”
“ Oh, no ; I advertised for a man !
He went off into a wild yell of laughter, as
though I had said something desperately funny :
“Oh, I say, you are sharp, bai Jove! But
what do you want him for—a husband, or just a
walking-stick ? ”
“Both ! ” I replied, emphatically.
“Oh !” he sniggered. “I know an awfully
nice place for a honeymoon.”
“ Do you—don’t you think you are a bit rapid ? ”
I asked, innocently. He came a little nearer—as
though lie felt sure of his ground—and, if I hadn’t
eluded him, he would have embraced me.
“Yes, we could leave to-morrow morning, by
the early train, and, if the beans are all right, we
could have a d d good time .”
“I beg your pardon !” severely—but the club
fell with a loud crash in the next room ; there was
a sudden oath, and my suitor had vanished through
the door.
My friend poked his head in at the other door and
made some facetious remarks, and explained how
the club came to bang suddenly on the floor. It
was pure accident, but it was also providential,
for, although it was not nearly 9.15, a determined
footstep was heard on the stair—we got into posi-
tion once more.
I was not sure whether it was a man or a
baboon that entered in answer to a pleasant
“Come in!” In appearance he was about 4ft.
Bin., with a large, bushy beard, and much grey
hair; his trousers were half-mast over a pair of
blucher shoes; his coat was of a fashion of 20
years ago, and was green with old age, and on his
head was a stove-pipe hat from which the nap had
long since disappeared. A weather-beaten um-
brella completed his remarkable get up. Before
speaking he carefully placed his umbrella in a
corner and poised his hat nicely on the handle.
I was tongue-tied. He settled himself into a
chair, and after a long stare through his steel-
rimmed glasses, lie commenced:
“Well, my dear, how are yous?”
“I am quite well, thank you; are you Mr.
Smithkinson who answered my advertisement?”
“ Yes, I’m old S. Let’s have a look at yous ! ”
He came closer, and I noticed that his beauty
was further enhanced by a wart on his nose. I
shuddered. I wondered would it be even possible
to kiss that toothless mouth. The old man seemed
satisfied with his survey, and re-commenced :
“You’re a purty gal—are yous strong enough
to milk?”
“ To what ? ” I asked.
“To milk—cows !” he explained. “ Yer see I
owns a bit o’ land and a bit o’ money put away,
and when I sees as you ’ad a bit o’ proputty, I
thought as how you’d be just the one fur me.
See?”
“ How good of you, ” I murmured.
“I’m a bit old —I s’pose” (with a horrid leer),
“ but I’m better nor many young ’uns knocking
about; and if yous can milk an’ be handy about
the place, you won’t have no call to complain
about my lovin’ness.”
“I’m afraid your offer wouldn’t suit me. I
don’t know anything about milking, nor how to
do any work, and, although you are really very
nice, I think I should prefer a younger husband.”
“ Well— er course-all young ’uns do be flighty ”
—with a sudden change of tone. “How much
did you say your bit o’ proputty was worth, my
dear ?”
“I didn’t say it was worth anything, and I
wish you would go away.”
What on earth were my friend and his club
doing. I couldn’t hear a sound from the next
room. The horrid old man was now in a towering
rage and shaking his fist within three inches of
my nose. Suddenly he sci’eamed out that I was
a painted “Jezerbel” and lots of other things
quite unfit for publication and rolled on the floor
in a fit.
• * * *
It was awful. We poured water over him, and
rammed his stove pipe on his head, and bribed the
watchman with ss. and expenses to carry him out
and put him in a cab and send him anywhere—to
a hospital preferred.
* * * ft
During the week I interviewed the other 14.
There were two well-known politicians and one
doctor (all married). Then there was a gentleman
who offered me an easy billet as governess of his
three children without salary, and who was pre-
pared to give his written promise that he would
marry me within three weeks of his wife’s demise.
Then there was a young man who wanted to
take a position as a married couple, and as he
knew Frenchwomen were good cooks, he thought
I would just suit him, and that we should be able
to ask at least £BO per annum, and be dirt-cheap
at that.
A fat man, with a silk waistcoat, beautifully
and skilfully cut to his rotundity, who offered me
a suburban villa, a cheque-book, and lots of dia-
monds, “ my tear ! ”
Two were auctioneers, who offered to sell my
little bit of property on most advantageous terms,
and charge merely “nominal commissi s.”
My friend complained bitterly that, so far, he
had had no sport out of it. I retorted meekly
that something of a sensational natui’e might
happen which would satisfy him. Twenty
minutes later his brother-in-law turned up under
the name of “Truth ’’—and there was great sport.
The club did splendid work, and I have often
wondered how Truth accounted to his six-months’
bride for his black eyes and damaged suit.
The exception was a most charming man ; tall,
intellectual and honorable. He came in easily, as
though paying an ordinary call. I rose to meet
him, and we were both at our ease immediately.
We discoursed art and literature, science and
political economy. He breathed refinement. His
nails were manicured and his clothes must have
been made in St. James’-street. In the course of
conversation he told me that he was visiting Aus-
tralia and was proceeding to Japan and then to the
East generally. He was looking for a travelling
companion, and asked gracefully :
“ Do you believe in free love ? ”
I blushed. I wasn’t sure if I did or not.
He quoted :
Some for the glories of this world, and some
Sigh for the Prophet’s Paradise to come I
Ah ! take the cash and let the credit go,
Nor heed the rumble of a distant drum.
I looked at him, and thought how handsome he
was. The air was charged with electricity. He
bent closer; his look was mesmeric, his voice a
caress as he murmured :
Thou art for happiness with me, love :
Then love me as thou wilt.
I care not, so I live with thee,
For goodness, or for guilt.
I was suddenly brought back to my senses by
hearing an ominous thud in the next room. He
looked at the door and breathed hard—the silence
was intense. I said in a loud voice :
‘ 4 No, I do not believe in free love. ”
Then I hastily opened the door. He looked at
me quickly, then at the other door; and, stooping,
he kissed my hand.
* * * *
My friend behaved like a hog. I almost thought
he was going to use the club on me—he brandished
it about so fiercely and yelled at me :
“ This d d tommy-rot is going to stop right
here.”
And it did.
SABAH JONES.

On Our Selection, written by A. H. Davis
(“ Steele Rudd ”) and published by The
Bulletin Co., has earned the title of “the
jolliest book ever printed in Australia.”
There is laughter on every page ; yet the
book remains grimly true to the facts. As
a picture of life on an Australian selection
it has never been surpassed. Since 1 it was
published, little more than two years ago,
upwards of 12,000 copies have been sold at
the comparatively high price of 6s.—making
an Australian publishing record. The reason of the sale is not far to seek : the
people who have bought the book know they have got value for their money ;
and they recommend it to others. The price is comparatively high because the book is expensive to
produce. It is well printed, and strongly bound in cloth in good style. There are numerous pictures
by such well-known illustrators as Mahony, Leist, Lambert, Fischer, Fullwood, and Vincent. From
cover to cover the book is Australian —the product of Aus-
tralian workmen. If it were produced by cheaper labour
in England, it would cost less, and could be sold at a
cheaper rate. The publishers prefer to employ Australian
labor only. When you buy On Our Selection you pay
a little more than you pay for an English book ; but
you get 100 times the Australian value, “ Steele Rudd’s ”
book has gone all over Australia, and wherever it has
gone it has become the treasure of a household, often
the delight of a township. People never leave off
reading it until it is thumbed to rags; and then they
buy another oopv. The characters of “Our Selection”—
“Old Dad,” “Joe,” “Dave,” “Sarah,” and the rest— , > V ■V ■ „
have become famous throughout the country ; and their imitation by weaker writers proves the force
of their grip on the public mind. Any bookseller will sell you the book; or if you send a postal
order for 65., with Bd. postage, direct to The Bulletin office, it will be forwarded to any address
by return mail.
